Nikah — Marriage

6 min
Disagreement on Mahr Amount: Testimony of Mahr al-Mithl
Ruling on whose statement is believed when spouses dispute the amount of mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for resolving disputes over mahr amount during marriage and explain the roles of mahr al-mithl, oath, and evidence.

Core Idea
When spouses dispute the mahr amount, mahr al-mithl determines whose statement is accepted with an oath.

This lesson deals with a common scenario: a married couple agrees that a mahr was specified, but they disagree on its amount. The Hanafi school provides a clear procedure using the wife's mahr al-mithl (appropriate mahr) as a benchmark to determine which spouse's claim is initially accepted. The rule balances fairness and practicality, using the apparent (zahir) to identify the denier and administer an oath. Importantly, either party may produce formal evidence (bayyinah) to prove their claim, regardless of who is the apparent denier.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Confirm that the marriage is still ongoing and that there is a disagreement on the amount of a specified mahr. This rule applies only when the marriage has not ended.
If the marriage has ended, a different rule (tahaluf) applies.
2
Determine the wife's mahr al-mithl
Ascertain or estimate the mahr al-mithl of the woman based on her social standing, family, etc. This is the objective benchmark.
Mahr al-mithl is the appropriate mahr for a woman in similar circumstances.
3
Compare each spouse's claim with mahr al-mithl
See which claim is closer to or equal to the mahr al-mithl. If mahr al-mithl equals or is less than the husband's claim, the apparent supports the husband. If equals or is greater than the wife's claim, the apparent supports the wife.
The apparent (zahir) is the natural expectation that the specified mahr is not less than mahr al-mithl.
4
Apply the rule: statement of the supported party is taken
The spouse whose claim is supported by the apparent is considered the denier (of the other's claim). Their statement is taken with an oath. The other spouse becomes the claimant who must produce evidence or accept the denier's statement.
The denier's statement with oath establishes the mahr amount if the claimant cannot prove otherwise.
5
Allow evidence from either party
Despite the initial preference, either spouse may produce formal evidence (bayyinah) to prove their claimed amount. If evidence is produced, it is accepted regardless of which party is the apparent denier.
This is because the dispute is over money, and the rules of evidence (bayyinah) apply fully.

Source Text

[٣] قوله : شهد ؛ يعني سواء شهد مهر المثل للزوج بأن كان مساوياً لما يدعيه أو أقل منه، أو شهد لها بأن كان مساوياً لما تدعيه أو أكثر منه، وكذا إذا لم يشهد لا له ولا لها، بأن كان مهر المثل بينهما، وهذا وإن لم يذكر في المتن أشار إليه الشارح له في مسألة التحالف، ففي الصور الثلاث تقبل البينة لأيهما كانت.

And regarding its amount while the marriage is subsisting: the statement is of the one for whom mahr al-mithl testifies, along with his/her oath. And whichever party produces evidence, it is accepted if mahr al-mithl testifies for him or her. (And regarding its amount while the marriage is subsisting: the statement is of the one for whom mahr al-mithl testifies, along with his oath): meaning if mahr al-mithl equals what the husband claims, or is less, then the statement is his, with an oath. And if it equals what the woman claims, or is more, then the statement is hers, with an oath. (And whichever party produces evidence, it is accepted if mahr al-mithl testifies for him or her): That is because the woman claims... (The commentator mentions that the correct view is that the denier is sworn here by consensus, because the dispute is about mahr which is money, and swearing is applicable there even according to Abu Hanifa.) [1] His saying: 'or less'; for example, if the husband claims the mahr is one thousand, and the woman claims two thousand, then if her mahr al-mithl is one thousand or less, the apparent (zahir) is a witness for the husband; because the apparent is that the specified amount is not less than mahr al-mithl, but equal or more. And whoever has the apparent as a witness, his statement is considered: meaning with an oath, so he is made the denier and the other the claimant. And if her mahr al-mithl is two thousand or more, then the apparent is a witness for the woman; because the apparent is that she sets the specified amount equal to her mahr al-mithl or slightly less, so the statement is hers with an oath. [2] His saying: 'And whichever'; meaning whichever of the husband or wife produces evidence to prove what he/she claims, his/her evidence is accepted and the claim is established. [3] His saying: 'testifies'; meaning whether mahr al-mithl testifies for the husband by being equal to his claim or less, or testifies for her by being equal to her claim or more, and likewise if it does not testify for either, i.e., mahr al-mithl is between the two claims. This, even if not mentioned in the matn, the commentator referred to it in the issue of mutual swearing (tahaluf). So in all three cases, the evidence is accepted from whichever party produces it. [4] His saying: 'That is'; meaning the acceptance of evidence from whichever of them produces it individually, and the purpose is to repel what is said: that evidence is only accepted from the claimant, and not from the denier, for evidence is for proof. In this issue, the husband is the denier of the increase that the woman claims, so his evidence should not be accepted, only hers. (But the correct view is that both are accepted because the dispute is about money, and the principle of evidence applies to both.)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where both spouses agree that a mahr was specified, but they disagree on its amount, and the marriage is still ongoing. The ruling is that the statement of the spouse whose claim is supported by the mahr al-mithl (the appropriate mahr for the woman) is taken, along with an oath. For example, if the husband claims the mahr is 1000 and the wife claims 2000, and the woman's mahr al-mithl is 1000 or less, then the apparent (zahir) supports the husband, as it is unlikely that the specified amount would be less than mahr al-mithl. So the husband's statement is taken with an oath, and he is considered the denier (of the excess). Conversely, if mahr al-mithl is 2000 or more, the apparent supports the wife, so her statement is taken with an oath. In all cases, if either party produces evidence (bayyinah), it is accepted, regardless of whether they are the apparent claimant or denier. This is because the dispute is about mahr, which is money, and the rules of evidence apply fully. The commentator also clarifies that the earlier remark about Abu Hanifa not swearing in marriage does not apply here, as the dispute is about the mahr (money), not the marriage itself, so swearing is unanimously allowed.

Key Terms
القول قول من شهد له مهر المثل (al-qawl qawl man shahida lahu mahr al-mithl): The statement of the person whose claim is supported by the mahr al-mithl is accepted, with an oath.
شهد مهر المثل (shahida mahr al-mithl): When the mahr al-mithl is equal to or more/less than a party's claim, it serves as circumstantial evidence (shahada) supporting one side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over the amount of a specified mahr, if the wife's mahr al-mithl is 1500 and she claims 2000 while the husband claims 1000, whose statement is taken first?
A) The husband's, because mahr al-mithl is closer to his claim.
B) The wife's, because mahr al-mithl is closer to her claim.
C) Neither; evidence must be produced first.
D) Both must swear against each other.

Key Takeaway
The mahr al-mithl serves as a decisive indicator to determine which spouse's statement is accepted in a mahr amount dispute during the marriage.

Nikah — Marriage

Evidence in Mahr Disputes During Marriage: The Rule of Mahr al-Mithl
Explains whose claim and evidence are accepted in mahr disputes with hadith proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi evidentiary rule for mahr disputes by determining which spouse’s statement is accepted based on mahr al-mithl and explaining when evidence from the denier is accepted.

Core Idea
In mahr disputes, the spouse supported by the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) prevails; evidence is accepted from either party to repel an oath.

When spouses disagree on the dower amount during an ongoing marriage, the Hanafi school employs a rule based on the woman's proper dower (mahr al-mithl). This rule, grounded in the hadith on burden of proof, balances the rights of both parties. This lesson unpacks the rule, the role of oaths and evidence, and a notable intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the mahr al-mithl
Determine the mahr al-mithl for the wife based on her status (age, beauty, wealth, education, etc.). This is the baseline, not necessarily the claimed amount.
The mahr al-mithl is established by custom and similarity to other women in her situation.
2
Compare each spouse's claimed amount to the mahr al-mithl
Check whether the mahr al-mithl is equal to, less than, or greater than each claim. This determines whose claim is supported by the apparent (zahir) – the original principle that mahr should be mahr al-mithl.
3
Apply the statement-with-oath rule
If the mahr al-mithl supports the husband (i.e., it is equal to or less than his claim), his statement with an oath is accepted. If it supports the wife (i.e., it is equal to or more than her claim), her statement with an oath is accepted. If it supports neither, they proceed to mutual oaths.
The oath reinforces the claim; the supported party does not have to bring evidence initially.
4
Understand the acceptance of evidence from either party
Even the denier (the one not supported by the zahir) can bring evidence (bayyinah) to prove his or her claim. This evidence is accepted because it can repel the oath that would otherwise be required.
This is an exception to the general rule that evidence is only for the claimant; here the denier's evidence is accepted to avoid an oath.
5
Connect to the hadith and the depository analogy
The rule is derived from the hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara'. The commentator analogizes to a depository who brings evidence of returning a deposit – his evidence is accepted despite him being the denier.
The qiyas shows that evidence can be accepted from the denier to repel the oath.

Source Text

( وفي قدره حال قيام النكاح : القولُ لَمن شهد له مهر المثل مع يمينه) : أي إن كان مهر المثل مساوياً لما يدعيه الزوج ، أو أقل منه ، فالقول له مع اليمين، وإن كان مساوياً لِمَا تدَّعيه المرأة ، أو أكثر منه ، فالقولُ لها مع اليمين. ( وأي أقام بينةٌ قُبلت شهد" مهر المثل له أو لها) : وذلك لأن المرأة تدعى من أحد مسامحات الشارح الله في هذا الشرح ، وكم له فيه، وللمصنف في «متنه» من مسامحة نبه عليها ابن كمال باشا الرومي في «الايضاح والاصلاح»، فإن الخلاف هاهنا ليس في النكاح بل في المهر وهو مال وفي مثله يجري التحليف عنده أيضاً ، فالصحيح أنه يحلف المنكر هاهنا إجماعاً.

And concerning its amount while the marriage is ongoing: the statement is for the one whom the proper dower supports, along with his oath, and whichever (party) presents evidence, it is accepted if the proper dower supports it for him or her. (And concerning its amount while the marriage is ongoing: the statement is for the one whom the proper dower supports, along with his oath): meaning if the proper dower is equal to what the husband claims, or less than it, then the statement is his with an oath; and if it is equal to what the woman claims, or more than it, then the statement is hers with an oath. (And whichever presents evidence, it is accepted if the proper dower supports it for him or her): That is because the woman claims... (one of the author's concessions)... the correct view is that the denier takes an oath here by consensus. ... (Then marginal notes explaining each part.) ... And if both present evidence, then her evidence is accepted if (the proper dower) supports him, and his evidence is accepted if (the proper dower) supports her; because evidence is prescribed to prove what is contrary to the apparent, and the oath is prescribed to preserve the original state. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The burden of proof is upon the claimant, and the oath is upon the denier.' And the original principle in marriage is that the proper dower (applies), so whoever claims otherwise, his evidence is stronger.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith. It addresses a dispute between spouses over the amount of dower (mahr) while the marriage still exists. The ruling states that the party whose claim is supported by the mahr al-mithl (the proper dower for a woman of her status) has the stronger position: if the mahr al-mithl equals or is less than what the husband claims, then the husband's statement (with an oath) is accepted. If it equals or is more than what the wife claims, then the wife's statement (with an oath) is accepted. If either party brings evidence (bayyinah) to support their claim, it is accepted regardless of whether they are the claimant or denier, because evidence can be brought to repel an oath as well. The text then explains that if both parties bring evidence, the evidence of the party whose claim contradicts the apparent (zahir) is given precedence. The hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara' is cited: the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath on the denier. Here, because the original principle in marriage is that the mahr is the mahr al-mithl, the party claiming a different amount is the one whose evidence is stronger. The commentary elaborates on the scenarios and clarifies that evidence from the denier is accepted to repel the oath, analogizing to a case of a depository who claims to have returned a deposit.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower: the amount typically given to a woman of similar status (age, beauty, wealth, family, etc.) at the time of marriage.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence: typically two male witnesses or one male and two females who testify to a fact.
يمين (yamin): Oath: a solemn swearing by Allah that affirms one's claim or denies the opponent's claim.
مدعي (mudda'i): Claimant: the party who asserts a fact contrary to the apparent (zahir) or contrary to the original state.
منكر (munkir): Denier: the party who denies the claimant's assertion and is supported by the apparent (zahir) or original state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
During a marriage, the wife claims the mahr was $3000, the husband claims it was $2000. The proper dower (mahr al-mithl) is $2000. Which statement (with oath) is accepted initially?
A) The wife's statement
B) The husband's statement
C) Both equally
D) Neither; they must bring evidence

Key Takeaway
In mahr disputes, the party supported by mahr al-mithl gives the statement with oath; evidence from either party, even the denier, is accepted to resolve the dispute.

Ruling on Evidence in Mahr Disputes
Explains when evidence from spouse is accepted in mahr dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for accepting evidence in mahr disputes, distinguishing between cases with one party's evidence, both parties' evidence, and no evidence with mutual oath.

Core Idea
In mahr disputes, the default is mahr al-mithl; evidence is accepted to prove a claim contrary to the default, and the oath preserves the default.

The Hanafi school provides detailed rules for resolving mahr disputes when spouses disagree on the amount. This passage from Al-Hidayah's commentary explains the evidentiary principles, centered on the hadith 'al-bayyinah ala al-mudda'i' and the concept of mahr al-mithl. Understanding these rules is essential for any jurist or mufti dealing with family law cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify that the act is resolving a mahr dispute. The ruling is that evidence is accepted according to specific rules: when only one party brings evidence, when both bring evidence, and when there is no evidence.
The focus is on evidentiary procedure, not on the validity of the marriage.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith 'al-bayyinah ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin ala man ankara' (the burden of proof is on the claimant, the oath on the denier). This is supported by the default principle of mahr al-mithl.
The hadith is recorded in al-Bayhaqi, Bukhari, and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith assigns the burden of proof to the claimant. In mahr disputes, the person whose claim differs from the default (mahr al-mithl) is considered the claimant. Therefore, their evidence is needed to prove the contrary.
The one whose claim aligns with the default is the denier and only needs to swear an oath if the other party lacks evidence.
4
Apply to the three scenarios
Scenario A: Only one party brings evidence – that evidence is accepted if it proves a claim contrary to the default. Scenario B: Both bring evidence – the evidence of the party whose claim is contrary to the default prevails. Scenario C: No evidence and mahr al-mithl is between claims – mutual oath (tahalf) is required, and if both swear or both present evidence, mahr al-mithl is due.
The qiyas to the depositor case illustrates that evidence can be accepted to repel the oath.
5
Note the tarjih
The commentary establishes the Hanafi position without mentioning a contrary school, indicating that this is the relied-upon ruling within the madhhab.
No tarjih is required as there is no ikhtilaf presented.

وإن أقاما فبينتها إن شهدَ له ، وبينته إن شهد لها
الزيادة، فإن أقامت بينة قُبِلَت ، وإن أقامَ الزَّوجُ وحده تقبل أيضاً ؛ لأن البينة تقبل لدفع اليمين كما إذا أقام المودعُ بينة على رد الوديعة إلى المالك تقبل. (وإن أقاما فبينتها إن شهد له ، وبينته إن شهد لها )؛ لأنَّ البينات شُرِعَتْ لإثباتِ ما هو خلاف الظاهر، واليمينُ شُرِعَتْ لابقاء الأصل على أصله، وقال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : «البيِّنةُ على المدَّعي واليمين على من أنكر» ، والأصل في النكاح أن يكون مهر المثل، فالذي يدعي خلاف ذلك فبينتُهُ أقوى.

[3] قوله : لأن البينة ... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن الزوج إن كان منكراً لِمَا تدَّعيه الزوجة فيلزم أن يحلف عند عجز المدعية عن بينتها ، لكن قد تقبلُ البينة لدفع اليمين، فيثبت بها ما ادعاه ويندفع عنه اليمين ، وهذا إذا كان من جانب المنكرِ أيضاً نوع إثبات لا مجرَّدَ رد وإنكار.

(۳) في صحيح البخاري (٤) : ١٦٥٦) ، و (صحيح مسلم (٣: ١٣٣٦)، وغيرها.
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. And if both bring evidence, then her evidence if it supports him, and his evidence if it supports her. (Commentary) The increase: if she brings evidence, it is accepted; and if the husband brings evidence alone, it is also accepted because evidence is accepted to repel the oath, just as when the depositor brings evidence of returning the deposit to the owner, it is accepted. (And if both bring evidence, then her evidence if it supports him, and his evidence if it supports her) because evidence is legislated to prove what is contrary to the apparent, and the oath is legislated to preserve the original state. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' And the default in marriage is that the mahr is the mahr al-mithl, so the one who claims contrary to that, his evidence is stronger. [1] His saying: 'accepted' because evidence is for proof, so it proves the increase. [2] His saying: 'alone' excludes the case when both bring evidence, the ruling of which will come in the text and commentary. [3] His saying: 'because evidence...' its summary: if the husband is denying what the wife claims, then he must swear when the claimant is unable to bring evidence, but evidence may be accepted to repel the oath, so what he claims is proven and the oath is repelled from him, and this is when from the side of the denier there is also a type of proof, not mere rejection and denial. [4] His saying: 'just as when...' meaning if a man claims against another that he deposited his wealth with him, and he asks him to return it, and the depositee says: I have already returned it to you before, then in this case the evidence of the defendant, the depositee, is accepted if he brings it to prove the return of the deposit. [5] His saying: 'her evidence' meaning the evidence of the wife is accepted if the mahr al-mithl supports her claim by being less than or equal to it, and the husband's evidence is accepted if the mahr al-mithl supports his claim by being equal to it or more. [6] His saying: 'because evidence' is an explanation of the difference between her evidence and his evidence, and its summary: evidence is made legally to prove what is not indicated by the apparent, so whoever's statement is contrary to the apparent, his evidence is accepted over the other's evidence. [7] His saying: 'and he said' is a support for what he mentioned, that the oath is to preserve the original state, and evidence is to prove contrary to the original, because when the Prophet ﷺ made the oath upon the denier and the evidence upon the claimant, that is known from it; because the denier denies what the claimant claims, so he adheres to the original; because the original in things is non-existence, and in liabilities is innocence. The claimant claims that his right is attached to his liability. This hadith in this wording is famous in fiqh books, and it is recorded in Sunan al-Bayhaqi from Ibn Abbas who said: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'If people were given according to their claims, some men would claim the wealth and blood of others, but the burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the one who denies.' It is recorded in the two Sahihs with the wording: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant.' And al-Daraqutni narrated it in marfu' form: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the one who denies, except in the case of qasamah.' And if there is mutual oath between them: meaning if the mahr al-mithl is between what the husband and wife claim, and there is no evidence for either, they take mutual oaths. Then if they both swear or both bring evidence, judgement is given by it: i.e., by the mahr al-mithl. If they both swear, judgement is by the mahr al-mithl, and likewise if each brings evidence. [1] His saying: 'between what they claim' as if the husband claims that the mahr is one thousand, and the wife claims it is two thousand, and the mahr al-mithl is one thousand five hundred. [2] His saying: 'and there is no evidence for either' meaning no evidence for the husband nor the wife for what they claim; this is a condition because if there were evidence for one, there is no need for mutual oath, but rather whichever brings evidence is accepted as before. [3] His saying: 'they take mutual oaths' then if the husband refuses to swear, judgement is for two thousand; if the wife refuses to swear, one thousand is obligatory. [4] His saying: 'if they both swear' this is on the assumption that there is no evidence for either. [5] His saying: 'judgement is by it' meaning in each of the two cases, but if they both bring proof, the husband has the choice in paying the mahr al-mithl between dirhams and dinars, unlike the case of mutual oath; because the evidence of each cancels the specification of the other, so the contract becomes free of specification, so the mahr al-mithl is obligatory; and it is not so in mutual oath because... (1) And lot must be cast in beginning the oath due to lack of preference for either, and al-Quduri said: He begins with the oath of the husband, and if they both refuse, the other's statement becomes binding. See: Majma' al-Anhur 1:360. (2) In Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir 1:252, al-Nawawi said: a hasan hadith. See: Talkhis al-Habir 4:208, Kashf al-Khafa' 1:342, etc. (3) In Sahih al-Bukhari 4:1656, and Sahih Muslim 3:1336, etc.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on Mahr in the Book of Nikah, presenting a ruling with proof regarding disputes over the dower amount. The ruling covers two scenarios: when only one party brings evidence, and when both bring evidence. The commentary explains each part in detail.

The matn states: 'And if both bring evidence, then her evidence if it supports him, and his evidence if it supports her.' This is explained by the sharh: if the wife claims an increase over what the husband admits, and she brings evidence, it is accepted. Similarly, if the husband alone brings evidence against her claim, it is accepted. The reason given is that evidence can be accepted to repel the oath, analogized to the case of a depositor proving return of a deposit.

When both bring evidence, the rule is that the evidence of the one whose claim aligns with the default (mahr al-mithl) is accepted, because evidence is designed to prove what contradicts the apparent, and the default in marriage is the mahr al-mithl. This is supported by the hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies,' which the commentary elaborates upon, noting that the denier holds to the original state (innocence of liability).

If there is no evidence and the mahr al-mithl lies between the two claims, the spouses take mutual oaths (tahalf). If both swear or both bring evidence, the ruling is based on mahr al-mithl. The footnotes add details: if the husband refuses to swear, the wife's claim prevails; if the wife refuses, the husband's claim prevails; and in the case of mutual oaths, lots are drawn to decide who swears first, though al-Quduri says the husband begins.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard dower for a woman of similar status in the absence of a specified mahr or when the specified mahr is invalid
تحالف (tahalf): Mutual oath-taking between spouses when there is no evidence and the mahr al-mithl lies between their claims

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both husband and wife bring evidence regarding the mahr, whose evidence is accepted?
A) The husband's evidence is always accepted
B) The wife's evidence is always accepted
C) The evidence of the one whose claim aligns with mahr al-mithl is accepted
D) Both are rejected and mutual oath is required

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mahr disputes are resolved by first determining the default mahr al-mithl, then applying the rule that evidence is accepted to prove a claim contrary to that default, with mutual oath as a fallback when no evidence and mahr al-mithl is between claims.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Mutual Oath and Mahr al-Mithl in Mahr Disputes
Explains the ruling when husband and wife dispute mahr amount with no evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on mutual oath (tahāluf) when husband and wife dispute mahr with no evidence, and identify the intra-Hanafi difference on who begins the oath.

Core Idea
When both spouses swear or bring evidence, the mahr reverts to mahr al-mithl — the default of fairness.

When a husband and wife disagree on the mahr amount and neither has proof, Islamic law provides a procedure to resolve the dispute. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi approach: mutual oath leading to mahr al-mithl, based on the prophetic hadith that allocates burden of proof and oath. We also explore a difference among Hanafi scholars on who swears first.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the absence of evidence
Recognize that the ruling applies only when both spouses claim different mahr amounts, mahr al-mithl lies between their claims, and neither has evidence (bayyinah).
If one party has evidence, that evidence is accepted without mutual oath.
2
Understand the two scenarios that trigger mahr al-mithl
The text says: if both swear (tahāluf) or if both bring evidence (bayyinah), the judge rules by mahr al-mithl. Note the parity: when proofs cancel out, the default dower applies.
3
Apply the hadith on burden of proof and oath
The hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara' is cited. In a mutual dispute where each is both claimant and defendant, the rule leads to both swearing or both bringing evidence, resulting in no resolution by oath/evidence alone.
The Hanafi method: when both swear, the judge disregards both oaths and imposes mahr al-mithl.
4
Analyze the procedural consequence of refusal (nukul)
If one party refuses to swear, the judge rules in favor of the other's claim. For example, if the husband refuses, the wife's claim is upheld.
Refusal is treated as admission.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on who begins the oath
The passage mentions two views: casting lots (to determine who swears first) vs. al-Quduri's view that the husband begins. Both are considered; the text does not give a definitive tarjih.
The mufta bihi may depend on the specific circumstances.

And if between them there is mutual oath: i.e., if the mahr al-mithl is between what the husband and wife claim, and there is no evidence for either, they perform mutual oath. If both swear or both bring evidence, the judge rules by it: i.e., by mahr al-mithl. For if both swear, he rules by mahr al-mithl, and likewise if each of them brings evidence. The original principle regarding things is non-existence, and regarding obligations is innocence. The claimant claims that his right is attached to the obligation of the defendant. This hadith in this wording is famous in fiqh books, and it is recorded in Sunan al-Bayhaqi from Ibn Abbas, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'If people were given according to their claims, men would claim the wealth and blood of others, but the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' And it is recorded in the two Sahihs with the wording: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant.' And al-Daraqutni narrated it as marfu': 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies, except in the oath of qasama.' [1] His statement: 'between what he claims' – for example, the husband claims the mahr is one thousand, and the wife claims the mahr is two thousand, and mahr al-mithl is one thousand five hundred. [2] His statement: 'and no evidence for either' – i.e., no evidence for the husband nor for the woman regarding what they claim. This is qualified because if there were evidence for one, there would be no need for mutual oath; rather, whichever of them brings evidence, it is accepted, as has passed. [3] His statement: 'they perform mutual oath' – if the husband refuses, the judge rules by two thousand; if the woman refuses, one thousand is obligatory. [4] His statement: 'if both swear' – this is on the assumption that there is no evidence for either. [0] His statement: 'the judge rules by it' – i.e., in both cases, but if they bring proof, the husband has the choice in mahr al-mithl between paying dirhams and dinars, unlike mutual oath; because the evidence of each one negates the specification of the other, so the contract becomes free of specification, so mahr al-mithl is obligatory, and not so for those who perform mutual oath. (1) It is necessary to cast lots regarding who begins the oath because there is no preponderance for either. Al-Quduri said: begin with the husband's oath, and if he refuses, what the other said becomes binding. See Majma' al-Anhur (1:360). (2) In Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (1:252). Al-Nawawi said: a good hadith. See Talkhis al-Habir (4:208), Kashf al-Khafa (1:342), etc. 10 (3) In Sahih al-Bukhari (4:1656), and Sahih Muslim (3:1336), etc. And if only one of them brings evidence, his evidence is accepted, and he did not mention this category because it is apparent. And this that we have mentioned is in the case when the marriage is in effect. So he intended to explain the difference after the occurrence of divorce, and he said:
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from hadith. It explains the Hanafi position when a husband and wife dispute the amount of mahr (dower) while the marriage is still in effect, and the mahr al-mithl (dower of a similar woman) falls between their two claims. The key ruling: if neither party has evidence (bayyinah), they perform mutual oath (tahaluf). If both swear, the judge rules by mahr al-mithl. Similarly, if both bring evidence, the judge also rules by mahr al-mithl because the evidence cancels out. This is based on the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' The commentator elaborates: If one party refuses the oath (nukul), the judge rules in favor of the other's claim. The hadith is cited from Sunan al-Bayhaqi and the two Sahihs. The passage then transitions to the case of divorce before consummation, where different rules apply.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath between two parties in a dispute; here, when husband and wife both swear regarding the mahr amount.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower appropriate for a woman similar to the wife in family status, age, beauty, etc., used when the specified mahr is unknown or disputed.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence sufficient to prove a claim, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
If both parties to a mahr dispute lack evidence, they perform mutual oath; if both swear or both have evidence, the judge imposes mahr al-mithl.

Mut'ah al-Mithl in Pre-Consummation Divorce Disputes
Ruling on mut'ah al-mithl for mahr disputes in pre-consummation divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mut'ah al-mithl when a dispute over mahr occurs after divorce before consummation, and apply the evidentiary rules for resolving such a dispute.

Core Idea
After pre-consummation divorce, mut'ah replaces mahr — the wife gets a customary gift, not the agreed mahr.

When a marriage ends before consummation and the mahr amount is disputed, the normal rules of mahr al-mithl do not apply. Instead, the wife is entitled to a consolatory gift called mut'ah al-mithl, determined by the custom for women of her status. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on how to resolve such disputes, including evidentiary rules and the role of arbitration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Confirm that the dispute occurs after a valid divorce before consummation. This is the key condition triggering mut'ah instead of mahr.
If divorce occurs after consummation, mahr al-mithl rules apply instead.
2
Recognize the Shift from Mahr to Mut'ah
Understanding that after pre-consummation divorce, the wife has no entitlement to mahr (whether specified or mahr al-mithl). The judge rules by mut'ah al-mithl, a consolatory gift based on what similar women receive.
Mut'ah al-mithl is wajib in this situation, not mahr.
3
Apply the Primary Rule: Mut'ah vs. Half Claim
If the mut'ah al-mithl is equal to or less than half of what the husband claims, the husband's statement regarding the amount he admits is accepted for that portion. Any excess beyond that is determined by mahr al-mithl (since the husband's claim may be inflated).
This is because the wife's entitlement is limited to mut'ah, not half the mahr.
4
Check Evidentiary Rules
The passage notes that if one party brings evidence (bayyinah), it is accepted. If mahr al-mithl supports one party, that party's statement is taken. If mahr al-mithl is equal for both and no evidence, they perform mutual oath (tahāluf).
These rules mirror the general dispute resolution framework for mahr, with mut'ah al-mithl replacing mahr al-mithl as the benchmark.

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for when a husband and wife dispute the amount of mahr after divorce before consummation. The key point: in pre-consummation divorce, the wife is not entitled to full mahr but to a consolatory gift (mut'ah). This mut'ah is determined by the mut'ah given to similar women (mut'ah al-mithl). If the mut'ah al-mithl is equal to or less than half of what the husband claims, the husband's statement is accepted regarding the amount he admits, and any excess beyond that is determined by mahr al-mithl. The commentator clarifies that if one party brings evidence, it is accepted, and this is derived from the earlier discussion about the case when marriage is ongoing. The passage also distinguishes the rule during marriage (where mahr al-mithl applies) and after divorce before consummation (where mut'ah al-mithl applies).

Key Terms
متعة المثل (mut'ah al-mithl): The consolatory gift (mut'ah) given to a woman divorced before consummation, based on what is customary for women of her status and attributes.
تحكيم (tahkīm): The act of making an arbitrator; here used to mean the judge rules by mut'ah al-mithl as an arbitrator.

Dower Disputes After Divorce Before Consummation: The Role of Mut'atu al-Mithl
Ruling on dower disputes after divorce before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which spouse's word is accepted in a dower dispute after divorce before consummation by comparing the mut'atu al-mithl against the husband's claimed half.

Core Idea
In dower disputes after divorce before consummation, the scale of mut'atu al-mithl decides who has the conclusive word.

When a couple divorces before consummation and the dower amount is disputed, the wife is entitled to a consolation gift (mut'atu al-mithl) rather than half the dower. The Hanafi ruling uses the mut'atu al-mithl as a benchmark: if it is equal to or less than half of what the husband claims, his word is taken; if it is equal to or greater than half of what the wife claims, her word is taken. This lesson unpacks that scale and the evidentiary rules that operate in the absence of conclusive evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of divorce
Confirm that the divorce occurred before consummation (talaq qabl al-wat'). This determines whether the wife receives half the specified mahr or mut'ah.
2
Determine the mut'atu al-mithl
Estimate the customary consolation for a woman of similar age, beauty, wealth, intellect, and family status.
3
Compare to the husband's claim
If mut'ah is equal to or less than half of what the husband claims, the husband's word is taken. He must pay the amount he admits plus any excess as mahr al-mithl.
4
Compare to the wife's claim
If mut'ah is equal to or greater than half of what the wife claims, the wife's word is taken.
5
Apply evidence rules
If one party presents evidence, it is accepted. If both present evidence, the wife's evidence is preferred if it supports the husband's claim and vice versa.

Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the matn's ruling about dower disputes after divorce before consummation. The author begins by explaining that when a dispute arises over the dower amount after divorce before consummation, the wife is entitled to a consolation gift (mut'atu al-mithl) rather than half the dower. The commentary details three scenarios: (1) If the mut'ah equals or is less than half of what the husband claims, the husband's word is accepted, and he must pay what he admits plus the difference as customary dower. (2) If the mut'ah equals or exceeds half of what the wife claims, the wife's word is accepted. (3) The rules of evidence: if one party produces evidence, it is accepted; if both do, the wife's evidence is preferred if it supports the husband's claim, and vice versa. If neither has evidence and the customary dower is balanced, they take oaths, and then the mut'ah becomes obligatory. The commentary then addresses the situation after death. If one spouse dies, the ruling remains the same as when both were alive, because customary dower is not invalidated by death. However, if both spouses die and their heirs dispute, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa holds that the husband's heirs have the say (as deniers), and no customary dower is enforced because the woman's peers are extinct, making estimation impossible. Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the other hand, maintains that the ruling is the same as during life, so customary dower can be applied. The passage concludes with a note that the word 'hukm' is derived from 'tahkim' (appointing an arbitrator).

Key Terms
متعة المثل (mut'atu al-mithl): A consolation gift given to a divorced woman before consummation, determined by what is customary for women of similar status and attributes.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower for women of similar status to the bride, used as a benchmark when the dower is not specified or is disputed.
طلاق قبل الوطء (talaq qabl al-wat'): Divorce pronounced before the marriage has been consummated.
تحالفا (tahālafā): They both take oaths (each swearing to their claim), used when there is no evidence and the customary dower is balanced between their claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute after divorce before consummation, if the mut'atu al-mithl equals half of the wife's claim, who has the word and what is owed?
A) Husband's word; he pays his admitted amount plus mahr al-mithl.
B) Wife's word; she gets her claimed amount.
C) Both take oaths; the wife gets mut'ah.
D) The judge decides the amount arbitrarily.

Key Takeaway
In dower disputes after divorce before consummation, the mut'atu al-mithl serves as a decisive benchmark: whoever's half-claim it matches or is below/above determines whose word prevails.

Dower Disputes After Death of One or Both Spouses: Abu Hanifa vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dower after death of both spouses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and Muhammad al-Shaybani's positions on dower disputes when both spouses have died and the heirs disagree.

Core Idea
After both spouses die, Abu Hanifa drops mahr al-mithl due to extinction of peers; Muhammad al-Shaybani maintains it as during life.

When both spouses have died and their heirs dispute the dower amount, the Hanafi school contains a key internal divergence. Abu Hanifa rules that the husband's heirs have the word as deniers, and no mahr al-mithl is enforced because the wife's peers are extinct, making estimation impossible. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, holding that death does not nullify mahr al-mithl, so the judge can still estimate it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequences of both views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Both spouses have died, and their heirs dispute the dower amount (whether it was specified or not).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
The husband's heirs (deniers) have the word; they pay what they admit. No mahr al-mithl is enforced because the woman's peers are extinct after her death, making estimation impossible.
3
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's position
The ruling is the same as during life: mahr al-mithl is not invalidated by death. The judge can estimate it based on the woman's status before death.
4
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa relies on the impossibility of estimating mahr al-mithl after the woman's peers are gone, as mahr varies with time. Muhammad al-Shaybani compares to the mufawwidah (woman without specified dower) who still gets mahr al-mithl if one dies.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the husband's heirs only owe what they admit. Under Muhammad, the judge can enforce a mahr al-mithl estimated by experts, potentially higher.

Likewise in al-Durar. His statement: So regarding the amount; meaning if a dispute occurs between the heirs of the husband and the heirs of the woman after their death, and the word is for the husband's heirs, so what they admit is binding on them, and they deny the increase, so the oath reverts to them, and customary dower is not judged because its consideration is dropped after her death according to Abu Hanifa. And according to Muhammad, the ruling therein is like the ruling during life. His statement: And regarding its principle; meaning if a dispute occurs between their heirs, the word is of the deniers of the stipulation who are the heirs of the husband, and nothing is ruled unless the stipulation is proved; because the consideration of customary dower is dropped by their death according to him; because their death indicates the extinction of their peers, so it is not possible for the judge to estimate customary dower; because customary dower varies with different times, so if the time is remote...

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the matn's ruling about dower disputes after divorce before consummation. The author begins by explaining that when a dispute arises over the dower amount after divorce before consummation, the wife is entitled to a consolation gift (mut'atu al-mithl) rather than half the dower. The commentary details three scenarios: (1) If the mut'ah equals or is less than half of what the husband claims, the husband's word is accepted, and he must pay what he admits plus the difference as customary dower. (2) If the mut'ah equals or exceeds half of what the wife claims, the wife's word is accepted. (3) The rules of evidence: if one party produces evidence, it is accepted; if both do, the wife's evidence is preferred if it supports the husband's claim, and vice versa. If neither has evidence and the customary dower is balanced, they take oaths, and then the mut'ah becomes obligatory. The commentary then addresses the situation after death. If one spouse dies, the ruling remains the same as when both were alive, because customary dower is not invalidated by death. However, if both spouses die and their heirs dispute, there is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa holds that the husband's heirs have the say (as deniers), and no customary dower is enforced because the woman's peers are extinct, making estimation impossible. Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the other hand, maintains that the ruling is the same as during life, so customary dower can be applied. The passage concludes with a note that the word 'hukm' is derived from 'tahkim' (appointing an arbitrator).

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dower for women of similar status, used when the dower is disputed or unspecified.
مفوضة (mufawwidah): A woman whose marriage contract did not specify a dower, leaving it to be determined by mahr al-mithl.
ورثة (warathah): Heirs who inherit from the deceased spouse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is mahr al-mithl not enforced after both spouses have died?
A) Because the husband's heirs deny the higher amount.
B) Because the wife's peers are extinct, making estimation impossible.
C) Because the dower was not specified in writing.
D) Because death nullifies all contractual obligations.

Key Takeaway
After both spouses die, Abu Hanifa drops mahr al-mithl due to extinct peers, while Muhammad al-Shaybani maintains it; the practical difference lies in whether the heir can be compelled to pay more than they admit.

Disagreement About Mahr After Death of Spouses
Ruling on mahr disputes after death of both spouses, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on mahr disputes after the death of both spouses, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
After both spouses die, mahr al-mithl is not applied if the time is distant due to estimation difficulty.

This passage from the chapter on Mahr addresses a nuanced scenario: when both husband and wife have died, and their heirs dispute the amount of the specified dower. The Hanafi school has an internal difference on whether mahr al-mithl can be adjudicated. Let's explore the reasoning behind each position and how the fatwa is established.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine the exact scenario: both spouses have died, and their heirs disagree about the amount of the specified dower (al-musamma). This is not about the origin (whether a dower was specified) but about the amount.
The text distinguishes between disputes about origin (asl) and amount (qadr).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the statement of the husband's heirs is accepted (with oath) regarding the amount, and mahr al-mithl is not adjudicated. His reasoning: death of both implies extinction of peers, making estimation of mahr al-mithl impossible; it varies with time.
He makes an exception if the time is recent—then he would adjudicate mahr al-mithl.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) analogize to the case when both are alive: they adjudicate mahr al-mithl even after both deaths.
Their analogy: the underlying cause (need for a fair dower) remains; estimation is still possible if witnesses exist.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: whether the judge will estimate mahr al-mithl or accept the husband's heirs' admission. The mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa) is the Sahibayn's view, so mahr al-mithl is adjudicated, especially if the time is recent.
The text explicitly states: 'and that is the fatwa.'

Source Text

وإن كانت بينهما تحالفا وموت أحدهما كحياتِهما في الحكم ، وبعد موتهما : ففي القدر القولُ لورثته ، وفي أصله لم يقض للمنكر بشيء وإن كانت مساوية لنصف ما تدَّعيه المرأة أو أكثر منه فالقول لها. وأي أقام بينة قبلت، وإن أقاما فبينة المرأة أولى إن شهدت له متعة المثل، وبينة الزوج أولى إن شهدت لها.
قوله : وموت ؛ أي إذا مات أحد الزوجين ووقع الاختلاف بين ورثة الميت وبين الحي منهما في الأصل أو القدر، فالحكم هو ما مرَّ في كل صورة بتفاصيله المارة؛ لأن مهر المثل لا يسقط اعتباراً بموت أحدهما.
قوله : ففي القدر ؛ يعني إذا وقع الاختلاف بين ورثة الزوج وبين ورثة المرأة بعد موتهما، والقولُ لورثة الزوج فيلزمهم ما اعترفوا به، وهم ينكرون الزيادة، فيعود عليهم اليمين، ولا يُحَكِّمُ مهر المثل؛ لأن اعتباره يسقط بعد موتها هذا عند أبي حنيفة. ومحمد له الحكم فيه كالحكم حال الحياة.
قوله : وفي أصله ؛ أي إن وقع الاختلاف بين ورثتهما، فالقول قول منكري التسمية وهم ورثة الزوج، ولم يقض بشيء ما لم يبرهن على التسمية؛ لأن اعتبار مهر المثل يسقط بموتهما عنده؛ لأن موتهما يدلُّ على انقراض أقرانهما، فلا يمكن للقاضي أن يقدر مهر المثل؛ لأن مهر المثل يختلف باختلاف الأوقات، فإذا تقادم العهد يتعذر الوقوف على مقداره، فلو كان العهد قريباً قضى به. ذكره قاضي خان في «شرح الجامع الصغير».
وقالا : قَضَى بمهر المثل ، وبه يُفتى
If there was mutual swearing between them, and one of them dies, it is like both being alive in the ruling. After both die: regarding the amount, the statement is of his heirs; and regarding the origin, nothing is decreed for the denier. If it is equal to half of what the woman claims or more, then her statement is accepted. Whichever establishes evidence, it is accepted. If both establish evidence, the woman's evidence is preferred if it supports that a mut'ah (gift) is due to him, and the man's evidence is preferred if it supports that a mut'ah is due to her. Explanation: 'And death': i.e., if one of the spouses dies and a dispute occurs between the heirs of the deceased and the surviving spouse regarding the origin or amount, the ruling is as previously detailed in each scenario, because the dower of the like is not nullified by the death of one. 'Regarding the amount': i.e., if a dispute occurs between the heirs of the husband and the heirs of the woman after both die, the statement is of the husband's heirs, so what they acknowledge is binding, and they deny the increase, so the oath is upon them, and the dower of the like is not adjudicated because its consideration is nullified after her death according to Abu Hanifa. Muhammad says the ruling is as during life. 'Regarding the origin': i.e., if a dispute occurs between their heirs, the statement is of the deniers of specification, i.e., the husband's heirs, and nothing is decreed unless the specification is proven, because the consideration of the dower of the like is nullified by their death according to him, because their death indicates the extinction of their peers, so the judge cannot estimate the dower of the like, as it varies with time; if the period was recent, he would adjudicate it. Qadi Khan mentioned this in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He adjudicates the dower of the like, and that is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from the chapter of Mahr deals with disputes about the dower after the death of one or both spouses. The base text (matn) states the general rule: when one spouse dies, the ruling is the same as if both were alive. When both die, the husband's heirs' word is accepted concerning the amount of the specified dower (al-musamma), and they take an oath. No judgment is made beyond what they admit. However, if the specified amount equals or exceeds half of what the wife claimed, the wife's word is accepted (this is a separate scenario from the death context, actually part of the broader discussion). The commentary elaborates: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that when both die, the dower of the like (mahr al-mithl) is not considered because the passage of time makes it impossible to determine the comparable value. Imam Muhammad agrees with the ruling in the matn. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that if the time is recent, the judge should decide based on mahr al-mithl, and this is the position relied upon for fatwa. The reasoning is that mahr al-mithl varies with time, and if the delay is long, estimation is impossible.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The dower of a woman similar to the wife in family, locality, and other relevant attributes, used when no dower is specified.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking between spouses when they disagree about the dower and have no evidence.
القول لورثته (al-qawl li-warathatihi): The statement of the husband's heirs is accepted (with oath) regarding the amount of the specified dower after his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When both spouses have died, and their heirs dispute the amount of the specified dower, what is the position of Abu Hanifa?
A) The statement of the wife's heirs is accepted.
B) Mahr al-mithl is always adjudicated.
C) No judgment is given unless the specified amount is proven; mahr al-mithl is not applied due to difficulty of estimation.
D) The heirs of both take an oath and share equally.

Key Takeaway
Although Abu Hanifa declines mahr al-mithl after both deaths, the relied-upon fatwa follows the Sahibayn: adjudicate mahr al-mithl if the time is recent.

Dispute Over Sent Item: Gift or Mahr?
Ruling when husband sends something and they dispute its purpose as gift or mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving disputes over the purpose of a sent item in marriage (gift vs. mahr) and identify the exception for food items based on custom.

Core Idea
When husband and wife dispute whether a sent item is gift or mahr, the husband's word is accepted except for food items, which custom treats as gifts.

In marriage transactions, it is common for a husband to send items to his wife before or after the wedding. A dispute may arise later about whether the item was a gift or part of the mahr payment. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is taken as true. The default rule favors the husband, but an exception exists for items customarily given as gifts – particularly food prepared for eating. By studying this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, you will learn the legal reasoning and the critical role of custom ('urf) in shaping the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
When the husband sends something and a dispute arises, the default is that the husband's word is accepted (with his oath). This is because he is the one paying the mahr and knows his intention.
The matn states: 'al-qawlu lahu' – the statement is his.
2
Locate the exception
The exception is for items 'prepared for eating' (ma hi'a li'l-akl), such as bread. For these, the wife's word is accepted. The reason is that such items are customarily sent as gifts, not as part of the mahr.
The exception is introduced by 'illa' (except).
3
Understand the basis: custom ('urf)
The exception is founded on customary practice ('urf). In the Hanafi school, custom is a recognized source of law when not contradicted by a primary text. The commentary from al-Fath explains that in many regions, items like wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, and live sheep are also customarily considered gifts, so the wife's word is accepted for them.
Custom can vary by time and place, so the ruling may adapt.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Practice applying the rule: if the husband sends bread, the wife's claim that it is a gift is accepted. If he sends gold coins, the husband's claim that it is part of the mahr is accepted, unless local custom dictates otherwise.
The key is to determine which items are customarily given as gifts in the specific society.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن بعث إليها شيئاً، فقالت : هو هدية، وقال هو مهر، فالقول له إلا فيما هيء للأكل كالخبز بخلاف الحنطة.
قوله : شيئاً ; أي من النقدين أو العروض، أو مما يؤكل قبل الزفاف، أو بعده.
قوله : فالقول له ; أي مع يمينه وإقامة البينة عليها، فإن حلف والمبعوث قائم فلها أن ترده وترجع بباقي المهر أو كله، وإن هلك وقد بقي لأحدهما شيء رجع به، ولو كانت قيمةُ الهالك مساوياً للمهر فلا رجوع لأحد.
قوله : إلا فيما هيء ; بصيغة المجهول يعني إن بعث إليها شيئاً ما يُهيء للأكل، ووقع الاختلاف في جهته، فالقولُ لها بيمينها ; لأن الظاهر كما قال في «الفتح»: «الذي يجب اعتبارُهُ في ديارنا أن جميع ما ذكر من الحنطة واللوز والدقيق والسكر والشاة الحية وما فيها يكون القول فيها قول المرأة ; لأنَّ المتعارف في ذلك كله أن يرسله هدية
If he sends something to her, and she says: it is a gift, and he says: it is mahr, then the statement is his except for what is prepared for eating, like bread, contrary to wheat. Explanation: 'something': i.e., coins, goods, or items to be eaten before or after the wedding. 'The statement is his': i.e., with his oath and establishment of evidence; if he swears and the sent item still exists, she may return it and claim the remainder of the mahr or all of it; if it perished while something remains for one of them, he takes it back; if the value of the perished item equals the mahr, no one has recourse. 'Except for what is prepared': passive voice, meaning if he sends something prepared for eating, and a dispute occurs about its category, then her statement (with oath) is accepted; because the apparent, as stated in al-Fath: 'What must be considered in our lands is that all that is mentioned—wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, a live sheep, and what is in it—the statement is the woman's, because the customary practice in all that is to send it as a gift.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses a common dispute: the husband sends something to the wife (before or after the wedding), and later they disagree whether it was a gift or part of the mahr payment. The default ruling is that the husband's word is accepted, with his oath, because he is the payer and knows his intention. The wife can return the item if it still exists. However, an exception is made for items prepared for eating, such as bread or other cooked foods, where the wife's word is accepted. The reason is custom ('urf): people customarily send such items as gifts, not as part of the mahr. The commentary from al-Fath explains that in their region, items like wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, and live sheep are also considered gifts by custom, so the woman's word is accepted for those as well. This shows that the ruling can vary based on local custom.

Key Terms
هيء للأكل (hi'a li'l-akl): Items prepared for eating, such as bread, as opposed to raw ingredients like wheat.
متعارف (mut'āraf): Customary practice that serves as evidence for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The wife's statement is accepted because bread is customarily a gift.
C) Both must swear and then divide the value.
D) The judge decides based on the item's value.

Key Takeaway
In disputes over whether a sent item is a gift or part of mahr, the husband's word is default, but custom can shift the burden to the wife for items customarily given as gifts.

Dispute over Gift vs. Mahr: Husband's Word Accepted Except for Food
Ruling on dispute when husband sends something unclear as gift or mahr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband and wife dispute whether a sent item was a gift or part of the mahr, and identify the exception for food items based on custom (urf).

Core Idea
Husband's word accepted for mahr claim except for food, where wife's word accepted due to custom.

When a husband sends gifts or mahr items to his wife, disputes can arise about the nature of the items. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling: the husband's claim that it was mahr is generally accepted, but for food items custom contradicts that presumption. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its basis in custom, drawing on the commentary of authoritative Hanafi texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The scenario is that the husband sends something to the wife (before or after marriage) without specifying its purpose. Later, the wife claims it was a gift, while the husband claims it was part of the mahr.
If the husband had specified at the time of sending that it was a gift, his later claim that it was mahr is not accepted.
2
State the general rule
The general rule is: 'His word is accepted' (al-qawl lahu). This means the husband's claim that the item was part of the mahr is given credence over the wife's claim that it was a gift, provided he swears an oath and the wife has no evidence to the contrary.
The husband must also swear an oath (yamin). The wife can still produce evidence (bayyina) to support her claim.
3
Identify the exception
The exception is for items that are 'prepared for eating' (huyyi'a li'l-akl), such as bread, cooked food, almonds, sugar, etc. For these, the wife's word is accepted because custom (urf) dictates that such items are usually sent as gifts, not as mahr.
Raw materials like wheat (not yet processed into bread) are not included in this exception; the general rule applies to them.
4
Understand the proof: custom (urf)
The proof for the exception is the prevailing custom in the society. The text cites al-Fath and al-Nahr, stating that in the lands of the commentators, custom is that food items are sent as gifts. Custom is a valid source of law in Hanafi fiqh, especially in matters of evidence and usage.
The custom may change over time or differ by region. A mufti should assess the local custom when applying this ruling.
5
Note the consequences and conditions
The passage explains that if the item still exists, the wife may return it and demand the full mahr. If it is destroyed, compensation is calculated based on its value. Also, if the item is a combination (e.g., clothes with sugar), the ruling for the whole may follow the predominant custom.
The ruling applies only when the husband sent the item without specifying its purpose. If he clearly stated it was a gift, the ruling does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب المهر
وقالا : قَضَى بمهر المثل ، وبه يُفتى ، وإن بعث إليها شيئاً، فقالت : هو هدية، وقال مهر، فالقول له إلا فيما هيء للأكل : كالخبز بخلاف الحنطة. يتعذر الوقوف على مقداره ، فلو كان العهد قريباً قضى به. ذكره قاضي خان في «شرح الجامع الصغير». كذا في «البحر»
[١] قوله : به يفتي ؛ أي بالقضاء بمهر المثل في صورة موتهما. كما في صورة حياتهما.
[٢] قوله : شيئاً ؛ أي من النقدين أو العروض، أو مما يؤكل قبل الزفاف، أو بعده. كذا في «النهر»، والمراد أنه بعثَهُ من غير ذكر جهة، فوقع الاختلاف بينهما ، فقالت : بعثتها إلي ى هدية ، وقال: بعثته على جهة المهر، وأما إن ذكر عند الإرسال جهةً غير المهر، ثم قال : إنه من المهر لا يقبل قوله لأن ما يقعُ هدية لا ينقلب مهراً. كذا في «القُنية»، وغيرها. [٣] قوله : فالقول له ؛ أي مع يمينه وإقامة البيِّنة عليها، فإن حلف والمبعوث قائم فلها أن ترده وترجع بباقي المهر أو كله، وإن هلك وقد بقي لأحدهما شيء رجع به، ولو كانت قيمةُ الهالك مساوياً للمهر فلا رجوع لأحد. كذا في «النهر»، وغيره. [٤] قوله : إلا فيما هيء ؛ بصيغة المجهول يعني إن بعث إليها شيئاً ما يُهيء للأكل، ووقع الاختلاف في جهته، فالقولُ لها بيمينها ؛ لأن الظاهر كما قال في «الفتح»: «الذي يجب اعتبارُهُ في ديارنا أن جميع ما ذكر من الحنطة واللوز والدقيق والسكر والشاة الحية وما فيها يكون القول فيها قول المرأة ؛ لأنَّ المتعارف في ذلك كله أن يرسله هدية ، والظاهر معها لا معه، ولا يكون القول قوله إلاّ في نحو الثياب والجارية». انتهى) وفي «النهر»: «ينبغي أن لا يقبل قوله أيضاً في الثياب المحمولة مع السكر ونحوه للعرف.
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He judges by mahr al-mithl, and by this fatwa is given. And if he sent something to her, and she said it is a gift, and he said it is mahr, then his word is accepted except for what is prepared for eating: like bread, unlike wheat. It is impossible to ascertain its amount, so if the time is near, he judges by it. Qadi Khan mentioned it in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir. Likewise in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'by this fatwa is given' means by judging mahr al-mithl in the case of their death, as in the case of their life. [2] His statement: 'something' means from dinars/dirhams or goods, or from what is eaten before or after the wedding. The meaning is that he sent it without mentioning the purpose, so disagreement occurred between them. She said: 'You sent it to me as a gift,' and he said: 'I sent it as mahr.' But if he mentioned at the time of sending a purpose other than mahr, then later claims it was from mahr, his statement is not accepted because what was given as a gift does not become mahr. [3] His statement: 'his word is accepted' means with his oath and her establishing evidence. If he swears and the sent item remains, she may return it and claim the remaining mahr or all of it. If it is destroyed and something remains for one of them, he takes it back. If the value of the destroyed item equals the mahr, neither has recourse. [4] His statement: 'except for what is prepared' in the passive voice means if he sent something prepared for eating and they dispute its purpose, her word is accepted with her oath because the apparent is as said in al-Fath: 'What must be considered in our lands is that for all mentioned—wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, a live sheep, etc.—the woman's word is accepted because custom is that all such are sent as gifts, and the apparent is with her, not with him. His word is not accepted except for things like clothes and a slave girl.' End quote. And in al-Nahr: 'It is appropriate that his word should not be accepted for clothes carried with sugar and the like, due to custom.'
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a common dispute in marriage: when a husband sends an item to his wife (before or after marriage) and the wife claims it was a gift while the husband claims it was part of the mahr (dower). The text begins by stating that the two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, rule that in such cases the judge decides mahr al-mithl (proper dower) for the wife if the husband dies, and this is the position given for fatwa. Then the main ruling: the husband's word is accepted when he says the item was part of the mahr, EXCEPT for items prepared for eating (like bread, cooked food). For food items, the wife's word is accepted, and she can claim it was a gift. The reason is custom: in their societies, food items are normally sent as gifts, not as mahr. The commentary explains that this ruling applies when the husband sent the item without specifying its purpose. If he clearly stated it was a gift at the time of sending, he cannot later change his mind. The husband must also swear an oath, and the wife can establish evidence. If the item still exists, the wife may return it and demand the mahr; if destroyed, compensation is calculated. The exception for food is based on the prevailing custom (urf) in those lands, as elaborated from al-Fath and al-Nahr.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower equivalent to what is given to women of similar status in the bride's family.
هيء للأكل (huyyi'a li'l-akl): Items prepared or intended for eating, such as bread, cooked food, etc.
القول له (al-qawl lahu): His statement is accepted; i.e., the husband's claim is given credence over the wife's in the absence of evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Bread (cooked)
C) Gold jewelry
D) A slave girl (jariya)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband sends an item to his wife and they dispute whether it was a gift or mahr, the husband's word is accepted except for food items prepared for eating, where the wife's word is accepted due to custom (urf).

Marriage of Non-Muslims with Invalid Mahr: No Mahr Due
Ruling on marriage of dhimmis/harbis with no mahr or prohibited items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr when a dhimmi/harbi marries with no mahr or prohibited items, and explain the principle of 'leaving them to their beliefs'.

Core Idea
A marriage valid in the couple's religion is not retroactively corrected by Islam: no mahr is due even after conversion.

This lesson explores a unique aspect of Hanafi fiqh: the treatment of marriages contracted by non-Muslims under their own laws. The key principle is that we do not impose Islamic mahr rules retroactively. By the end, you will understand why and how this applies in various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the mahr situation
Determine whether the couple are dhimmis (in Islamic state) or harbis (in dar al-harb). Identify the mahr: none, dead animal, wine, or pig.
These are the specific examples given in the text.
2
Check validity in their religion
Ascertain whether such a mahr (or lack thereof) is permissible in their own religious law. The text assumes it is permissible.
If their religion requires a mahr al-mithl, the ruling may differ.
3
Apply the principle of non-interference
Apply the principle 'We are commanded to leave them and what they believe' (أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون). This means we do not enforce Islamic mahr rules on a contract that was valid at its inception.
This is a core Hanafi maxim for interfaith matters.
4
Determine the ruling on mahr
Conclude that no mahr is due from the husband, even if the couple later converts to Islam or brings the case to a Muslim judge.
Other effects of marriage (maintenance, divorce, inheritance) remain valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن نكح ذمي ذميّة، أو حربي حربية ثمة ، بميتة، أو بلا مهر، وذا جائز عندهم فوطئت ، أو طُلقت قبلَهُ ، أو مات أحدهما ، فلا مهرلها ، وإن نكحها بخمر، أو خنزير عين ، ثم أسلما ، أو أسلم أحدهما
[١]قوله : ذمي؛ هو الكافرُ الذي قبل الجزية في دار الإسلام، وفي حكمه المستأمن. كما في «العناية»، وفي الإطلاق إشارة إلى أن هذا حكم كل كافر مشركاً كان أو كتابياً. [٢]قوله : وذا جائز عندهم ؛ بأن كان في مذهبهم لا يلزم مهر المثل، ولا شيء بنفي المهر، وبجعل المهر ما ليس بمال. [٣] قوله : هذا ؛ أي النكاح بلا مهر أو مع نفيه. وكذا النكاح بالميتة. [٤]قوله : أو يجب المهر ؛ أي كان في مذهبهم وجوب المهر مهر المثل في مثل هذه الصور. [٥]قوله : عدم وجوب المهر ؛ بل يجب المهر، والوجه في ذلك أنا أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون. [٦] قوله : فلا مهر لها ؛ أي ولو أسلما، أو رفع أحدهما ، أو كلاهما أمرهما إلينا، وتثبت بقية أحكام النكاح من وجوب النفقة ووقوع الطلاق والعدة والنسب وتوارث بنكاح صحيح وغير ذلك.
If a dhimmi marries a dhimmi woman, or a harbi marries a harbi woman there, with a dead animal, or without any mahr, and this is permissible in their religion, then she is consummated, or divorced before that, or one of them dies, then there is no mahr for her. And if he married her with wine or a specified pig, then they both embrace Islam, or one of them embraces Islam [the ruling is the same]. [1] His statement: 'dhimmi' is the disbeliever who accepts jizya in the Abode of Islam; and the musta'min is in his ruling. This indicates that this ruling applies to all disbelievers, whether polytheist or People of the Book. [2] His statement: 'and this is permissible in their religion' means that in their madhhab, mahr al-mithl is not required, and nothing is required by negating the mahr, and by making the mahr something that is not property. [3] His statement: 'this' refers to marriage without mahr or with its negation, and similarly marriage with a dead animal. [4] His statement: 'or if they require the mahr' means if in their madhhab they require the mahr al-mithl in such situations. [5] His statement: 'absence of obligation of mahr' rather, the mahr is required, and the reason is that we are commanded to leave them and what they believe. [6] His statement: 'so there is no mahr for her' means even if they both embrace Islam, or one of them, or both bring their case to us, and the remaining rulings of marriage are established: obligation of maintenance, occurrence of divorce, waiting period, lineage, and inheritance from a valid marriage, etc.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage deals with marriages contracted by non-Muslims (dhimmis in Islamic state or harbis in enemy territory) where the mahr is either absent or consists of something forbidden in Islam (dead animal, wine, pig). The key principle stated in the commentary (footnote 5) is: 'We are commanded to leave them and what they believe' (أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون). This means that as long as the marriage is valid according to their own religion, we do not interfere even if the mahr is invalid in Islam. The ruling: if they marry with no mahr or with a dead animal, and then the wife is consummated or divorced before consummation or one dies, no mahr is due from the husband under Islamic law. This remains the case even if they later convert to Islam or bring their case to a Muslim judge. The reason is that we do not retroactively impose Islamic mahr rules on a marriage that was valid in their own faith at the time. The commentary also clarifies that this applies to all non-Muslims (polytheists or People of the Book), and that if their religion actually requires a mahr al-mithl, then our ruling might differ, but the text assumes their religion does not require it. The marriage itself, if valid in their religion, is considered a valid marriage in Islam for purposes of other rulings like maintenance, divorce, iddah, and inheritance.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state who pays jizya.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic rule and is considered in a state of war (or from dar al-harb).
ميتة (maytah): A dead animal, which is not permissible as mahr in Islam but may be permissible in their religion.
خمر (khamr): Wine/intoxicating beverage, prohibited in Islam but potentially permissible in their religion.
خنزير (khinzir): Pig, which is also not considered mal (valuable property) in Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must pay mahr al-mithl of a Muslim woman.
B) No mahr is due from him.
C) The wife chooses between mahr al-mithl and no mahr.
D) The marriage is invalid, so no mahr.

Key Takeaway
No mahr is due in marriages of non-Muslims that were valid in their religion, even after conversion, because we leave them to their beliefs.

Nikah — Marriage
