10 min
Mahr in Non-Muslim Marriages with Invalid Dower: The Hanafi Approach
Ruling and commentary on mahr in non-Muslim marriages with invalid dower

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity and mahr of dhimmi/harbi marriages contracted with carrion, no mahr, wine, or pig, and apply the adjustments upon conversion.

Core Idea
We leave them and their customs: non-Muslim marriages with invalid dower are valid; post-Islam, original mahr stands with adjustments for impurity.

This lesson explores a peculiar but important fiqh issue: what happens to the dower (mahr) when non-Muslims (dhimmis or harbis) marry with items that are unlawful in Islamic law (carrion, wine, pig) or without any dower, and then convert to Islam. The Hanafi school upholds the original marriage contract based on the principle 'we leave them and their customs' (nahn umirnā bi-tarkihim wa mā yadīnūn). However, upon conversion, the dower is enforced as originally agreed, with modifications to avoid consuming forbidden substances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the initial marriage contract
Determine whether the parties are dhimmis (protected non-Muslims under Muslim rule) or harbis (non-Muslims from enemy territory). Note that the original marriage included either carrion, no mahr, a specific wine/pig, or an unspecified wine/pig as dower.
The status of dhimmi/harbi affects jurisdiction but not the core ruling.
2
Apply the principle of 'leaving them and their customs'
Recognize that we do not nullify their marriages merely because the dower is invalid in Islam. As long as their own religion considered the contract valid, we uphold it. This principle is the foundation of the ruling.
The text states: "نحن أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون" (We are commanded to leave them and what they practice).
3
Determine the consequence for carrion or no mahr
If the marriage was with carrion or without any mahr, and it was permissible in their religion, then upon conversion (or even if they remain non-Muslim) no mahr is due. The marriage stands, but the dower is non-existent.
Even after Islam, she gets nothing because the original agreement lacked a valid mahr.
4
Distinguish between specified and unspecified wine or pig
If the dower was a specific (ʿayn) wine or pig, she receives that item after conversion, but the Muslim husband must dispose of it (e.g., pour away wine, release the pig). If the dower was unspecified, she receives the value of wine (since wine is fungible) or mahr al-mithl for the pig (since pig is non-fungible). The rationale: taking the equivalent of a fungible is like taking the item itself, but since the item is ḥarām, we substitute with value.
Two objections are raised and answered in the text: 1) why allow taking the specified item when we must turn away from it? 2) why treat preemption differently? Study the answers.
5
Analyze the objections and responses
The commentary addresses two objections: (a) since a Muslim must avoid wine/pig, why is she allowed to take the specified item? Answer: because when specified, there is no alternative; value can be imposed only when unspecified. (b) In preemption (shufʿa), a Muslim preemptor can take a house sold for a pig by paying its value, so why not here? Answer: in preemption, value is a substitute for the house, not the pig, and there is a necessity to protect the preemptor's right; here no such necessity.
These objections refine the application of the rule and show the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن نكح ذمي ذميّة، أو حربي حربية ثمة، بميتة، أو بلا مهر، وذا جائز عندهم فوطئت، أو طُلقت قبلَهُ، أو مات أحدهما، فلا مهرلها، وإن نكحها بخمر، أو خنزير عين، ثم أسلما، أو أسلم أحدهما (فإن نكح ذمي () ذميّةً، أو حربي حربيةً ثُمَّة): أي في دار الحرب، (بميتة، أو بلا مهر، وذا جائز عندهم): أي والحالُ أنّ النكاح بلا مهر يجوز عندهم، ولا يجب شيء؛ وإنما قال هذا لأنه إن لم يجز هذا في دينهم، أو يجب المهر لا يكون حكم المسألة عدم وجوب (المهر)، (فوطئت، أو طُلقت قبلَهُ، أو مات أحدهما، فلا مهر لها. وإن نكحها بخمر، أو خِنزير عين، ثُمَّ أسلما، أو أسلم أحدهما [٤] (1) عندهم والظاهر معها لا معه، ولا يكون القول قوله إلاّ في نحو الثياب والجارية». انتهى) وفي «النهر»: «ينبغي أن لا يقبل قوله أيضاً في الثياب المحمولة مع السكر ونحوه للعرف(٢). (۱) من «فتح القدير»(۳: ۳۸۰). (۲) انتهى من «النهر الفائق»(٢ : ٢٦٥). عن كتاب النكاح باب المهر فلها ذلك، وفي غير عين فقيمة الخمر فيها، ومهر المثل في الخنزير فلها ذلك، وفي غير عين) فقيمة الخمر فيها، ومهر المثل في الخنزير)؛ لأنَّ الخمر عندهم مثلي كالخل عندنا، ولا يحلُّ أخذها، فإيجاب القيمة يكون إعراضاً الخمر، وأما الخنزير" فمن ذواتِ القيم عندهم كالشاة عندنا، فإيجاب القيمة لا [١] قوله: فلها ذلك؛ أي عين الخمر والخنزير المقدر عند النكاح بكماله، وإن طلقها قبل الدخول، فلها نصفه؛ لأن تسليم الخمر والخنزير وإن كان حراماً للمسلم لكن هاهنا جُعِلَ مهراً في حالة كان ذلك جائزاً له فيها، ويجب على المسلم أن يخلل الخمر أو يريقه ويرسل الخنزير). [٢]قوله: وفي غير عين؛ أي فيما إذا كان المهر الخمر، أو الخنزير مطلقاً غير مشار إليه. [٣] قوله: وأما الخنزير ... الخ؛ حاصلُهُ: أن أخذ المثل في المثلي، والقيمة في القيمي، بمنزلة أخذ عينه، وليس أخذ القيمة في المثلي كأخذ عينه، فَلَمَّا كان الخمرُ مثلياً، ولم يمكن أخذ مثله للزوم أخذ عينه، فحكم بأخذ قيمته ليكون إعراضاً عن الخمر والخنزير لما كان من ذوات القيم، فأخذ قيمته يكون كأخذه؛ فلذلك حكم فيه بوجوب مهر المثل. واعترض هنا بوجهين: أحدهما: إن الإعراض لَمَّا كان واجباً على المسلم، فَلِمَ حُكم بأخذ الخمر والخنزير في صورة كون المهر الخمر والخنزير العين. وأجيب عنه: بأن هناك كان المهر عيناً مشاراً إليه، فلا سبيل إلى أخذ مثله أو قیمته، وهاهنا ليس بعين فيمكن إيجاب القيمة. وثانيهما: إنه لو شرى ذمي من ذمي داراً بخنزير وشفيعها مسلم كان له أخذها بقيمة الخنزير، فما وجه الفرق؟ وأجيب: بأن القيمة في مسألة الشفعة بدلّ عن الدار عن الخنزير، وإنما صير إليها للتقدير بها مع أن هناك ضرورة، وهي إبقاء حق الشفيع، ولا ضرورة هاهنا ليتيسر المصير إلى مهر المثل. (1) ينظر: «مجمع الأنهر» (۱) : (٣٦٣)، و (رد المحتار (٢ : ٣٦٨)، وغيرهما.
If a dhimmi marries a dhimmi woman, or a harbi marries a harbi woman in the dar al-harb, with carrion or without mahr, and this is permissible according to them, and he had intercourse with her, or she was divorced before that, or one of them dies, then she has no mahr. And if he marries her with wine or a specific pig, then they both become Muslim, or one of them becomes Muslim (then she gets that item). If it is not specific, then she gets the value of wine and mahr al-mithl for the pig. (Explanation: wine is fungible like vinegar for us, and taking it is not permissible, so imposing value is a turning away from wine; as for the pig, it is a non-fungible item like a sheep for us, so imposing value is like taking it. Two objections are raised and answered: 1) Since turning away is obligatory on a Muslim, why is taking the specific wine/pig allowed? Answer: Because when specified, there is no alternative; when unspecified, value can be imposed. 2) If a dhimmi buys a house from another dhimmi with a pig, and the Muslim preemptor can take it by paying the pig's value, what is the difference? Answer: In preemption, value is a substitute for the house, not the pig, and there is a necessity to preserve the preemptor's right; no such necessity here.)
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and its commentary (sharh) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the ktab al-mahr from Al-Wiqayah. The ruling addresses the validity of mahr in marriages of non-Muslims (dhimmis or harbis) where the mahr is invalid in Islamic law (carrion, no mahr, wine, pig) but valid in their own religious law. The key principle is that we leave them and their customs (نحن أمرنا بتركهم وما يدينون). Therefore, the marriage contract is considered valid, and if they convert to Islam, the original mahr terms are enforced as far as possible, with adjustments for impurity or unlawfulness. For carrion or no mahr, no mahr is due even after conversion because their custom did not require it. For wine or pig specified as ayn (a specific item), she gets that item despite its impurity, but the Muslim husband must dispose of it properly (e.g., turn wine into vinegar or pour it away, set the pig free). If the wine or pig was not specified, she gets the value of the wine (since wine is fungible) and mahr al-mithl for the pig (since pigs are non-fungible). The reason is that taking the equivalent of a fungible is like taking the identical item, but since taking wine itself is haram, the value substitutes as a turning away from it. For a non-fungible, taking value is akin to taking the item. The glossary explains key terms: dhimmi is a pact-protected non-Muslim; harbi is from enemy territory. The commentary concludes with two objections and their answers, refining the application of the rule.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim who has accepted the jizya and lives under Muslim rule; includes the musta'min (temporary protection). In this passage, it refers to any non-Muslim under Muslim jurisdiction.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim from dar al-harb (enemy territory) with whom no peace treaty exists.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible goods that are sold by weight, measure, or count, and are replaceable with similar items.
قيمي (qimi): Non-fungible goods that vary in quality and cannot be replaced exactly with similar items.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi marries a dhimmi woman with a specific pig as mahr, then both become Muslim, what does the wife receive?
A) Nothing, because pig is impure.
B) Mahr al-mithl (the dower of her peers).
C) The specific pig, but the husband must dispose of it.
D) The value of the pig.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school upholds non-Muslim marriages with invalid dower by enforcing the original mahr with necessary adjustments to avoid haram consumption.

Ruling on Mahr Specified as Wine or Pig
Ruling and commentary on mahr with forbidden items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when mahr is specified as wine or pig, distinguish between specified and unspecified scenarios, and explain the legal reasoning based on the mithli/qimi distinction.

Core Idea
Wine as mahr gives value; pig as mahr gives proper mahr — because wine is fungible, pig is not.

In Hanafi fiqh, the mahr (dower) can be any commodity of value, even if it is forbidden for Muslims to possess. But the entitlement of the wife depends on whether the item is specified or not, and on the item's legal category: mithli (fungible) or qimi (non-fungible). This ruling from the commentary on al-Wiqaya clarifies these distinctions through analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage distinguishes between a specified (عين) mahr of wine/pig and an unspecified (غير عين) mahr. Determine which scenario you are analyzing.
2
Apply the mithli/qimi categorization
Wine is classified as mithli (fungible), like vinegar. Pig is classified as qimi (non-fungible), like a sheep. This classification determines the remedy when the mahr is unspecified.
Mithli items have exact substitutes; qimi items vary in value.
3
Trace the analogical reasoning (qiyas)
The ruling for unspecified wine is derived by analogy to vinegar: since wine is mithli, taking its value is like taking the item itself. For pig, analogized to sheep (qimi): taking its value does not avoid possession, so proper mahr (mahr al-mithl) is imposed to turn away from the pig.
The key difference: for mithli, value is a substitute; for qimi, value is not a substitute.
4
Address the objections
Two objections are raised: (1) Why allow the wife to take the actual forbidden item in the specified case? Answer: Because it is a specific item already designated; disposal is then the husband's duty. (2) Why in preemption (shuf'a) does a Muslim pay the value of a pig, but here we impose proper mahr? Answer: In preemption, value is for the house, not the pig; here, there is no necessity to use value.
These objections test the consistency of the reasoning.
5
Formulate the final ruling
For specified mahr: wife takes the specific wine/pig, then the husband must dispose of it. For unspecified mahr: wife takes value of wine, and proper mahr for pig.
The ruling is wajib (obligatory) as it determines the wife's right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب المهر: فلها ذلك، وفي غير عين فقيمة الخمر فيها، ومهر المثل في الخنزير؛ لأن الخمر عندهم مثلي كالخل عندنا، ولا يحل أخذها، فإيجاب القيمة يكون إعراضاً عنها، وأما الخنزير فمن ذوات القيم عندهم كالشاة عندنا، فإيجاب القيمة لا يكون إعراضاً عنه، فيجب مهر المثل إعراضاً عن الخنزير. [قوله: فلها ذلك؛ أي عين الخمر والخنزير المقدر عند النكاح بكماله، وإن طلقها قبل الدخول، فلها نصفه؛ لأن تسليم الخمر والخنزير وإن كان حراماً للمسلم لكن هاهنا جعل مهراً في حالة كان ذلك جائزاً له فيها، ويجب على المسلم أن يخلل الخمر أو يريقه ويرسل الخنزير]. [قوله: وفي غير عين؛ أي فيما إذا كان المهر الخمر أو الخنزير مطلقاً غير مشار إليه]. [قوله: وأما الخنزير... الخ؛ حاصله: أن أخذ المثل في المثلي، والقيمة في القيمي، بمنزلة أخذ عينه، وليس أخذ القيمة في المثلي كأخذ عينه، فلما كان الخمر مثلياً، ولم يمكن أخذ مثله للزوم أخذ عينه، فحكم بأخذ قيمته ليكون إعراضاً عنه، والخل والخنزير لما كان من ذوات القيم، فأخذ قيمته يكون كأخذه؛ فلذلك حكم فيه بوجوب مهر المثل. واعترض هنا بوجهين: أحدهما: إن الإعراض لما كان واجباً على المسلم، فلم حكم بأخذ الخمر والخنزير في صورة كون المهر الخمر والخنزير العين؟ وأجيب عنه: بأن هناك كان المهر عيناً مشاراً إليه، فلا سبيل إلى أخذ مثله أو قيمته، وهاهنا ليس بعين فيمكن إيجاب القيمة. وثانيهما: إنه لو شرى ذمي من ذمي داراً بخنزير وشفيعها مسلم كان له أخذها بقيمة الخنزير، فما وجه الفرق؟ وأجيب: بأن القيمة في مسألة الشفعة بدل عن الدار لا عن الخنزير، وإنما صير إليها للتقدير بها مع أن هناك ضرورة، وهي إبقاء حق الشفيع، ولا ضرورة هاهنا ليتيسر المصير إلى مهر المثل. [قوله: عن الخنزير؛ فيه بحث: فإن الإعراض عن الخنزير لا يوجب مهر المثل؛ لجواز أن يؤخذ فيه؟ وجوابه: إن الأخذ بعدما فرض كون النكاح مع المهر ضروري فلا سبيل إلى الإعراض عن الخنزير حينئذ إلا بإيجاب مهر المثل.]
Book of Marriage, Chapter of Mahr: So she has that (the specific wine or pig). And if it is not specific, then the value of wine is (given) in it, and the proper mahr is (given) for the pig. Because wine among them (the scholars) is fungible like vinegar among us, and taking it is not permissible, so imposing value constitutes turning away from it. As for the pig, it is among non-fungibles among them like a sheep among us, so imposing value does not constitute turning away from it, so the proper mahr is obligatory as a turning away from the pig. [He said: 'So she has that' meaning the specific wine or pig designated at marriage in its entirety, and if he divorces her before consummation, she has half of it; because delivering wine and pig, though forbidden for a Muslim, but here it was made a mahr in a situation where it was permissible for him, and it is obligatory for the Muslim to turn wine into vinegar or pour it out, and to set the pig free.] [He said: 'And if it is not specific' meaning when the mahr is wine or pig absolutely, not pointed to.] [He said: 'As for the pig...' its summary: Taking the fungible in the case of fungibles, and value in the case of non-fungibles, is like taking the specific item itself; but taking value in the case of fungibles is not like taking the specific item. So since wine is fungible and it is impossible to take its like due to the necessity of taking its specific item, it was ruled to take its value to turn away from it. As for the pig, since it is among non-fungibles, taking its value is like taking it; therefore it was ruled that the proper mahr is obligatory. Two objections are raised here: First: Since turning away was obligatory upon the Muslim, why was it ruled to take the wine and pig in the case where the mahr is specific wine or pig? Answer: Because there the mahr was a specific item pointed to, so there is no way to take its like or value, and here it is not specific so imposing value is possible. Second: If a dhimmi buys a house from another dhimmi with a pig, and its preemptor is a Muslim, he has the right to take it by paying the value of the pig. What is the difference? Answer: The value in the case of preemption is compensation for the house, not for the pig; it is resorted to for estimation, along with necessity, which is preserving the preemptor's right. Here there is no necessity to resort to proper mahr.] [He said: 'About the pig'; there is discussion: For turning away from the pig does not necessitate proper mahr because it is permissible to take it? Answer: Taking it after the marriage with the mahr is assumed necessary, so there is no way to turn away from the pig except by imposing proper mahr.]
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya deals with the ruling of mahr when it is specified as wine or pig. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios: if the mahr is a specific item (عين) of wine or pig, the wife gets that exact item, despite it being forbidden for a Muslim to possess. The Muslim husband must then dispose of it by turning wine into vinegar or pouring it out, and setting the pig free. If the mahr is not specified (i.e., mentioned in the contract without identification), then for wine the wife gets its market value, because wine is fungible (مثلي) like vinegar. Taking value is a form of turning away from it, which is obligatory. For pig, which is non-fungible (قيمي) like a sheep, taking its value would not constitute turning away; so instead the proper mahr (مهر المثل) is wajib. The commentator explains the logic: for fungibles, taking the equivalent (مثل) is like taking the item itself, but taking value is not. For non-fungibles, taking value is like taking the item. Two objections are raised: (1) If turning away from forbidden items is obligatory, why allow the wife to take them in the specified case? Answer: In the specified case, it is a specific item already owned, so the wife must take it and the husband then disposes. In the unspecified case, we can impose value to avoid outright possession. (2) Why in preemption (شفعة) does a Muslim preemptor pay value of the pig, but here we impose proper mahr? Answer: In preemption, the value is compensation for the house, not the pig; it is just a measure. There is no necessity here to resort to value of pig because proper mahr achieves the goal. The hashia adds that turning away from pig does not oblige proper mahr unless there is necessity, but since marriage is contracted with mahr, necessity dictates proper mahr. This passage is a clear example of Hanafi legal reasoning using the categories of مithli and qimi.

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): Wine, an intoxicating beverage, considered mithli (fungible) in this passage.
الخنزير (al-khinzir): Pig, considered qimi (non-fungible) in this passage.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): Proper mahr: the mahr that is customarily given to a woman of her status.
مثلي (mithli): Fungible: a commodity that has equivalents in the market and can be replaced by its like.
قيمي (qimi): Non-fungible: a commodity that has no standard equivalent and is valued by market price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When mahr is unspecified wine, why does the wife receive its value rather than the proper mahr?
A) Because wine is forbidden so value is a penalty
B) Because wine is mithli so value is like taking the item
C) Because the husband cannot give wine
D) Because proper mahr is only for animals

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling for mahr with forbidden items hinges on whether the item is mithli or qimi, and the wife's entitlement varies accordingly.

Ruling on I'rad 'an al-Khinzir and Mahr al-Mithl
Clarifying that turning away from a pig requires mahr al-mithl in a marriage contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school obligates mahr al-mithl when a husband turns away from a pig as mahr in a marriage contract.

Core Idea
Turning away from an impermissible mahr still requires compensation because marriage must have a valid mahr.

This passage addresses a specific scenario where a husband stipulates a pig as mahr in a marriage contract but then turns away from it. The commentator defends the rule that mahr al-mithl becomes wajib, countering an objection that the pig could simply be taken. The reasoning highlights a key Hanafi principle: a valid marriage contract cannot be without a proper mahr, so setting aside an invalid mahr necessitates an alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The husband turns away from the pig as mahr. What is the base ruling? (Mahr al-mithl becomes wajib.)
2
Understand the objection
Why might someone object? (Since the pig is not valid property, why not just take it? Turning away from it seems to have no effect.)
3
Analyze the commentator's response
How does he refute the objection? (He argues that the marriage is assumed to have a mahr; thus, the only way to 'turn away' from the pig is to provide a valid mahr-al-mithl. This is based on necessity.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون إعراضاً عنه، فيجب مهر المثل اعراضاً عن الخنزير [١] قوله : عن الخنزير ؛ فيه بحث : فإنّ الإعراض عن الخنزير لا يوجب مهر المثل ؛ لجواز أن يؤخذ فيه ؟ وجوابه: إن الأخذ بعدما فرض كون النكاح مع المهر ضروري فلا سبيل إلى الإعراض عن الخنزير حينئذ إلا بإيجاب مهر المثل.
It becomes turning away from it, so mahr al-mithl becomes obligatory, turning away from the pig. [1] His statement: 'from the pig'; there is discussion in it: for indeed turning away from the pig does not necessitate mahr al-mithl because it is permissible that it be taken? And its answer: Indeed taking, after it is assumed that the marriage is with a mahr, is necessary, so there is no way to turn away from the pig then except by imposing mahr al-mithl.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) addressing an apparent objection to a prior ruling. The base text states that if a husband turns away from a pig (a specific scenario in a marriage contract), he must pay mahr al-mithl. A questioner objects: turning away from a pig should not require mahr al-mithl because it is permissible to simply take the pig (i.e., it is not a valid form of payment). The commentator responds: once the marriage is contracted with the stipulation of a mahr, it becomes necessary to have a valid mahr; therefore, the only way to achieve the 'turning away' (which is interpreted as setting aside the pig as mahr) is to impose mahr al-mithl as compensation. In other words, the assumption that the marriage is valid with a mahr precludes the possibility of simply ignoring the pig without providing an alternative. This reasoning is based on necessity (zarurah) and the principle that a marriage contract cannot be without a proper mahr.

Key Terms
إعراض (i'rad): Turning away or setting aside something; in this context, the husband's act of rejecting the pig as mahr.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The mahr that is equivalent to what is given to women of similar status; used when the stipulated mahr is invalid or absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does i'rad 'an al-khinzir necessitate mahr al-mithl?
A) Because the pig is inherently worthless
B) Because the marriage contract cannot be without a valid mahr
C) Because turning away from a pig is a sin
D) Because the pig becomes impure

Key Takeaway
Turning away from an impermissible mahr in a valid marriage contract obligates the husband to pay mahr al-mithl, ensuring the wife receives proper compensation.

Marriage of Slaves without Master's Permission: Definitions and Ruling
Definition of slave categories and ruling on marriage without master's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of slaves (qinn, mukatab, mudabbar, umm walad) and state the ruling that their marriage without the master's permission is suspended (mawquf) in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without the master's permission is not invalid but suspended, pending the master's consent.

This lesson opens the chapter on the marriage of slaves and disbelievers. The text defines the key categories of slaves recognized in Hanafi law and establishes the fundamental ruling that any marriage contracted by a slave or a female slave without the master's permission is not automatically void but rather suspended (mawquf). This distinction is crucial because it determines the legal effect of the contract once permission is given or denied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave categories
List the five types of slaves mentioned: qinn, mukatab, mudabbar, ama (female slave), and umm walad. Note their definitions as given in the commentary.
The passage explains each term in detail; focus on the key characteristics that distinguish them.
2
Locate the ruling
Find the statement that the marriage of these slaves without the master's permission is 'mawquf' (suspended). This is the central ruling.
'Mawquf' is a technical term meaning the contract is formed but its effect is conditional.
3
Understand the meaning of mawquf
Analyze the commentary's clarification that 'mawquf' means the marriage is not effective immediately but becomes valid upon subsequent permission of the master.
This distinguishes the Hanafi position from other schools that may consider such marriages batil (void).
4
Note the role of the master (sayyid)
Identify the definition of 'sayyid' (master) and understand that the slave lacks the capacity to marry without his owner's consent.
The commentary cites al-Durar sharh al-Ghurar emphasizing that a slave cannot marry except from one who can free him.

Chapter of marriage of the slave and the disbeliever. Marriage of the qinn, the mukatab, the mudabbar, the female slave, and umm walad without the permission of the master is suspended. [1] Chapter of marriage of the slave and the disbeliever. (Marriage of the qinn, the mukatab, the mudabbar, the female slave, and umm walad without the permission of the master is suspended.) (4) 1 His statement: Chapter of marriage... etc. When he finished from discussing marriage of those who have capacity for marriage and what pertains to it, he began discussing marriage of those who have no capacity, and included in this chapter issues of marriages of the disbeliever; due to the existence of proportionality; because slavery originally is a consequence of disbelief, and he generalized the disbeliever so it includes the polytheist, Jew, Christian, and others, and likewise the slave is absolute, to include all its types: 1. al-qinn: with kasr of qaf and tashdid of nun: he who is wholly owned. 2. al-mukatab: he to whom his master said: I have made a kitaba with you for such-and-such, and his ruling is that he becomes free after paying that amount. 3. al-mudabbar: he to whom his master said: you are free if I die. 4. umm walad: she is the female slave whom her master has intercourse with and she gives birth to a child from him, and the master claims the child, and her ruling is freedom after the death of the master. [2] His statement: marriage of al-qinn: it is linguistically: wholly owned, so it includes the mukatab and others, and its opposite is partially owned, and both are covered by: the slave and the owned, and what is intended by it in the custom of jurists is pure slavery; therefore they use it in contrast to the mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab; therefore the author needed to mention these after mentioning al-qinn. [3] His statement: al-sayyid: it is with fat-h of sin and tashdid of the ya under it: the master owner, and it is mentioned in 'al-Durar sharh al-Ghurar' and others: the slave does not have the right to marry except from one who has the power to free him. As for the female slave, the guardianship of her marriage is established for other than her master and owner as well, like the father of an orphan, he may marry his female slave, likewise his grandfather and his executor, and the mukatab is allowed to marry his female slave. [4] His statement: suspended; this is what is meant by one who said: their marriage is not permissible without the permission of the master, not... (End of excerpt from Durar al-Hukkam 1:352).
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter in the fiqh work on the marriage of slaves and disbelievers. The author first presents the ruling from the matn: the marriage of a qinn (full slave), mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), mudabbar (slave whose manumission is contingent upon the master's death), female slave (ama), and umm walad (slave mother of her master's child) is suspended (mawquf) if conducted without the master's permission. The commentary then explains each term: 'qinn' is a full slave, 'mukatab' is one who has a kitaba agreement to earn his freedom, 'mudabbar' is a slave who will be freed after the master's death, 'umm walad' is a female slave who bears her master's child and will be freed at his death. The commentary notes that 'qinn' is often used broadly for any slave but here is restricted to those not yet partially free, hence the need to mention the other categories separately. The term 'sayyid' (master) is also defined. The commentary cites al-Durar sharh al-Ghurar stating that a slave does not have the right to marry except from one who can free him, implying that a slave cannot marry without his master's permission. For a female slave, the guardianship of marriage can belong to others besides her master, such as the father of an orphan. The chapter aims to cover all slaves (including disbelievers) because slavery originates from disbelief, and the rulings for marriages involving disbelievers are included due to similarity.

Key Terms
قن (qinn): A full slave owned completely.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who will be freed upon the master's death.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who gives birth to her master's child and the master acknowledges paternity.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract that is not immediately effective but can become valid upon fulfillment of a condition (here, master's permission).
سيد (sayyid): Master and owner of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this chapter, what is the status of a slave's marriage conducted without the master's permission?
A) Valid (sahih) immediately
B) Invalid (batil) forever
C) Suspended (mawquf) pending permission
D) Revocable by the slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the marriage of a slave without the master's permission is suspended (mawquf), not void, allowing the possibility of subsequent ratification.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Marriage of Slaves Without Master's Permission: Suspended Ruling
Commentary on the ruling of slave marriages without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage of slaves without master's permission, explain why it is suspended (mawquf) rather than invalid, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without permission is suspended — it can be ratified by the master, not void from the start.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling of marriage contracted by slaves without their master's permission. The Hanafi school holds that such a marriage is 'mawquf' (suspended) — it is neither completely valid nor completely invalid, but awaits the master's decision. This intermediate status is based on a hadith that labels such a marriage as fornication, yet allows for ratification, reflecting the slave's limited capacity. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling, the evidence, and how the Hanafi position differs from that of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its category
The matn says marriage of slaves without permission is 'mawquf'. Understand that mawquf is an intermediate category between valid and void.
Mawquf means suspended; it becomes effective upon ratification.
2
Define each type of slave
The passage defines qinn (fully owned), mukatab (contract for freedom), mudabbar (free upon master's death), amah (female slave), and umm walad (mother of master's child). Note that all are covered by the ruling.
Later categories have partial freedom, but still require permission.
3
Identify the dalil
The hadith: 'Any slave who marries without permission of his masters is a fornicator.' Locate it in the text and note its authenticity grading.
The hadith is narrated by al-Tirmidhi, al-Hakim, and others.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi school derives suspension from the word 'عاهر' (fornicator), which implies prohibition but not automatic nullity. The contract is valid in essence but suspended due to lack of capacity.
Other schools might interpret the same word differently.
5
Contrast the Shafi'i position
Shafi'is hold the marriage is batil (void) outright because fornication cannot be a valid marriage. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi, master can ratify; in Shafi'i, ratification is impossible.
This difference affects the financial consequences and the ability to salvage the marriage.

Chapter on the marriage of slaves and disbelievers. Marriage of the qinn (full slave), the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), the mudabbar (slave whose manumission is deferred until master's death), the female slave (amah), and the umm walad (mother of her master's child) without the master's permission is suspended. [1] Chapter on marriage of slaves and disbelievers. (Marriage of the qinn, the mukatab, the mudabbar, the amah, and the umm walad without the master's permission is suspended (4). 1 His saying: 'Chapter on marriage...' etc.; when he finished discussing marriage of those who have the capacity for marriage and what pertains to it, he began discussing marriage of those who lack capacity, and included in this chapter issues of the marriages of disbelievers due to the suitability; because slavery is originally a consequence of disbelief, and he generalised 'disbeliever' to include the polytheist, Jew, Christian and others, and likewise 'slave' absolutely to include all its categories: 1. al-qinn: with kasrah on the qaf and shaddah on the noon: he who is fully owned. And al-mukatab: he to whom his master said: 'I have contracted with you for such amount,' and his ruling is that he becomes free after paying that amount. 3. al-mudabbar: he to whom his master said: 'You are free when I die.' 4. umm al-walad: the female slave whom her master had intercourse with and she bore him a child which the master claimed, and her ruling is that she becomes free after the master's death. [2] His saying: 'Marriage of the qinn': linguistically it means the fully owned slave, so it includes the mukatab and others, and its opposite is the partially owned slave, and both are encompassed by the terms 'slave' and 'bondsman', and what is intended by it in the terminology of the jurists is pure servitude; hence they use it in contrast to the mudabbar, umm walad, and mukatab; hence the author needed to mention these after mentioning the qinn. [3] His saying: 'the master' (al-sayyid): he is - with fatha on the seen and shaddah on the underdotted ya' - the owner master. It is mentioned in 'al-Durar Sharh al-Ghurar' and others: 'The male slave does not have the right to give himself in marriage except to the one who has the right to manumit him. As for the female slave, the guardianship of her marriage is established for someone other than her master and owner as well, like the father of an orphan having the right to give his female slave in marriage, and likewise his grandfather, his executor, and the mukatab is permitted to give his female slave in marriage.' [4] His saying: 'suspended' (mawquf); this is what is meant by the one who said: 'Their marriage is not permitted without the master's permission.' If he permits it, it becomes effective; if he rejects, it becomes void. If they marry with permission, the dower is upon them, and the sale of the qinn is for it (the dower), not the other two, i.e., the mukatab and mudabbar, but they work to pay it. And his saying: 'He divorced her with a revocable divorce, so it is permission' (i.e., it indicates permission). If he permits, it is effective; if he rejects, it is void. If they marry with permission, the dower is upon them, and the sale of the qinn is for it (1), not the other two (i.e., the mukatab and mudabbar), but they work to pay it. And his saying: 'He divorced her with a revocable divorce, so it is permission' indicates the nullity. And the basis for it is the hadith: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his masters is a fornicator,' i.e., an adulterer; narrated by al-Tirmidhi who declared it fair, and by al-Hakim who declared it authentic, and similarly narrated by Ibn Majah. [1] His saying: 'it becomes void'; if both permission and rejection occur before consummation, then the matter is apparent; if after consummation, then in case of rejection the slave is demanded of the mahr al-mithl after his manumission. [2] His saying: 'if they marry'; i.e., those mentioned. [3] His saying: 'the dower'; i.e., the dower and likewise the maintenance are obligatory on these marrying persons, not on their master, due to the semblance of obligation from them, which is marriage. And they (dower and maintenance) are dropped by their death, unless the slave is permitted (to trade) and leaves earnings, then he pays the dower from his earnings. So in 'al-Bahr'. [4] His saying: 'and sold' (yuba') in the passive voice; i.e., his master sells him for the debt of the dower, because it is a debt attached to his neck, and it became apparent with respect to the master by his permission, so he is ordered to sell him. If he refuses, the judge sells him in his presence, unless the master agrees to pay the amount of his price. So in 'al-Muhit'. [0] His saying: 'not the other two'; i.e., the mukatab and mudabbar are not sold for the debt of their dowers, because they do not allow transfer from ownership to ownership as long as they are in the status of tadbir and kitabah. Yes, if the mukatab becomes unable (to pay), then the dower becomes a debt on his neck, so he is sold for it. (6) His saying: 'and his saying...', etc.; the purpose of mentioning this issue is to indicate that permission... (1) That is, the dower is upon the qinn and others, and it is a debt on the neck of the qinn only, so he is sold for it, because it is a debt that became obligatory on his neck by his action, and it became apparent with respect to the master as it occurred with his permission, so it attaches to his neck to prevent harm to the woman, like the debts of the authorized slave for trade. See: 'al-Ikhtiyar' (2:144) and others. (2) In 'Sunan al-Tirmidhi' (3:419), 'Sunan Abi Dawud' (1:633), 'Mushkil al-Athar' (6:207), 'al-Sunan al-Sughra' (5:282), 'Ma'rifat al-Sunan' (11:274), and others. (3) 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (3:210). (4) See: 'Durar al-Hukkam' (1:349).
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of marriage of slaves without their master's permission. The matn states that the marriage of a qinn (full slave), mukatab (slave under contract), mudabbar (slave with deferred manumission), amah (female slave), and umm walad (mother of master's child) without the master's permission is 'mawquf' (suspended). The author explains that 'mawquf' means the marriage is not effective until the master permits it; if he permits, it is effective; if he rejects, it is void. The commentary then defines each slave category: qinn is fully owned, mukatab is a slave who will be free upon paying a sum, mudabbar is free upon master's death, umm walad is freed upon master's death after bearing his child. The master (sayyid) is the owner. The proof is the hadith: 'Any slave who marries without permission of his masters is a fornicator,' which indicates the need for permission; without it the marriage is irregular. If the master permits, the dower (mahr) is on the slave, not the master, and the qinn can be sold to pay the dower debt, but the mukatab and mudabbar cannot be sold as they are not fully owned; instead they work to pay. If the master rejects after consummation, the slave must pay mahr al-mithl after his freedom. The commentary also notes that if the master divorces the wife revocably, that implies permission. This passage is from the Hanafi school; the ruling is based on the hadith and the principle that the slave lacks full capacity. The mahr debt attaches to the qinn's neck, but not to mukatab/mudabbar due to their partial freedom.

Key Terms
القِنّ (al-qinn): A slave who is fully owned (kamil al-riqq), as opposed to a partially owned slave.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master to pay a sum and become free.
المدبر (al-mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared 'you are free when I die' (tadbir).
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who bore her master's child and the master claimed paternity; she becomes free upon his death.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; the marriage contract is valid in essence but its legal effects are held pending the master's permission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a slave marries without the master's permission, the master later refuses after consummation, but then the slave is freed?
A) The marriage is valid because the slave was freed.
B) The marriage is void and the slave owes mahr al-mithl.
C) The marriage is void and no financial obligations exist.
D) The marriage is suspended and becomes valid upon freedom.

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage without master's permission is suspended (mawquf) and can become valid through ratification, reflecting the Hanafi principle of balancing capacity and contract validity.

6 min
Slave Marriage Without Permission: Suspension, Validation, and Mahr Liability
Ruling on slave marriage without permission and sale of slave for mahr debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's marriage without master permission and explain the legal consequences for different types of slaves (qinn, mukatab, mudabbar) regarding payment of mahr.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without permission is suspended – valid only if the master permits; invalid if rejected.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a key fiqh issue: the marriage of a slave without his master's permission. The Hanafi school treats such a marriage as suspended (mawquf), not initially valid or invalid. The master's subsequent permission (ijazah) makes it valid; rejection (radd) voids it. The passage also clarifies who bears the mahr debt and how it can be collected, distinguishing between a full slave and those with partial freedom. The proof is a hadith that labels such a marriage as fornication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave marrying without his master's permission. The ruling: if the master gives permission afterward, the marriage is valid (nafadh); if the master rejects, it is void (batal). The marriage is suspended (mawquf) until the master decides.
This is a case of a contract that is neither valid nor invalid immediately, but pending confirmation.
2
Determine the mahr liability and method of payment
If the marriage is with permission (or subsequently permitted), the mahr is a debt on the slave, not the master. For a full slave (qinn), the master may sell the slave to satisfy the mahr. For a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) or mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred after master's death), they cannot be sold; rather, they must work to earn the mahr.
The distinction rests on the slave's status: qinn is a marketable commodity; mukatab and mudabbar have a right to eventual freedom and cannot be sold.
3
Locate the dalil and examine its implication
The proof is a hadith: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his masters is a fornicator.' (Tirmidhi, Hakim, Ibn Majah). The word 'fornicator' (عاهر) indicates the marriage is legally invalid without permission, but the Hanafi school interprets it as implying suspension rather than immediate nullity.
The hadith is ahad (single-narrator) and thus zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that such a marriage is invalid ab initio (batil) regardless of subsequent permission. The Hanafi view allows the master's permission to retroactively validate it, based on the principle that the hadith's threat of fornication is understood to apply only if permission is never given.
This difference affects whether the marriage can be ratified after the fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن أجاز له نفذ، وإن رَدَّ بطل ، فإن نكحوا بالإذن، فالمهر عليهم، وبيع القن فيه لا الآخران : أي المكاتب والمدبر ، بل يسعيان . والأصل فيه حديث: «أيما عبد تزوج بغير إذن مواليه، فهو عاهر»: أي زان، أخرجه الترمذي وحسنه ، والحاكم وصححه، ومثله أخرجه ابن ماجة.
If he gives permission, it is effective; if he rejects, it is void. If they marry with permission, then the dower is upon them. And the [full] slave is sold for it, not the other two—i.e., the mukatab and the mudabbar—rather they are made to earn. The basis for this is the hadith: 'Any slave who marries without the permission of his masters is a fornicator'—i.e., an adulterer. Recorded by al-Tirmidhi who deemed it good, and al-Hakim who authenticated it, and a similar narration by Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on a slave's marriage without the master's permission. The ruling is that the marriage is suspended: if the master subsequently permits it, it becomes valid (nafadh); if he rejects it, it is void (batal). If the slave marries with the master's permission, the dower (mahr) is a debt on the slave, not on the master. To satisfy this debt, the master may sell the full slave (qinn) but not a mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) or mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred), because those cannot be sold while their contracts are in effect. Instead, they are made to earn to pay the mahr. The proof is a hadith (marfu') that calls such a marriage fornication, implying it is invalid without permission, and the hadith is considered hasan or sahih by some scholars.

Key Terms
قن (qinn): A full-owned slave (who can be sold).
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared him free after the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a slave who marries without his master's permission, and then the master rejects the marriage?
A) The marriage becomes valid (sahih).
B) The marriage is void (batal) and the slave owes nothing.
C) The marriage is void (batal) and the slave must pay mahr al-mithl after his freedom if consummated.
D) The marriage is suspended until the master changes his mind.

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage without master permission is a suspended contract (mawquf), validated by permission or voided by rejection, with mahr liability differing by slave type.

Master's statement 'divorce her revocably' as authorization
Ruling on implied authorization through divorce command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a command for revocable divorce and a general command to divorce, and apply the principle that the former constitutes implicit authorization (ijazah) of the marriage.

Core Idea
A master's command to divorce revocably implies a valid marriage and thus serves as authorization; unconditional commands do not.

In Hanafi law, when a slave marries without the master's permission, the master's subsequent words or actions can ratify the marriage. The passage you will study clarifies that specifically a command for revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī) is treated as authorization, while a mere 'divorce her' or 'separate' is not. This distinction arises because revocable divorce logically presupposes a valid marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the source: the master says 'divorce her revocably' vs 'divorce her' or 'separate'. These are distinct legal statements.
Focus on the specific wording: rajʿiyya vs. unqualified.
2
Understand why revocable divorce implies authorization
Revocable divorce can only occur after a valid marriage. So the master's command assumes the marriage exists, thereby ratifying it.
This is the key talil (reasoning).
3
Extend the principle
The passage also states that permission for marriage can be explicit or implicit. Any action indicating consent, like sending the mahr, also counts as authorization.
Recognize the broader principle of implicit permission (dalālah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : طلقها رجعية فهو إجازة لا طلقها ، أو فارقها . وإذنُه لعبده بالنكاح يعم جائزه وفاسده كما تكون صريحاً كقوله : رضيت وأذنت كذلك قد تكون دلالة كأمره بالطلاق الرجعي أو بفعل يدلُّ عليها كسوق المهر إلى المرأة.
And his saying: 'Divorce her revocably' is authorization, not 'divorce her' or 'separate from her.' And his permission to his slave for marriage covers both its valid and invalid forms. Just as it may be explicit like his saying: 'I am pleased and I permit,' similarly it may be indicative, like his command of revocable divorce or an action that indicates it, such as sending the dower to the woman.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage explains that when a slave marries without permission, if the master says 'Divorce her revocably' (ṭallaqhā rajʿiyya), this statement is considered an authorization (ijāzah) of the marriage, because revocable divorce presupposes a valid marriage. In contrast, simply saying 'Divorce her' (without qualification) or 'Separate from her' (fāriqhā) is not considered authorization; it is more likely a mere command to separate, especially if the slave is rebellious. The passage also introduces a principle: a master's general permission to his slave for marriage covers both valid and invalid types of marriage. Such permission can be explicit (like 'I permit') or implicit (dalālah), such as commanding a revocable divorce or sending the mahr to the woman.

Key Terms
إجازة (ijāzah): Subsequent authorization that validates a suspended contract.
طلاق رجعي (ṭalāq rajʿī): Revocable divorce where husband may take wife back during the waiting period without a new contract.
دلالة (dalālah): Inductive indication; implicit evidence of intent.
صريح (ṣarīḥ): Explicit, clear statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A slave marries without permission; the master says: 'Divorce her revocably.' What is the legal effect?
A) The marriage is void (batal) and the slave must divorce.
B) The command is not clear; the slave should ask.
C) This statement serves as authorization (ijazah) of the marriage.
D) The marriage becomes valid only if the master also pays the mahr.

Key Takeaway
The master's command for revocable divorce is authorization because revocable divorce presupposes a valid marriage; unconditional commands are mere orders to separate.

Scope of Master's Permission: Does It Cover Fasid Marriage?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of master's permission for slave marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a master's absolute permission for his slave to marry includes fasid marriage, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Abu Hanifah and the two companions.

Core Idea
Absolute permission covers both valid and fasid marriage; the two companions restrict it to valid.

When a master gives his slave unconditional permission to marry, does that permission cover only a valid marriage or also a marriage that is technically defective (fasid)? Imam Abu Hanifah and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagreed on this point, with practical implications for how the mahr debt is enforced. This lesson unpacks that dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their positions
First, identify that the dispute is between Imam Abu Hanifah and his two main disciples (Sahibayn). State each position concisely.
2
State Abu Hanifah's position
Abu Hanifah holds that absolute permission ('amm) includes both sahih and fasid marriage. The mahr becomes a debt on the slave's neck, and he can be sold to pay it.
The key point: permission is general unless restricted.
3
State the companions' position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that even absolute permission does not include fasid marriage. The mahr is still owed, but it is only collectable after emancipation; the slave cannot be sold.
They argue that fasid is not truly 'marriage' in the intended sense.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is whether the slave can be sold immediately to pay the mahr (Abu Hanifah) or must wait until freedom (companions).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Although the text does not state an explicit tarjih, the context suggests Abu Hanifah's view is the one being explained and relied upon. Check later sources for mufta bihi.
Later Hanafi fatwa works may treat the Sahibayn's view as stronger; this lesson focuses on the raw dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعم يعني إذا أذن المولى لعبده بالنكاح ولم يقيده بالصحيح والفاسد يشمل ذلك الإذن كليهما حتى لو نكح العبد بعد إذن مولاه نكاحاً فاسداً بيع في دين المهر لتعلقه برقبته بإذن مولاه، ولا كذلك لو قيد الإذن بالصحيح. وفيه خلاف لهما، فإن الإذن عندهما وإن كان مطلقاً لا يتناول الفاسد، فلا يباع في دين مهره فيه، بل يطالب به بعد العتق.
It is general: meaning when the master gives permission to his slave for marriage and does not restrict it to valid or invalid, that permission includes both, such that even if the slave after his master's permission marries an invalid marriage, he is sold for the debt of the dower because it is attached to his neck by the master's permission. Not so if he restricts the permission to valid. And there is a dispute with them (the two companions): for permission according to them, even if absolute, does not include the invalid, so he is not sold for the debt of its dower, rather he is demanded for it after emancipation.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of a master's unconditional permission for his slave to marry. According to Imam Abu Hanifah (and his is the relied-upon position here), if the master says 'I permit you to marry' without specifying 'valid' or 'invalid', the permission covers both valid and invalid marriages. Consequently, if the slave contracts a fasid (invalid) marriage after such permission, the slave can be sold to pay the mahr debt because the debt attaches to his neck (raqabah) by virtue of the master's permission. However, if the master specified 'valid marriage', the permission is restricted. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree: in their view, even an absolute permission does not include fasid marriage. Therefore, if the slave contracts a fasid marriage, he cannot be sold for the mahr; rather, he is pursued for it after he gains freedom (i.e., his personal liability). The text does not give a tarjih here, but the context (previous lines) suggests the Hanafi position is as stated.

Key Terms
نكاح فاسد (nikāḥ fāsid): A marriage that lacks a condition for validity (e.g., no witnesses) but is not fundamentally prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifah, if a master gives absolute permission for marriage and the slave contracts a fasid marriage, what is the ruling regarding the mahr?
A) The marriage is void and no mahr is owed.
B) The mahr is a debt on the slave's neck, and he can be sold to pay it.
C) The slave must pay the mahr after emancipation, but cannot be sold.
D) The master must pay the mahr because he gave permission.

Key Takeaway
Absolute permission from a master for his slave to marry includes both valid and invalid marriage according to Abu Hanifah, leading to the slave being sold for the mahr debt; the two companions restrict it to valid marriage only, delaying enforcement until freedom.

Absolute Permission for Slave Marriage: Does It Cover Invalid Contracts?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of master's permission for slave marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position that an absolute permission covers both valid and invalid marriages, and explain the Sahibayn's disagreement and its practical consequence.

Core Idea
An absolute permission for a slave's marriage includes invalid contracts, but the Sahibayn disagree.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a key intra-Hanafi dispute: whether a master's absolute permission for his slave to marry also covers an invalid (fasid) marriage. Abu Hanifa says yes, the permission is general. The two companions (Sahibayn) argue that permission implies validity, so it does not extend to fasid. Understanding this difference affects who is liable for the dower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The base text states that the master's permission for his slave to marry includes both valid and invalid types. This is the starting point.
2
Understand the scope of absolute permission
According to Abu Hanifa, if the master does not restrict the permission, it applies to any marriage contract that can be called nikah, even if it lacks some condition of validity.
The phrase 'yam' (includes) is key.
3
Recognize the Sahibayn's objection
The two companions hold that permission for marriage is inherently for a valid marriage; a fasid contract is not really a marriage, so it is not covered.
4
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
For Abu Hanifa, if the slave contracts a fasid marriage and consummates, the dower becomes a debt attached to his person, and he can be sold to pay it. For the Sahibayn, the dower is not due until after emancipation; the slave cannot be sold for it.
5
Note the authority weight
The dispute is within Zahir al-Riwayah; both positions are transmitted.
The mufta bihi is not explicit in this passage, but later sources weigh in.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وإذنُهُ لعبده بالنكاح يعم جائزه وفاسده. قوله: يعم؛ يعني إذا أذن المولى لعبده بالنكاح، ولم يقيده بالصحيح والفاسد، وهو الذي فقد شرطاً من شروط الصحة يشمل ذلك الإذن كليهما حتى لو نكح العبد بعد إذن مولاه نكاحاً فاسداً بيع في دين المهر لتعلقه برقبته بإذن مولاه، ولا كذلك لو قيد الإذن بالصحيح. وفيه خلاف لهما، فإن الإذن عندهما وإن كان مطلقاً لا يتناول الفاسد، فلا يباع في دين مهره فيه، بل يطالب به بعد العتق. قوله: وفاسده؛ هذا إذا لم ينو المولى الصحيح، فإن نواه تقيد به كما لو نص على الصحيح، فإنه يتقيد به اتفاقاً، ولو نص على الفاسد صح الفاسد، وهل يصح الصحيح في هذه الصورة أم لا، اختلف فيه: فرجح في «النهر» صحته اتفاقاً، ورجح في «البحر» عدم صحته اتفاقاً، وهو الأصح.
His statement: 'And his permission to his slave for marriage includes both its valid and invalid [types].' His statement: 'includes'; meaning when the master permits his slave to marry, and does not restrict it to valid or invalid, and it is that which lacks a condition of validity, that permission includes both, such that if the slave after his master's permission marries an invalid marriage, he is sold for the debt of the dower because it is attached to his person by his master's permission. Not so if he restricted the permission to valid. And there is a disagreement with them (the two companions), for permission according to them, even if absolute, does not include the invalid, so he is not sold for the debt of the dower therein, but is pursued after emancipation. His statement: 'and its invalid'; this is when the master did not intend the valid, for if he intended it, it is restricted as if he specified the valid, then it is restricted by agreement. And if he specified the invalid, the invalid is valid. And whether the valid is valid in this case or not, there is disagreement: in 'al-Nahr' it is preferred that it is valid by agreement, and in 'al-Bahr' it is preferred that it is not valid by agreement, and that is the most correct.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of a master's permission for his slave's marriage. The commentator explains that according to Abu Hanifa, an absolute permission covers both valid and invalid (fasid) marriages. Therefore, if the slave contracts a fasid marriage, the master is liable for the dower because the permission attached the dower to the slave's person. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that an absolute permission does not cover a fasid marriage, so the slave is not sold for the dower; rather, the dower becomes a debt payable after emancipation. The passage also discusses cases where the master intends a specific type: if he intends valid, it is restricted; if he intends invalid, only the invalid is valid, and there is disagreement whether the valid could also be valid in that scenario. The preferred view is that the valid is not valid.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission given by a master to his slave to perform an action, here specifically to marry.
فاسد (fasid): A marriage contract that lacks a condition of validity (e.g., witnesses) but is not batil (void). It can become valid through consummation or other factors.
جائز (ja'iz): Valid and permissible; here it means a valid marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave is sold to pay the dower.
B) The dower is not due.
C) The slave is emancipated first, then pays.
D) The master pays directly.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a master's permission determines liability for dower in a slave's fasid marriage; Abu Hanifa covers it, Sahibayn do not.

When the Master Specifies: Effect of Intention on Permission for Slave Marriage
Nuances of restricted permission for slave's marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings when a master restricts his permission to valid or specifies invalid, and identify the disagreement on whether a valid marriage is also valid when invalid is specified.

Core Idea
If the master intends valid, only valid is permitted; if he specifies invalid, only invalid is valid; disagreement on whether valid is also valid.

This passage continues the discussion on the master's permission for his slave's marriage by examining cases where the master specifies his intent. If the master intends a valid marriage, the permission is restricted to valid by agreement. If he specifies an invalid marriage, there is a later dispute: is a valid marriage also covered? This lesson unpacks these nuances and the preferred view.

Identify the base cases
The text discusses: (1) master intends valid, (2) master specifies invalid.

Understand the agreed ruling

'Ittifaqan' indicates consensus.
3
Analyze the disputed case
If master specifies invalid, the invalid marriage is valid. The question: is a valid marriage also valid under such a permission?
4
Trace the tarjih
Al-Nahr says valid is also valid by agreement; al-Bahr says valid is not valid, and that is the most correct.
The preferred view is al-Bahr's.

8 min
Subsequent Valid Marriage After Void Marriage Requires New Permission
Rules on slave's valid marriage after a fasid marriage requires new permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether a valid marriage contracted after a fasid marriage is valid under the original permission or requires a new permission, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: any marriage, even fasid, exhausts the master's absolute permission.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement about the scope of a master's permission for a slave to marry. The central question: if a slave enters a fasid (invalid but not void) marriage under the master's general permission, and later contracts a valid marriage (with the same or another woman), is the valid marriage automatically valid under the original permission, or is it suspended pending new permission? The passage presents Abu Hanifa's view that the fasid marriage consumes the permission, and the Sahibayn's view that it does not. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your grasp of how absolute permissions interact with contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: if a slave, after a fasid marriage under his master's general permission, marries the same or another woman validly, the new marriage is suspended (mawquf) until the master gives a new permission. The previous permission was exhausted by the fasid marriage.
The permission is considered absolute, and its occurrence (even invalid) terminates it.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the valid marriage is valid without new permission, because the original permission did not encompass the fasid marriage and thus was not consumed by it.
For them, the fasid marriage is outside the scope of the permission, so the permission remains active.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn argue that a permission is exhausted only by the act for which it was given. Since the fasid marriage was not a valid exercise of the permission, it cannot consume it. Therefore, the original permission still covers a subsequent valid marriage.
They see the permission as specific to permissible marriages.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa counters that the permission is absolute and includes any marriage contract, valid or invalid. The absolute nature means that once any instance of the permitted act occurs, the permission is spent. The fasid marriage is still a marriage, so it exhausts the permission.
This is akin to an absolute command being fulfilled by a single instance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: According to Abu Hanifa, the valid marriage is suspended until a new permission is given. According to the Sahibayn, it is valid immediately without additional approval. This difference affects whether the slave's marriage is binding or pending.
Both positions have implications for the status of offspring and other rights.

Source Text

[0] قوله: انتهت ... الخ؛ وذلك لأن الإجازة السابقة مطلقة، والمطلق ينتهي بوجود فرد منه، فإن نوى به مرة بعد أخرى لم يصح؛ وذلك لأن الأمر يتضمَّنُ المصدر، وهو المفرد الحقيقي، أو الاعتباري: أي جملة ما يملكه دون العدد المحض، كما قالوا في: طلق امرأتي ونوى الواحدة، أو الثلاث يصح دون اثنتين، وتفصيله في كتب الأصول.
'And if he marries her a second time or another [woman] after her validly, it is suspended on permission.' That is, if he marries her a second time validly, or marries another woman after that woman validly, it is suspended on permission, because the permission was exhausted by that marriage in the invalid. In reality. [3] His statement: 'And if he marries her'; this too is a consequence of the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. According to him: if the slave marries that woman whom he first married invalidly a second time in a valid marriage, this marriage is suspended on permission, because his previous permission, being absolute, was terminated by the invalid [marriage], and did not include the other, so a new permission is necessary. Similarly, if he marries another woman in a valid marriage after the first [invalid] marriage, it is suspended on permission according to him. According to them, since the absolute permission did not include the invalid marriage, the permission is not terminated by it; because permission is only terminated by occurrence of what was permitted, not by occurrence of what was not permitted. So the invalid does not enter into permission, and the valid is valid by that permission. [4] His statement: 'validly'; he restricted it because if he marries the second invalidly, it is suspended on permission by agreement. [0] His statement: 'exhausted' etc.; that is because the previous permission is absolute, and the absolute is terminated by the existence of one individual of it. If he intended by it one after another, it is not valid; because the command implies the infinitive, which is a single real or considered unit: that is, the totality of what he possesses, not the pure number, as they said in: 'He divorced my wife and intended one [divorce] or three, it is valid, but not two.' And its detail is in the books of usul.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage continues the intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of the master's permission. The commentator explains that if a slave, after contracting a fasid marriage with a woman, later contracts a valid marriage with the same woman or another, the valid marriage is suspended on the master's new permission according to Abu Hanifa. This is because the previous permission, being absolute, was exhausted by the first marriage (even though it was fasid). The master's permission is considered consumed when any marriage occurs, including a fasid one. However, the two companions (Sahibayn) hold that the absolute permission did not cover the fasid marriage, so it was not consumed, and the valid marriage is valid without new permission. The passage also notes that if the second marriage is also fasid, it is suspended by agreement. The commentator draws an analogy to the principle that an absolute command is fulfilled by a single instance; if the master intended multiple instances, it would not be valid unless expressed.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a marriage that is not immediately effective but depends on the master's subsequent approval.
إجازة (ijazah): Permission or approval; here it refers to the master's ratification of the marriage.
انتهى (intahā): To be exhausted or terminated; the permission is considered spent when a marriage occurs under it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if a slave, after a fasid marriage under his master's permission, marries the same woman validly?
A) The valid marriage is automatically valid under the original permission.
B) The valid marriage is suspended until the master gives a new permission.
C) The valid marriage is invalid because the prior fasid marriage dissolved the contract.
D) The master must grant permission explicitly for any subsequent marriage.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's doctrine that an absolute permission is consumed by any marriage, even fasid, leads to the requirement of a new permission for a subsequent valid marriage; the Sahibayn's contrary view stems from restricting the permission to valid marriages only.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate

Slave's Second Marriage After Fasid Nikah: Dispute on Permission
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slave's marriage permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a slave's general permission to marry is exhausted by a fasid marriage, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A fasid marriage exhausts a general permission according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the Sahibayn.

When a slave is given general permission to marry and then contracts a fasid (irregular) marriage, the ruling on a subsequent valid marriage depends on whether the original permission is considered spent. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the absolute permission covers any marriage, even fasid, so the second marriage requires new permission. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the permission was for a valid marriage only, so the fasid marriage does not exhaust it. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The passage begins by stating that a slave who marries a woman in a fasid marriage with permission and then has intercourse is sold to pay the mahr. Note that if no intercourse occurs, no mahr is due.
This sets the context: the slave's actions have financial consequences.
2
Understand the core dispute
The main issue is whether the slave's subsequent valid marriage (to the same woman or another) is automatically valid under the original permission or requires new permission. Abu Hanifa says it is suspended; the Sahibayn say it is valid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both positions are within the school.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the master's permission was absolute (mutlaq). In usul, an absolute command is fulfilled by any instance of the act. Since the fasid marriage is a form of marriage, it exhausts the permission.
He relies on the legal maxim: 'al-amr al-mutlaq yanhadim bi wujud fard' (an absolute command is fulfilled by the existence of one instance).
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The Sahibayn counter that the fasid marriage is not the intended object of permission; the permission was for a valid contract. Therefore, the fasid marriage does not exhaust it, and the subsequent valid marriage still falls under the original permission.
They consider the fasid marriage as not falling under the permission at all.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf and exceptions
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the second marriage is suspended until the master grants new permission; under the Sahibayn, it is valid immediately. Note that if the second marriage is also fasid, all agree it is suspended on permission.
This shows how the difference affects real-world cases.

Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a slave's general permission to marry on subsequent marriages after a fasid (invalid) marriage. The matn states the ruling: if a slave marries a woman in a fasid marriage with permission and then has intercourse (which makes mahr wajib), he is sold to pay that mahr. If he later marries the same woman or another woman in a valid marriage, that valid marriage is suspended upon the master's permission (ijazah). The key issue is whether the initial general permission (given by the master for the slave to marry) is exhausted by the fasid marriage or not. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the fasid marriage, being a form of marriage, consumes the general permission, so the subsequent valid marriage requires a new permission from the master. According to Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the general permission does not cover a fasid marriage because the permission was given for a valid marriage. Therefore, the fasid marriage does not exhaust the permission, and the subsequent valid marriage is already covered by the original permission, making it valid without the need for new permission. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under Abu Hanifa's view, the second marriage is suspended until the master approves; under the Sahibayn's view, it is immediately valid. The text also notes that if the second marriage is also fasid, then all agree it is suspended on permission. The commentator adds an usuli principle: an absolute permission ends with the existence of one instance of its subject, analogous to divorce where an absolute pronouncement covers only one instance unless intent indicates multiplicity.

Key Terms
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Permission (from the master) for the slave's action; in this context, the master's approval for the marriage to take effect.
النكاح الفاسد (al-nikāh al-fāsid): A marriage contract that is defective in some condition (e.g., missing witnesses) but not fundamentally invalid (batil); it has some legal effects like mahr after intercourse.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilāf): The practical legal consequence resulting from a difference of opinion between jurists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), why is a slave's subsequent valid marriage after a fasid marriage not suspended on the master's permission?
A) Because the fasid marriage exhausted the permission.
B) Because the fasid marriage was not covered by the original permission, so the permission remains for a valid marriage.
C) Because the slave must ask for new permission for each marriage.
D) Because the master's permission is always revocable.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the effect of a fasid marriage on a slave's general permission to marry is disputed: Abu Hanifa holds it exhausts the permission, while the Sahibayn hold it does not.
