Marriage of an Indebted Slave and the Wife's Right to Mahr
Ruling and commentary on marriage of indebted slave and wife's creditor status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of marrying off an indebted slave and explain how the wife's mahr claim ranks among other creditors.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage is valid despite debts; the wife's mahr is an ordinary debt, equal to other creditors.

In Hanafi fiqh, the master's authority to marry off his slave remains intact even if the slave is indebted. However, when the slave's assets are liquidated, the wife's right to mahr does not take precedence over other creditors. This lesson examines the ruling and its implications for the distribution of the slave's price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master marrying off his male slave who is indebted and permitted to trade. The ruling in the Hanafi school is that such a marriage is valid (sahih).
The validity is based on the master's guardianship (wilāyah) over the slave, which stems from ownership of the slave's body (raqaba).
2
Analyze the basis for validity
The master's guardianship for marriage is not affected by the slave's debts because it arises from ownership, which remains intact even if the slave owes money. The commentary states: 'because the guardianship of marriage is based on ownership of the slave-body, and that remains after the debt, as it was before.'
This contrasts with financial transactions where the slave's indebtedness might restrict his capacity.
3
Discuss the wife's position among creditors
When the slave is sold to pay debts, the wife's claim for mahr is treated like any other debt. She ranks equally (tasāwat) with other creditors, but only up to the amount of mahr al-mithl (the appropriate dower). The sale proceeds are divided proportionally among all creditors.
The wife does not have priority over other creditors; the mahr is pari passu with other debts.
4
Differentiate between named mahr and mithl mahr
If the named mahr (al-musammā) is less than or equal to mithl mahr, the wife takes her full share of the proceeds covering the named amount. If the named mahr exceeds mithl mahr, she only takes a share up to mithl mahr from the sale; the excess she can only claim from the slave personally after the creditors are satisfied—either while he remains a slave or after he becomes free.
This protects creditors from excessive mahr claims that would unfairly reduce their recovery.
5
Note the extension to female slaves
The text begins to address the marriage of a female slave ('amatuhu'). It specifies that the ruling applies to his slave-girl, his umm walad, and his mudabbara, but excludes the mukataba because the master does not have the right to use her service.
For a mukataba, maintenance is obligatory without tabyī' (providing a dwelling).

Source Text

She takes a share of her mahr if the mahr is less than or equal to mithl mahr. But if it is more, she does not take a share of the excess; rather it is deferred until the creditors collect their debts. (And whoever marries off his slave-girl... [0] [1] His statement: 'slave...' means if he permitted his slave to trade, so he transacted buying and selling and became indebted, then his master marries him off, it is valid because the guardianship of marriage is based on ownership of the slave-body (raqaba), and that remains after the debt, as it was before. Thus in al-Bahr. [2] His statement: 'his creditors' (ghuramā'uhu) - plural of gharīm, i.e., the debt-holders. Summary: the mahr is like other debts, so the woman equals the creditors. If the price of the slave suffices for his debts, that is it; otherwise his price is divided among all creditors according to the ratio of their debts. The woman also shares with them in her mithl mahr. [3] His statement: 'a share of her mahr' i.e., a portion of the price that comes to her. [4] His statement: 'if it is...' Summary: she takes with the creditors the full share of the mahr debt if the named mahr equals mithl mahr or is less. If the named mahr exceeds mithl mahr, she shares with them in the amount of mithl mahr, and the excess she claims from the slave after the creditors are satisfied. She pursues him for it if he remains under his master's ownership or waits until he becomes free. [0] His statement: 'his slave-girl' (amatuhu) – likewise his umm walad (mother of his child) and similarly the mudabbara (one whose manumission is postponed after master's death). The mukataba (who has a contract of manumission) is not included, as indicated by his words 'she serves him' because the master may not use the mukataba for service. Thus maintenance is obligatory for her without tabyī' (providing a dwelling?). Thus in al-Bahr. (1) i.e., the debt-holders, and it states explicitly that the mahr is like other debts, so if the slave dies and has earnings, it is paid from them. See: Radd al-Muhtar 2:375. (2) al-Bahr al-Raiq 3:210.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the marriage of an indebted slave who has been permitted by his master to engage in trade. The matn states: if a master marries off such a slave, the marriage is valid. The wife's right to mahr (dower) ranks equally with other creditors when the slave's assets are liquidated. Specifically, if the slave is sold, the sale proceeds are distributed proportionally among the creditors and the wife up to the amount of mithl mahr (the appropriate mahr for a woman of her status). If the named mahr (al-musammā) is less than or equal to mithl mahr, she takes a share of the price covering the full named mahr. If the named mahr exceeds mithl mahr, she only takes a share up to mithl mahr, and the excess she can only claim from the slave personally after the creditors are satisfied—either while he is still a slave (by her pursuing him for work) or after he becomes free. The commentary further explains that the validity of the marriage stems from the master's guardianship of marriage, which is based on ownership of the slave (raqaba), and this ownership continues despite the slave's debts. Additionally, the passage begins to address the ruling for marrying off a slave-girl (amatuhu), specifying that this includes an umm walad and a mudabbara, but excludes a mukataba because the master does not have the right to use her service (the text mentions 'takhdimu' – she serves him). The ruling implies that for a mukataba, maintenance is obligatory without tabyī' (a likely reference to providing a dwelling or service). The text draws from al-Bahr al-Raiq and Radd al-Muhtar.

Key Terms
غریم (gharīm): A creditor or person to whom a debt is owed. Plural: ghuramā'.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The appropriate dower for a woman of similar status to the bride, used as a benchmark when the named mahr is excessive or in fasid marriages.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master marries off his indebted slave who is permitted to trade, and the named mahr (al-musammā) is greater than the mithl mahr, what happens to the excess?
A) The wife takes the full named mahr from the sale proceeds before other creditors.
B) The wife takes up to mithl mahr from the sale proceeds, and the excess is written off.
C) The wife takes up to mithl mahr from the sale proceeds, and she can claim the excess from the slave personally after creditors are satisfied.
D) The wife takes nothing until all creditors are paid in full.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of an indebted slave is valid, and the wife's mahr is an ordinary debt ranked equally with other creditors up to mithl mahr.

Validity of Marriage of an Indebted Slave
Ruling on validity of marriage of indebted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave's debt does not affect the validity of his marriage and state the basis for the master's guardian authority.

Core Idea
The master's authority to marry his slave is based on ownership, not solvency.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a male slave authorized for trade becomes indebted, and then his master marries him off. The passage confirms that the marriage is valid despite the debt. We will analyze the legal reasoning behind this ruling, focusing on the concept of wilayat al-nikah (guardianship for marriage) and its foundation in milk al-raqabah (ownership of the person of the slave).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a master marrying his indebted authorized slave. The ruling: the marriage is valid (sahih).
2
Locate the basis for validity
The passage states the basis: wilayat al-nikah is built upon milk al-raqabah, which remains after debt.
3
Contrast with potential objection
One might think debt invalidates the marriage because the slave's obligations affect his status; but ownership is unaffected.
4
Note the source
The passage cites Al-Bahr, a Hanafi reference, showing the authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو زوج عبداً مديوناً مأذوناً له صح ، وساوت غرماءه في مهر مثلها ) : أي ساوت المرأة غرماءه في مقدار مهر المثل : أي إن بيع العبد يقسم ثمنه بين المرأة والغرماء بالحصة، فتأخذ بحصة مهرها " إن كان المهر أقل من مهر المثل، أو مساوياً، أما إذا كان زائداً فلا تأخذ بحصة ما زاد بل يؤخر إلى استيفاء الغرماء ديونهم.

قوله : عبد ... الخ؛ يعني لو أذِنَ عبده للتجارة فتصرف بيعاً وشراءً وصارَ مديوناً ، فزوجه مولاه يصح؛ لأن ولاية النكاح تبتني على ملك الرقبة، وهو باق بعد الدين، كما كان قبله. كذا في «البحر».

قوله : غرماءه - بضم الغين وفتح الراء - جمع الغريم : أي أصحاب الديون، حاصله : إن المهر كسائر الديون، فتساوي المرأة الغرماء، فإن وَفَّى ثمن العبد دیونه، فذلك وإلا فيقسم ثمنه على جميع الغرماء على حسب نسبة ديونهم، أداء جميع.

قوله : بحصة مهرها؛ أي بحصة من الثمن وقعت لها.

قوله : إن كان ... الخ؛ حاصله : إنّها تأخذ مع الغرماء حصة دين المهر بكماله إن كان المسمّى مساوياً لمهر المثل ، أو أقل منه ، وإن كان المسمى أكثر من مهر المثل سادتهم في قدره والزائد عليه تطالب به العبد بعد استيفاء الغرماء، فيسعى لها به إن بقي في ملك مولاه أو تصبر إلى أن يعتق.
And if a master marries his slave who is indebted and authorized [for trade], the marriage is valid, and [the wife] equals his creditors in [the amount of] her mahr al-mithl. That is: the woman equals his creditors in the amount of mahr al-mithl. That is, if the slave is sold, his price is divided between the woman and the creditors proportionally; so she takes a share of her mahr if the [named] mahr is less than or equal to mahr al-mithl. But if it is more, she does not take a share of the excess; rather it is postponed until the creditors are paid their debts. 

His saying: 'slave' etc. means: if a master permits his slave for trade, and he transacts by selling and buying and becomes indebted, then his master marries him off, it is valid, because the guardianship for marriage is based on the ownership of the slave (raqabah), which remains after debt as it was before. Quoted from Al-Bahr.

His saying: 'creditors' - plural of gharim: the debt-holders. Summary: Mahr is like other debts; so the woman equals the creditors. If the price of the slave suffices for his debts, then so; otherwise his price is divided among all creditors according to the proportion of their debts, paying all.

His saying: 'a share of her mahr' i.e. a share from the price that falls to her.

His saying: 'If...' etc. Summary: She takes with the creditors the full share of the mahr debt if the named dower equals mahr al-mithl or is less. If the named dower is more than mahr al-mithl, then the creditors take precedence over her for the amount of mahr al-mithl; and the excess she pursues from the slave after the creditors are satisfied; he must earn it for her if he remains in his master's ownership, or she waits until he is freed.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the marriage of a male slave who is both indebted and authorized for trade. The passage type is a ruling statement with explanatory commentary. The author states that the marriage is valid despite the debt because the guardian authority (wilayat al-nikah) is based on ownership of the slave (raqabah), which remains intact when he is indebted. The key legal point is the status of the wife's mahr: she becomes a creditor like the others. If the slave is sold to pay debts, the wife shares proportionally in the proceeds up to the amount of mahr al-mithl. If the named dower (mahr musamma) is equal to or less than mahr al-mithl, she takes her full mahr share from the price. If it exceeds, she only takes the amount of mahr al-mithl from the price, and the excess becomes a personal debt of the slave, to be paid later if he earns or becomes free. The commentary emphasizes that mahr is treated like any other debt, and the wife does not enjoy priority over other creditors. This illustrates an important Hanafi principle: debts are pari passu, and the wife has no special security for an excessive dower beyond the fair mahr.

Key Terms
مهر مثلها (mahr al-mithl): The fair dower for a woman of her status, used as a benchmark for the wife's claim against the slave's price.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors; plural of gharim, referring to all debt-holders including the wife for her mahr.
ملك الرقبة (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the slave; the basis for the master's authority to contract marriage on behalf of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the marriage of an indebted authorized slave valid?
A) Because the slave consented.
B) Because the master's authority is based on ownership which remains despite debt.
C) Because the debt is forgiven.
D) Because the wife is not a creditor.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of an indebted authorized slave is valid because the master's guardianship is based on ownership of the slave, which persists after debt.

Wife's Mahr in the Sale of an Indebted Slave
Rule on wife's share in proceeds from sale of indebted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the wife's share in the proceeds of a sold indebted slave and distinguish between claims up to mahr al-mithl and claims exceeding it.

Core Idea
The wife's mahr is treated as a debt; she shares proportionally but only up to mahr al-mithl from the sale price.

This lesson addresses what happens when an indebted authorized slave is sold to satisfy debts. The wife, whose mahr is a debt, becomes one of the creditors. The passage specifies that if the named mahr does not exceed mahr al-mithl, she takes her full share from the price. If it exceeds, she only gets the amount of mahr al-mithl, and the excess becomes a personal claim on the slave. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that debts are pari passu and the wife has no special priority for an excessive dower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
An indebted authorized slave is sold; proceeds are to be divided among creditors.
2
Understand the wife's status
She is treated like any other creditor for the amount of mahr al-mithl.
Mahr al-mithl is the fair dower, determined by her status.
3
Determine the share
If named mahr ≤ mahr al-mithl, she takes full named mahr from proceeds. If named mahr > mahr al-mithl, she takes only mahr al-mithl share, and the excess is deferred.
The excess becomes a personal debt of the slave.
4
Examine the rationale
The fair dower is the benchmark; any excess is not secured by the slave's price.
This prevents the wife from being preferred over other creditors for an inflated dower.

Source Text

Marriage of a Female Slave: Tabwiah, Right of Intercourse, and Maintenance
Ruling on husband's rights and maintenance conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tabwiah for a married slave-wife, explain the husband's right to intercourse, and identify when maintenance becomes due.

Core Idea
Tabwiah is not required; husband's right is only to intercourse when free; maintenance due only with tabwiah.

This passage clarifies the legal position of a female slave who is married but continues to serve her master. The key question is whether the master must hand her over exclusively (tabwiah) and what rights the husband has. The Hanafi ruling distinguishes between the master's ownership (milk al-raqabah) and the husband's right to enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), leading to a nuanced set of obligations. Maintenance, in particular, is tied to the principle of ihtibas (confinement), which is absent without tabwiah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on tabwiah
Read the statement 'wa la tajib al-tabwiah' – tabwiah is not obligatory on the master. Understand that tabwiah means freeing the slave from service and handing her over to the husband for exclusive residence.
Even if the husband stipulates tabwiah in the contract, the condition is void but does not invalidate the marriage.
2
Understand the husband's right to intercourse
The husband may have intercourse 'in dhafara biha' – only when he finds her free from the master's service. He cannot prevent her from serving her master because his right is only milk al-mut'ah.
The husband's right is limited to lawful enjoyment, not control over her labor.
3
Recognize the condition for maintenance
The passage states 'la nafaqah wa la sukna illa biha' – no maintenance or housing is due from the husband except with tabwiah. Maintenance is compensation for ihtibas (confinement); without tabwiah, there is no confinement.
This parallels rulings for a nashizah (disobedient wife) – maintenance is linked to availability.
4
Analyze the nature of tabwiah
Tabwiah requires making the slave free from service and delivering her to the husband. Nighttime residence in the husband's house is key; daytime service does not invalidate tabwiah.
The commentary clarifies that tabwiah is not tied to the husband's house specifically; any arrangement that frees her from service suffices.
5
Connect to the principle of ihtibas
Maintenance is due as compensation for the wife's confinement (ihtibas) for the husband. Since the slave-wife is not confined (she serves her master), no maintenance is owed unless the master voluntarily hands her over exclusively.
This principle is consistent across Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زوج أمته تخدمه ، ويطؤها الزوج إن ظفر بها ، ولا تجب التّبوئة : وهي أن يُخلَّى بينها وبينَه في منزله، ولا يستخدمها ، لكن لا نفقة ولا سكنى إلا بها

تخدمه ، ويطؤها الزوج إن ظفر بها ، ولا تجب التبوئة : وهي أن يُخلى بينها وبينه ) : أي بين الأمة والزوج ، ( في منزله ، ولا يستخدمها ) : أي المولى، لكن لا نفقة ولا سكنى إلا بها ) : أي لا يجب على الزوج نفقتها أو سكناها إلا بالتبوئة

قوله : تخدمه؛ أي تخدم تلك الأمة مولاها، وليس للزوج منعها منها؛ لأن الزوج ليس له إلا ملك المتعة، فلا يبطل به حق المولى الثابت بملك الرقبة.

قوله : إن ظفر بها؛ أي إن وجدها في وقت خالية فارغة عن خدمة المولى، قال «النهر»: ويكفي في تسليمها قوله : متى ظفرت بها وطئتها.

قوله : ولا تجب؛ أي لا يجب على المولى أن يبوئها في منزل الزوج، وإن شرط الزوج ذلك؛ لأنه شرط باطل، فيبطل ولا يفسد النكاح؛ لأن النكاح لا يفسد بالشروط الفاسدة؛ وذلك لأن المستحق للزوج هو ملك الحلّ لا غير، نعم؛ ليس له أن يمنع الزوج مطلقاً، بل من وطء في وقت تصير فيه مشغولة بخدمة المولى.

قوله : في منزله؛ الضمير إلى الزوج، وهذا القيد ليس بداخل في حقيقة التبوثة، فإنّه لو خلى بينها وبينه في منزل نفسه، أو في منزل غيرهما كفى ذلك، والذي لا بُدَّ في وجودها هو أنه يجعلها فارغة عن خدمته ويسلّمها إليه، فلو كانت تذهب إلى الزوج وتجيء وتخدم مولاها لا تكون تبوثة. كذا في «البحر».

قوله : ولا يستخدمها؛ قال في «البحر»: «التحقيق أن العبرة لكونها في بيتِ الزوج ليلاً، ولا يضر الاستخدام نهاراً». انتهى.

قوله : لكن؛ أي لا تجب على الزوج نفقتها وسكناها؛ لأنها جزاء الاحتباس؛ وإذ ليس فليس؛ ولذا لا تجب نفقةُ الناشزة والمغصوبة والمحبوسة بدين عليها كما سيأتي تفصيله في موضعه إن شاء الله تعالى.
And whoever marries his female slave while she serves him, the husband may have intercourse with her if he finds her [free], and tabwiah is not required: which is to leave her alone with him in his house, and [the master] does not employ her [in service]. But there is no maintenance or housing [due from the husband] except with [tabwiah].

She serves him, and the husband may have intercourse with her if he finds her [free], and tabwiah is not required: which is to leave her alone with him: i.e., between the female slave and the husband, in his house, and [the master] does not employ her: i.e., the master. But there is no maintenance or housing except with [it]: i.e., maintenance or housing is not obligatory upon the husband except with tabwiah.

His saying: 'She serves him': i.e., that female slave serves her master, and the husband may not prevent her from that, because the husband only has the right to enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), so the master's right established by ownership of the slave (milk al-raqabah) is not invalidated.

His saying: 'If he finds her': i.e., if he finds her at a time free from the master's service. Al-Nahr said: 'It is sufficient for her delivery [to the husband] that he says: Whenever you find her, have intercourse with her.'

His saying: 'It is not required': i.e., the master is not required to hand her over in the husband's house, even if the husband stipulates that; because it is a void condition, so it is invalid but does not nullify the marriage, because the marriage is not nullified by invalid conditions. This is because what the husband is entitled to is only the right to lawful intercourse. However, he may not prevent the husband absolutely, but rather [only prevent] intercourse at a time when she is occupied with the master's service.

His saying: 'In his house': the pronoun refers to the husband, and this condition is not part of the reality of tabwiah, for if he leaves her alone with him in his own house, or in someone else's house, that suffices. What is necessary for its existence is that he makes her free from his service and delivers her to him. So if she goes to the husband and returns and serves her master, that is not tabwiah. Quoted from Al-Bahr.

His saying: 'He does not employ her': He said in Al-Bahr: 'The criterion is that she be in the husband's house at night; daytime employment does not harm.' End.

His saying: 'But': i.e., maintenance and housing are not obligatory upon the husband, because they are compensation for confinement (ihtibas), and since there is no [confinement], there is no [compensation]. Hence, maintenance is not due for a nashizah (disobedient wife), a [wife] seized [by force], or one imprisoned for debt, as will be detailed in its place, God willing.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage discusses the marriage of a female slave who continues to serve her master. The passage type is a ruling statement with explanatory commentary. The key ruling is that the master is not required to hand over the slave-wife for exclusive residence (tabwiah); rather, she continues to serve him. The husband may have intercourse with her only when she is free from service. Maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (sukna) are not due from the husband unless the master voluntarily provides tabwiah, i.e., frees her from service and delivers her to the husband. The commentary clarifies several points: the husband's right is only to lawful intercourse (milk al-mut'ah), not to control her labor; if the husband stipulates tabwiah, it is a void condition but does not invalidate the marriage; the essence of tabwiah is freeing her from service and delivering her, not necessarily in the husband's house; nighttime residence in the husband's house is the criterion, and daytime service does not negate tabwiah. The ruling is based on the principle that maintenance is compensation for the wife's confinement (ihtibas) for the husband; since the slave-wife is not confined (she is serving her master), no maintenance is owed.

Key Terms
تبوئة (tabwiah): The act of handing over the slave-wife for exclusive residence with the husband by freeing her from service.
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'ah): The husband's right to lawful sexual enjoyment of his wife, which does not extend to control over her labor.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance (food, clothing, housing) owed to a wife in exchange for her availability for marital relations.
احتباس (ihtibas): Confinement; the wife's being available exclusively for the husband, which is the basis for maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, under what condition does the husband have to provide maintenance for his slave-wife?
A) Always, as soon as the marriage contract is made.
B) Only if the master hands her over for exclusive residence (tabwiah).
C) Only if she becomes pregnant.
D) Maintenance is never due for a slave-wife.

Key Takeaway
Tabwiah is not obligatory; maintenance is due only when the master voluntarily hands over the slave-wife for exclusive residence, because maintenance compensates for her confinement.

Ruling on Service, Tabyiah, and Maintenance in Marriage of a Female Slave
Explains the legal relationship between master, husband, and married female slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's right to service, the husband's right to intercourse, and the conditions for the husband's obligation to provide maintenance for a married female slave.

Core Idea
In a slave marriage, the master retains ownership rights; the husband's maintenance depends on tabyiah (confinement).

This passage from 'Umad al-Ri'ayah expounds the delicate balance between a master's ownership of a female slave and her husband's marital rights. It clarifies that marriage does not nullify the master's right to her service, and the husband's duty to provide maintenance is contingent upon the master leaving her free for the husband – a concept called tabyiah. Understanding these rules is essential for grasping the Hanafi view of ihtibas as the foundation for nafaqah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rights
The master has milk al-raqabah (full ownership) entitling him to her service. The husband has milk al-mut'ah (right to sexual enjoyment). The slave stands between these two rights.
2
Understand the rule of service
The text states 'She serves him' – the slave serves her master even after marriage. The husband cannot prevent this because his right does not override the master's ownership.
The husband's right is limited to mut'ah, not full control.
3
Define tabyiah
Tabyiah is giving the slave a separate abode in the husband's house, free from the master's service. It is not obligatory on the master; even if the husband stipulates it, the condition is void.
The key is freeing her from service and delivering her to the husband; location can vary.
4
Link maintenance to tabyiah
Maintenance (nafaqah) and housing (sukna) are due only with tabyiah because maintenance is recompense for confinement (ihtibas). Without confinement, no maintenance.
This is the core principle: nafaqah is compensation for ihtibas.
5
Consider revocation and nuances
The master can revoke tabyiah, and maintenance drops. Daytime service does not invalidate tabyiah if the slave stays at the husband's house at night. Voluntary service by the slave does not drop maintenance.
The measure is nighttime confinement; daytime service does not affect.

قوله : ولا يستخدمها ؛ قال في «البحر»: «التحقيق أن العبرة لكونها في بيتِ الزوج ليلاً، ولا يضر الاستخدام نهاراً». انتهى

فإن بوأها ثم رجع صح : أي الرجوع ، وسقطت : أي النفقة عن الزوج برجوع المولى عن التبوئة.

She serves him, and the husband has intercourse with her if he finds her. And tabyiah is not obligatory: it is that she be left alone with him in his house, and he (the master) does not use her for service. But there is no maintenance or housing except with it. She serves him, and the husband has intercourse with her if he finds her, and tabyiah is not obligatory: it is that she be left alone with him, i.e., the slave woman and the husband, in his house, and he does not use her for service, i.e., the master. But there is no maintenance or housing except with it: i.e., maintenance or housing for her is not obligatory upon the husband except with tabyiah. His statement: 'She serves him'; i.e., that slave woman serves her master, and the husband does not have the right to prevent her from that, because the husband only has ownership of enjoyment, so it does not nullify the master's right established by ownership of the slave's person. His statement: 'If he finds her'; i.e., if he finds her at a time when she is free from the master's service. Al-Nahr said: 'It is sufficient in her delivery for him to say: Whenever I find her, I have intercourse with her.' His statement: 'It is not obligatory'; i.e., it is not obligatory upon the master to give her a separate abode in the husband's house, even if the husband stipulates that, because it is an invalid condition, so it becomes void and does not invalidate the marriage, because marriage is not invalidated by invalid conditions; and that is because what is entitled to the husband is only ownership of permissible sexual intercourse, nothing else. Yes, he does not have the absolute right to prevent the husband, but rather from intercourse at a time when she is occupied with the master's service. His statement: 'In his house'; the pronoun refers to the husband, and this restriction is not part of the reality of tabyiah, for if he leaves her alone with him in his own house, or in someone else's house, that is sufficient. What is indispensable in its existence is that he makes her free from his service and delivers her to him. If she would go to the husband and come back and serve her master, that would not be tabyiah. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. His statement: 'And he does not use her for service'; said in 'al-Bahr': 'The correct view is that the consideration is for her being in the husband's house at night, and daytime service does not harm.' End. His statement: 'But'; i.e., maintenance and housing are not obligatory upon the husband, because they are recompense for confinement; and if there is no confinement, there is no maintenance. Therefore, maintenance is not obligatory for a disobedient wife, a kidnapped woman, or a woman imprisoned for a debt, as will be detailed in its place, God willing. If he gives her a separate abode and then revokes it, it is valid and drops; and if she serves him without his using her, it does not drop. 'If he gives her a separate abode and then revokes it, it is valid'; i.e., the revocation, 'and it drops'; i.e., maintenance from the husband drops upon the master's revocation of tabyiah. 'And if she serves him without his using her, it does not drop'; i.e., if the slave woman serves her master without his using her while tabyiah exists, maintenance does not drop from the husband. Tabyiah is the masdar of 'bo'athu manzilan' and 'bo'athu lahu' meaning you prepare a house for him. His statement: 'If he gives her a separate abode'; i.e., if the husband performs tabyiah and leaves usage, then later decides to use her, the tabyiah becomes void and usage becomes valid, because the right remains due to the continuity of ownership, so it is not lost by tabyiah once, just as it is not lost by marriage. His statement: 'i.e., maintenance'; i.e., absolutely if he revokes tabyiah absolutely; but if the master uses her during the day and returns her to her husband's house at night, then daytime maintenance is upon the master and nighttime maintenance upon the husband. Thus in 'al-Qunyah'. His statement: 'And if'; i.e., if the slave woman serves her master after tabyiah with her husband's permission without the master using her, her maintenance does not drop from the husband, because confinement exists and there is no impediment, unless she leaves from her husband's house against his will, then she becomes disobedient and maintenance drops. His statement: 'I gave him a separate abode'; the tā' in 'bo'athu manzilan' and 'bo'athu lahu' is damma (in the first) and in 'haya'tu' it is fatha (in the second); in the addressing form you say: 'I gave him a separate abode' and 'I prepared a house for him' meaning you made the house ready for him.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal commentary on the rights and obligations in the marriage of a female slave. The main text states that the slave continues to serve her master; the husband may have intercourse when she is free; tabyiah (giving her a separate house) is not required of the master; and without tabyiah, the husband has no duty to provide maintenance or housing. The commentator (the author of 'Umad al-Ri'ayah) clarifies each phrase. He explains that the husband's right is only to enjoyment (milk al-mut'ah), which does not override the master's ownership of the slave's person (milk al-raqabah). Therefore, the husband cannot prevent the master from using the slave for service. The concept of tabyiah is then defined: it is not simply giving a house, but ensuring the slave is free from service and delivered to the husband. If the master allows the slave to go to the husband but she still comes back for service, that is not sufficient tabyiah. Importantly, tabyiah can be revoked by the master; if he revokes, maintenance drops. The commentator also discusses nuances: the measure of tabyiah is that the slave stays in the husband's house at night; daytime service does not harm. If the slave voluntarily serves the master without being asked, maintenance remains. Finally, the commentator clarifies the linguistic details of the word tabyiah. Throughout, the reasoning is based on Hanafi principles: maintenance is compensation for confinement (احتباس), so if confinement is lacking (e.g., due to revocation of tabyiah or disobedience), maintenance is not due.

Key Terms
تبوئة (tabyiah): Giving a married female slave a separate abode in the husband's house, free from the master's service, such that she is confined to the husband.
ملك المتعة (milk al-mut'ah): The husband's right to sexual enjoyment of his wife, which is limited and does not include ownership of her person.
ملك الرقبة (milk al-raqabah): Full ownership of a slave's person, entitling the master to her service and labor.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance (food, clothing, housing) owed by the husband to his wife.
احتباس (ihtibas): Confinement of the wife to the husband's home, making her available for his exclusive companionship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Maintenance for a slave wife is not automatic upon marriage; it depends on the master's tabyiah, which creates the confinement that maintenance compensates.

Ruling on Tabwi'a and Its Revocation
Ruling on master's revocation of dwelling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the revocation of tabwi'a and explain how it affects the husband's maintenance obligation, including the exception for voluntary service.

Core Idea
Revocation of the slave's dwelling by the master nullifies the husband's maintenance, unless the wife serves voluntarily.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a ruling from al-Wiqayah regarding the master's provision of a dwelling (tabwi'a) for his married slave woman and her husband. The key question: does the master's later revocation affect the husband's duty to pay maintenance? The ruling clarifies that revocation is valid and the maintenance drops, but with a nuance if the slave woman voluntarily serves the master. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the master's ownership rights and the marital obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The act is tabwi'a (master providing a dwelling). The ruling is about its revocation: the revocation is valid (sahih) and the maintenance (nafaqah) drops from the husband.
The text says 'فإن بوأها ثم رجع صح وسقطت'.
2
Understand the Basis for Maintenance
Maintenance is owed by the husband because the wife is confined to his house (ihtibas) and available for him. Tabwi'a removes the wife from the husband's house but she remains confined to the separate dwelling; when revoked, she returns, so maintenance obligation resumes? Actually, the ruling says it drops, so the revocation causes the loss of the condition for maintenance? Let's see the reasoning.
The commentary explains that the master's right over the slave persists, so he can revoke.
3
Examine the Exception for Voluntary Service
If the slave woman serves her master voluntarily (without his request) while the dwelling exists, the maintenance does not drop unless she leaves the husband's house against his will (nushuz).
The text: 'ولو خدمته بلا استخدامه لا' and explanation.
4
Distinguish Linguistic and Technical Meanings
Linguistically, tabwi'a means preparing a dwelling; technically in fiqh, it means the master vacating a space for the couple. This distinction clarifies why the act is attributed to the master (he enables the dwelling).
The passage discusses this at the end.

If he provides a dwelling for her (the slave woman) then revokes, it is valid and the maintenance drops. And if she serves him without his using her, it does not drop. (The meaning of the first ruling): i.e., the revocation is valid, and the maintenance drops from the husband due to the master's revocation of the dwelling. (The meaning of 'and if she serves him without his using her'): i.e., if the slave woman serves her master without his using her while the dwelling exists, the maintenance does not drop from the husband, because she is confined (to the husband's house) and there is no impediment, unless she leaves her husband's house despite his prohibition, then she becomes nashizah and maintenance drops. ... The linguistic meaning vs. technical meaning: the tabwi'a intended by jurists is that the master vacates the space for the husband, so it is truly attributed to the master because he is the one who vacates.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the ruling on tabwi'a (providing a dwelling) for a married slave woman. The key ruling: if the master provides a dwelling and then revokes it, the revocation is valid and the husband's obligation to pay maintenance (nafaqah) drops. This is because the master's right over his slave woman continues. The commentary also addresses a secondary scenario: if the slave woman voluntarily serves her master after the dwelling is provided, but without the master's request, the maintenance remains on the husband unless the wife leaves the house against the husband's will (nushuz). The text then clarifies the linguistic meaning of tabwi'a and its technical usage in fiqh: technically, it means the master vacating a place for the slave woman and her husband, and this act is truly performed by the master. The footnote includes a biographical note on Shihab al-Din al-Khafaji, a Hanafi scholar and commentator.

Key Terms
التبوئة (al-tabwi'a): The act of the master providing a separate dwelling for his married slave woman and her husband.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance obligation of the husband towards his wife, including food, clothing, and lodging.
الاحتباس (al-ihtibas): The wife being confined to the husband's house and available for him, which is the basis for maintenance.
النشوز (al-nushuz): The wife's rebellion against her husband, such as leaving the house without permission, which causes the loss of maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It drops because she is serving another.
B) It does not drop because she remains confined to the husband.
C) It drops only if the master requests the service.
D) It is split between master and husband.

Key Takeaway
The master's revocation of the dwelling is valid and ends the husband's maintenance, but voluntary service by the slave woman does not affect maintenance unless she becomes nashizah.

Master's Authority to Marry Slaves Without Consent
Ruling on master's right to marry slaves without consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master marrying his slaves without their consent and explain the meaning of 'mukrihan' in this context.

Core Idea
A master can marry his slave without consent; 'mukrihan' means without consent, not by force.

In this passage, the author states a clear fiqh ruling: the master has the authority to contract marriage on behalf of his unmarried slaves, male or female, without requiring their consent. The commentary clarifies that the term 'mukrihan' (forcibly) here means 'without consent' and does not imply physical coercion. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi position on slave marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What act is permitted for the master? Look at the phrase 'وله إنكاح عبده وأمته مكرهاً'.
2
Understand the term 'mukrihan'
The commentator explains two possible grammatical readings: active participle (master acting forcibly) or passive participle (slave being forced).
3
Clarify the intended meaning
The commentator explicitly states that ikrah here is not about physical compulsion but marrying without consent.
Key: 'ليس المراد بالإكراه إجبارها على الإيجاب والقبول بالضرب ونحوه'
4
Note the implication
Does the slave's consent affect the validity of the marriage? According to the Hanafi school, the marriage is sahih (valid) even without consent.
This is based on the master's ownership and authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله إنكاحُ عبده وأمته مكرهاً ) : أي يزوج كل واحدٍ بلا رضاه.
[۲] قوله : مكرها ؛ بصيغة اسم الفاعل : حال من فاعل الانكاح ، أو بصيغة اسم المفعول : حال من العبد ،والأمة وليس المراد بالإكراه إجبارها على الإيجاب والقبول بالضرب ونحوه، بل تزويجهما بلا رضاهما كما أشار إليه الشارح له.

[Commentary on 'mukrihan': It can be read as active participle referring to the subject of marriage, or passive participle referring to the slave. The intended meaning of ikrah here is not coercion by beating etc., but rather marrying them without their consent, as the commentator indicated.]

Commentary
This passage states a clear Hanafi ruling: the master has the authority to contract marriage on behalf of his unmarried slaves (both male and female) without their consent. The commentary clarifies that the term 'mukrihan' does not imply physical force or duress; rather, it simply means that the slave's consent is not a condition for the validity of the marriage. This is based on the master's ownership and authority over his slaves. The marriage contract is still valid and binding on the slave even if he or she objects.

Key Terms
مكرهاً (mukrihan): In this context, 'without the slave's consent', not necessarily with physical coercion.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Literally compulsion, but here it refers to acting without the other's consent, not physical duress.

Ruling on Mahr When Death Occurs Before Intercourse
Expansion of the ruling on mahr with conditions and 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr when death occurs before intercourse, identify the conditions for dropping the mahr, and explain the two-part effective cause ('illa).

Core Idea
The mahr is dropped only if the killer is the master and is legally responsible (mukallaf).

This passage expands the classical Hanafi ruling on mahr when death happens before intercourse. The main distinction is between a free woman who commits suicide (mahr still due) and a master who kills his slave woman (mahr dropped under specific conditions). The commentary provides the underlying rationale ('illa) that governs all scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Cases
The text opens with two contrasting rulings: free woman suicide vs. master killing slave woman. Note the outcome for each.
Free woman suicide = full mahr; master killing slave woman = no mahr for master.
2
Understand the 'Before Intercourse' Condition
The ruling is restricted to before intercourse. Why? Because after intercourse the mahr is fully confirmed regardless of death.
Intercourse (wat') confirms the entire mahr.
3
Recognize the Conditions for the Master
The master's act of killing drops the mahr only when he is legally responsible (mukallaf). A minor master does not cause the mahr to drop.
Non-mukallaf: minor or insane. Their acts have no worldly legal consequences.
4
Analyze the Two-Part 'Illa
The text gives the 'illa for dropping the mahr: (1) the act must be from the one who owns the mahr, (2) it must entail a worldly ruling. Both are missing in the free woman suicide case (she doesn't own the mahr) and in the slave woman suicide case (the master didn't act).
This 'illa explains why the master's killing drops the mahr but not a stranger's killing.
5
Apply to Other Scenarios
Using the 'illa, deduce the ruling for: (a) stranger kills slave woman, (b) slave woman kills herself, (c) master is a minor. Confirm with the text.
Stranger: mahr not dropped. Slave suicide: mahr not dropped. Minor master: mahr not dropped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولحرة قتلت نفسها قبل الوطء المهرُ كله ، لا لمولى أمة قتلها قبله) : أي قبل الوطء
[٣] قوله : قبل الوطء ؛ قيَّد به لأن الوطء يؤكِّدُ المهر كله ، فلا دخل هناك لموتها قتلاً كان أو حتفاً.
[٤] قوله : قتلها ؛ أي المولى فلو قتلها أجنبي لا يسقط مهرها، ولو قتلت نفسها فكذلك على الصحيح؛ لأنَّ المهر لمولاها ، ولم يوجد منه منع المبدل، ويشترط في القاتل المولى أن يكون مكلفاً، فلو صبيّاً لم يسقط ؛ وذلك لأنّ العلة في سقوط المهر أمران :
الأول : أن يكون صادراً ممن له المهر.
الثاني : أن يترتب عليه حكم دنيوي، ففي الأمة غير المأذونة وغير المكاتبة إذا قتلت نفسها فُقِدَ الأمران، وفي الحرَّةِ إذا قتلت نفسها، والمولى الغير المكلف إذا قتل أمته
And for a free woman who kills herself before intercourse, the entire mahr is (due). Not for the master of a slave woman whom he kills before intercourse. (i.e., before intercourse). [Commentary on 'before intercourse': He restricted it because intercourse confirms the full mahr, so her death (by murder or natural) has no effect on that. Commentary on 'he kills her': i.e., the master; so if a stranger kills her, her mahr does not drop; and if she kills herself, similarly according to the correct view, because the mahr belongs to her master, and he did not prevent the exchange. And it is required that the killer master be legally responsible (mukallaf), so if a minor, it does not drop. This is because the cause for dropping the mahr are two: first, that the act be from the one who owns the mahr; second, that it entail a worldly ruling. So in the case of an unauthorized or non-mukatab slave woman who kills herself, both conditions are missing. And in the case of a free woman who kills herself, and the non-mukallaf master who kills his slave woman...
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed ruling on the mahr (dower) when death occurs before sexual intercourse (al-wat'). The main ruling distinguishes two cases: (1) A free woman who commits suicide before intercourse: her full mahr is still due to her heirs, because suicide does not nullify the husband's obligation. (2) A master who kills his slave woman before intercourse: the mahr is dropped (i.e., the master does not receive it). The commentary clarifies that this ruling is limited to before intercourse because after intercourse, the mahr is fully confirmed regardless of subsequent death. The underlying rationale ('illa) for dropping the mahr when the master kills his slave woman is that the master is the one entitled to the mahr, and by killing her, he destroys the subject of the contract before delivery. However, this only applies if the master is legally responsible (mukallaf, i.e., adult and sane). If a minor kills the slave woman, the mahr is not dropped because the minor's actions do not carry worldly legal consequences. The text also notes that if a stranger kills the slave woman, the mahr is not dropped because the stranger does not own the mahr. Similarly, if the slave woman kills herself, the mahr is not dropped on the correct view, because the master (who is entitled to the mahr) did not commit the act.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower paid by the husband to the wife upon marriage or at consummation.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause for the legal ruling; here, the two-part 'illa for dropping the mahr: (1) the act must be from the one who owns the mahr, (2) it must have a worldly legal consequence.
المكلف (al-mukallaf): A legally responsible person who has reached puberty and is sane; a minor is not mukallaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the mahr drop for a slave woman according to this text?
A) When she is killed by a stranger.
B) When she kills herself.
C) When her master (who is mukallaf) kills her before intercourse.
D) When she dies naturally.

Key Takeaway
The mahr is dropped only when the killer is the master of the slave woman and is legally responsible; in all other scenarios (free woman suicide, stranger killing, slave suicide, minor master), the mahr remains due.

Master's Authority Over Slaves' Marriage and Mahr Rules on Pre-Consummation Death
Ruling with commentary on slave marriage and mahr forfeiture

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a master may marry his male and female slave without their consent, and explain the rules of mahr when death occurs before consummation, distinguishing between free and slave women and identifying the killer.

Core Idea
A master may marry his slaves without consent; the mahr is forfeited only if the master kills his own slave before consummation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Riayah addresses two distinct but related nikah rulings: the master's right to contract marriage for his slaves without their consent, and the effect of death before consummation on the mahr. The commentary resolves a linguistic objection about who prepares the home, clarifies that 'coercion' here means lack of consent, and applies the legal maxim 'whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it' to the case of a master who kills his slave woman to collect the mahr early.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal scope
The passage begins with 'wa lahu inkaahu 'abdihi wa amatihi mukrihan'. Determine what 'ikrah' means here (not physical compulsion but marrying without consent). Recognize that this is a general rule for the master's authority over his slaves' marriages.
The commentary explicitly states that 'ikrah' here is not forcing the contract but marrying without their consent.
2
Analyze the mahr ruling for the free woman
The matn says a free woman who kills herself before consummation receives full mahr. Identify the reason: the mahr is owned by her, and the two causes for forfeiture are absent (the killer is not the owner of the mahr, and there is no worldly legal consequence for suicide).
This is 'ala al-sahih' according to the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the mahr ruling for the slave woman killed by her master
The matn states that if the master kills his slave woman before consummation, the mahr is forfeited. Understand the principle: the master hastened to get the mahr, so he is deprived of it. The commentary specifies conditions: the master must be legally responsible (mukallaf).
This is a branch of the maxim: 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it.'
4
Understand the two causes for forfeiture of mahr
The commentary lists two conditions for forfeiture: (1) the killer must be the one who owns the mahr (the master, for a slave; the woman herself, for a free woman), and (2) the act must have a worldly legal consequence. Use this to distinguish the cases of a stranger, heir, or non-responsible master.
These two causes are essential to predict rulings in other scenarios.
5
Apply the rulings to new scenarios
Given the principles, determine what happens if a stranger kills the slave woman, or if the slave woman kills herself, or if a child master kills her. The commentary gives these explicitly.
Test your understanding with the practice tasks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله إنكاح عبده وأمته مكرهاً) : أي يزوج كل واحدٍ بلا رضاه.
( ولحرة قتلت نفسها قبل الوطء المهرُ كله ، لا لمولى أمة قتلها قبله) : أي قبل الوطء [١]قوله المولى ؛ دفع دخل مقدر ، تقرير الدخل : إن تهيئة المنزل لا يفعلها المولى، بل الزوج، فإنه الذي يهيئ البيت لزوجته ، فكيف أسندت التبوئة، وهي بمعناها إلى المولى، وتقرير الدفع : إن المولى يجعل الزوج قادراً على هذه التهيئة، فإنه لو منعه منه لم يفعل ذلك، فكان إسناد التبوئة إلى الزوج إسناد إلى السبب، وهذا كله باعتبار المعنى اللغوي. وأما المعنى الاصطلاحي: التبوئة الذي يريده الفقهاء من إطلاقها، وهو الذي ذكره المصنف له سابقاً بقوله : وهي أن يخلي .... الخ، فهو مسند إلى المولى حقيقة؛ لأنه فاعل التخلية المذكورة. [۲] قوله : مكرها ؛ بصيغة اسم الفاعل : حال من فاعل الانكاح ، أو بصيغة اسم المفعول : حال من العبد ،والأمة وليس المراد بالإكراه إجبارها على الإيجاب والقبول بالضرب ونحوه، بل تزويجهما بلا رضاهما كما أشار إليه الشارح له. [٣] قوله : قبل الوطء ؛ قيَّد به لأن الوطء يؤكِّدُ المهر كله ، فلا دخل هناك لموتها قتلاً كان أو حتفاً.
[٤] قوله : قتلها ؛ أي المولى فلو قتلها أجنبي لا يسقط مهرها، ولو قتلت نفسها فكذلك على الصحيح؛ لأنَّ المهر لمولاها ، ولم يوجد منه منع المبدل، ويشترط في القاتل المولى أن يكون مكلفاً، فلو صبيّاً لم يسقط ؛ وذلك لأنّ العلة في سقوط المهر أمران :
الأول : أن يكون صادراً ممن له المهر.
الثاني : أن يترتب عليه حكم دنيوي، ففي الأمة غير المأذونة وغير المكاتبة إذا قتلت نفسها فُقِدَ الأمران، وفي الحرَّةِ إذا قتلت نفسها، والمولى الغير المكلف إذا قتل أمته
فقد الثاني، وفي الأجنبي أو الوارث إذا قتل حرَّةً أو أمةً فُقد الأول. كذا في «البحر»،
ورد المحتار»(4). [١] قوله : لأنه؛ يعني أن المولى قصد بالقتل أخذ المهر كله قبل أوانه فجوزي بحرمانه، وهذا من فروع قاعدة : من عجل بالشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه ؛ ولهذا
يحرم الوارث القاتل من تركة المقتول وتفصيله في الأشباه والنظائر .
And for him to marry his male slave and his female slave forcefully: meaning he marries each one without their consent. And for a free woman who kills herself before intercourse, the entire mahr is due; not for a master of a slave woman he killed before it: meaning before intercourse. [1] The word 'al-mawla' repels a presumed objection. The objection: preparing the home is not done by the master, but by the husband, for he is the one who prepares the house for his wife, so how is 'tabwi' (preparation) attributed to the master? The repelling: the master makes the husband able to do this preparation, for if he prevented him he would not do it, so attributing 'tabwi to the husband is attributing to the cause, and all this is according to the linguistic meaning. As for the technical meaning: 'tabwi' which the jurists intend by its usage, and which the author mentioned previously saying: 'It is that he vacates... etc.', it is attributed to the master in reality, because he is the doer of the vacating mentioned. [2] The word 'mukrihan' (forcefully) as the active participle is a state of the subject of marriage, or as the passive participle is a state of the slave, and coercion here does not mean forcing her to offer and accept by beating etc., but rather marrying them without their consent as the commentator indicated. [3] The word 'before intercourse' is a restriction because intercourse confirms the entire mahr, so there is no effect there of her death, whether killed or natural. [4] The word 'killed her' means the master, for if a stranger killed her, her mahr is not forfeited, and if she killed herself, likewise according to the correct opinion, because the mahr is for her master, and there was no prevention of the compensation by him. It is required for the master who kills to be legally responsible, so if he is a child it is not forfeited. This is because the cause for the forfeiture of mahr are two: First: that it proceeds from the one who owns the mahr. Second: that a worldly legal consequence is attached to it. Thus, in the case of an unpermitted and non-mukatab slave woman who kills herself, both causes are absent. In the case of a free woman who kills herself, and the non-legally responsible master who kills his slave woman, the second is absent. In the case of a stranger or heir who kills a free woman or slave woman, the first is absent. This is as stated in 'al-Bahr' and 'Radd al-Muhtar'. [1] The word 'because' means that the master intended by the killing to take the entire mahr before its time, so he was penalized by deprivation. This is a branch of the principle: Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it. For this reason, the killer heir is deprived of the estate of the killed, and its detail is in 'al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir'.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with explanatory commentary from 'Umdat al-Riayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. It addresses two issues: the master's authority over his slaves' marriages, and the effect of death before consummation on the mahr. The matn states that the master (mawla) may marry his male and female slave without their consent ('mukrihan' meaning without seeking their permission, not by physical coercion). The commentary clarifies that 'ikrah' here is not compulsion at the contract but rather the lack of requirement of the slave's consent.

Then the matn turns to mahr: a free woman who kills herself before intercourse is entitled to the full mahr, but if the master kills his slave woman before intercourse, the mahr is not due to the master. The commentary explains each part. It first addresses a potential objection about the word 'al-mawla' in the context of providing a home (tabwi'a): the husband provides the home, so why attribute it to the master? The answer is that the master enables the husband by not preventing him, so he is the cause. Moreover, in legal terminology, 'tabwi'a' refers to the master vacating the dwelling for the couple, so he is the direct doer.

The term 'mukrihan' is parsed grammatically but the core meaning is that the master marries the slave without his/her consent, not by physical force.

Regarding the mahr, the permissibility of killing is not relevant; the ruling focuses on who kills. For the free woman, the mahr is due in full; for the master who kills his slave, the mahr is forfeited. The reason is the principle: 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it.' The master killed to get the mahr early, so he loses it. If the killer is a stranger, the mahr remains; if the slave kills herself, the correct opinion (sahih) is that the mahr is still due because the master did not prevent delivery. There are conditions: the master must be legally responsible (mukallaf), otherwise if a child kills, the mahr is not forfeited. The commentary details the two causes for forfeiture: the killer must be the one who owns the mahr (i.e., the master), and the act must have a worldly legal consequence (like deprivation). In cases where the killer is a stranger or heir, the first cause is absent; where the killer is a free woman (suicide) or non-responsible master, the second cause is absent. This is all consistent with the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التبوئة (al-tabwīʾa): The preparation of a home for the wife; in this text, linguistically attributed to the husband but technically to the master who enables it by vacating the dwelling.
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): In this context, marrying a slave without their consent, not physical compulsion in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master kills his slave woman before the marriage is consummated, what happens to the mahr?
A) The mahr is due in full to the master.
B) The mahr is forfeited because the master hastened to take it early.
C) The mahr is divided between the master and the slave's family.
D) The mahr is given to the slave's heirs.

Key Takeaway
A master may marry his slaves without consent; the mahr is forfeited if the master kills his own slave before consummation, applying the principle of deprivation for hastening.

'Azl: Permissibility and Conditions with Slave and Free Women
States the ruling on 'azl with a slave woman with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with a slave woman and a free woman, identify the condition of permission, and apply the hadith evidence to each scenario.

Core Idea
'Azl is mubah with your own slave woman, makruh with a free woman without her permission, and requires the master's permission with another's slave woman.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on 'azl (coitus interruptus) based on the hadith of Ibn 'Umar. The ruling is not absolute but depends on the partner's status: free woman, one's own slave woman, or another's slave woman. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the Shari'ah in intimate matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The act is 'azl. The text states it is mubah with one's own slave woman, makruh with a free woman without permission, and requires permission for another's slave woman.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Ibn 'Umar: 'The Prophet forbade 'azl with a free woman except with her permission.' Also the narration: 'Practice 'azl with the slave woman, and seek permission from the free woman.'
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (nahy) indicates karahah, not tahrim, because it is a zanni dalil. The exception (illa bi-idhniha) shows that permission changes the ruling to mubah. For the slave woman, the narration directly permits 'azl.
4
Apply to three scenarios
Own slave woman: mubah; free wife: makruh without permission, mubah with permission; another's slave woman: requires master's permission.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference between Hanafi and other schools that may hold 'azl as generally makruh or haram.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزوج الأمة يعزل) (۳) [٢] قوله : يعزل ; مضارع معروف من العزل، وهو الإنزال خارج الفرج، وهو مباح في أمة الواطئ مكروه في الحرّة إلا بإذنها، وفي أمة الغير إلا بإذن سيدها لحديث ابن عمر : نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يعزل عن الحرّة إلا بإذنها، أخرجه ابن ماجة والبيهقي. وأخرج البيهقي عن ابن عمر ، قال : «تعزل عن الأمة وتستأمر عن الحرّة). (۲) قيد بالأمة أي أمة الغير ؛ لأن العزل جائز عن أمة نفسه بغير إذنها، والإذن في العزل عن الحرّة لها ولا يباح بغيره لأنه حقها ينظر: ((البحر»(۳: ٢١٤). (۳) عزل عن المرأة : هو صرف مائه عنها في الوطء مخافة الولد بأن ينزع ويمنى خارج الفرج. (طلبة الطلبة» (ص٤٧) ، و(المصباح»(ص٤٠٨).
And the husband of a slave woman practices 'azl (coitus interruptus). (3) [2] The word 'ya'zil' (he practices 'azl) is a known verb from 'azl, which is ejaculation outside the vagina. It is permissible with the slave woman of the penetrator, disliked with a free woman except with her permission, and with another's slave woman except with the permission of her master, due to the hadith of Ibn 'Umar: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) forbade practicing 'azl with a free woman except with her permission – narrated by Ibn Majah and al-Bayhaqi. And al-Bayhaqi narrated from Ibn 'Umar that he said: 'Practice 'azl with the slave woman, and seek permission from the free woman.' (2) The restriction 'with the slave woman' means another's slave woman, because 'azl is permissible with one's own slave woman without her permission, and permission for 'azl with a free woman is for her and it is not permissible without it because it is her right. See: al-Bahr (3:214). (3) 'Azl from a woman is: diverting one's fluid from her during intercourse out of fear of offspring, by withdrawing and ejaculating outside the vagina. (Talibat al-Talaba, p.47; al-Misbah, p.408).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on the permissibility of 'azl (coitus interruptus). The matn states 'wa zawj al-amati ya'zil' – 'the husband of a slave woman practices 'azl'. The commentary then explains the word 'ya'zil' lexically and legally. 'Azl is defined as ejaculation outside the vagina. The ruling is detailed: 1) With one's own slave woman (amat al-wati'), 'azl is permissible (mubah) without her consent. 2) With a free woman, if she is his wife, 'azl is disliked (makruh) unless she gives permission; if she is another's slave woman, it requires the master's permission. The evidence is a hadith from Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet forbade 'azl with a free woman except with her permission, and a narration that 'azl is permitted with a slave woman but permission should be sought from a free woman. The commentary also clarifies that the restriction 'with the slave woman' in the matn refers to another's slave woman, because with one's own slave woman no permission is needed. The reason for seeking permission from a free woman is that she has a right to offspring. This is consistent with Hanafi fiqh as recorded in al-Bahr and other sources.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Ejaculation outside the vagina during intercourse to avoid pregnancy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible (mubah).
B) Disliked (makruh).
C) Forbidden (haram).
D) Recommended (mustahab).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on 'azl is partner-dependent: mubah with one's own slave woman, requires consent with free woman, and master's permission with another's slave woman.

Ruling on Coitus Interruptus ('Azl) with Slave and Free Women
Explaining permissibility and conditions of 'azl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with free women and slave women, identify the conditions of permissibility, and explain the proof from hadith.

Core Idea
The ruling on 'azl depends on the woman's status: permissible without permission for one's slave, requires permission for free wife and another's slave.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi position on coitus interruptus ('azl). The ruling is not uniform; it distinguishes between one's own slave woman, a free wife, and a slave woman belonging to another. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Umar that the Prophet forbade 'azl with a free woman except with her permission. Understanding this distinction helps clarify the role of ownership and consent in marital relations from a Hanafi perspective.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the act and its categories
Determine what 'azl is and the three categories of women it applies to: one's own slave woman, a free woman, and another's slave woman.
'Azl is ejaculation outside the vagina to avoid pregnancy.
2
2. State the ruling for each category
For one's own slave woman: mubah (permissible) without permission. For a free woman: makruh tanzihi (disliked) unless she permits. For another's slave woman: not allowed (haram?) unless her master permits.
The text says 'mubah' for one's slave, 'makruh' for free without permission, and 'except with permission of her master' for another's slave.
3
3. Examine the dalil (proof)
The hadith: 'The Messenger of Allah forbade practicing 'azl with a free woman except with her permission.' This is the primary proof for the ruling on free women.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so the ruling is makruh, not haram.
4
4. Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (nahy) indicates the act is disliked (makruh), and the exception (illa) shows that permission makes it permissible. For slave women, the absence of prohibition in other narrations indicates mubah.
Also, the actions of the Companions (Ibn Umar, Ibn Abbas) support this distinction.
5
5. Note the practical application
This ruling applies in marital relations and master-slave relations. It highlights the importance of consent and ownership rights.
The mufta bihi is as stated in Zahir al-Riwayah.

[٢] قوله : يعزل؛ مضارع معروف من العزل، وهو الإنزال خارج الفرج، وهو مباح في أمة الواطئ مكروه في الحرّة إلا بإذنها، وفي أمة الغير إلا بإذن سيدها لحديث ابن عمر : نهى رسول الله أن يعزل عن الحرّة إلا بإذنها، أخرجه ابن ماجة والبيهقي. وأخرج البيهقي عن ابن عمر، قال : «تعزل عن الأمة وتستأمر عن الحرّة). وعن ابن عباس : «تستأمر الحرّة في العزل ولا تستأمر الأمة».

With a slave woman belonging to someone else, it is only permissible with the permission of her owner. Khiyar al-'Itaq: Option of a Freed Slave Woman to Annul Her Marriage
Inter-madhhab dispute with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-'itaq and explain why the hadith of Barirah supports it against the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A freed slave woman has the option to annul her marriage regardless of whether her husband is free or a slave.

This lesson examines the option (khiyar) granted to a female slave upon emancipation to either continue or annul her marriage. The Hanafi school, based on the hadith of Barirah, holds that this option applies even if her husband is free — a position contested by Imam al-Shafi'i. We will unpack the proof and the dispute, clarifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn gives a freed slave woman (including mukatabah and mudabbarah) an option to choose. Note that the option applies whether the husband is free or a slave.
The text explicitly includes all female slaves, regardless of age or husband's status.
2
Locate the Dalil
The hadith of Barirah is cited. Write down the key phrase: 'قد عتق بضعك فاختاري' (Your private part has become free, so choose).
This hadith is in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Analyze the Wajh al-Dalalah
The imperative 'اختاري' gives her a choice. The Hanafi school interprets this as general, not restricted to the husband's status.
In Hanafi usul, al-amr does not always indicate wujub; here the context clearly indicates takhyir.
4
Understand the Ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i restricts the option to cases where the husband is a slave. What is his possible proof? (Not stated in the text, but likely based on a different interpretation of the hadith or an analogy restricting the option to cases of status disparity.)
The ikhtilaf is specifically about the case of a free husband.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) because the hadith is general and not restricted by any specific evidence.
The disharmony (khilaf) is legitimate; both schools have strong proofs, but the Hanafi view is preferred within our madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وخيرت أمة ومكاتبة عتقت تحت حر أو عبد بإذن سيدها). [٢] قوله : وخيرت؛ مجهول من التخيير، وهذا الخيار يسمى خيار العتق، وهو مختص بالأمة ولو مكاتبة أو مدبرة كبيرة كانت أو صغيرة سواء كان زوجها عند عتقها حراً أو عبداً، وفي الزوج الحرّ خلاف الشافعي. والأصل فيه حديث بريرة مولاة عائشة رضي الله عنهما، فإنه لَمَّا عتقتها عائشة، قال لها النبي : «قد عتق بضعك فاختاري»، وفي «الصحيحين» عن عائشة : «خيرها رسول الله من زوجها فاختارت نفسها).
(And a female slave and a mukatabah who is freed while married to a free man or a slave with the permission of her master are given the option). [2] His saying: 'are given the option'; it is passive from al-takhyir. This option is called khiyar al-'itaq (option of emancipation). It is specific to the female slave, even if she is a mukatabah or mudabbarah, whether adult or young, whether her husband at the time of her emancipation is free or a slave. Concerning a free husband, there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i. The basis for this is the hadith of Barirah, the maidservant of 'A'ishah (may Allah be pleased with them both), that when 'A'ishah freed her, the Prophet said to her: 'Your private part has become free, so choose.' And in the Two Sahihs from 'A'ishah: 'The Messenger of Allah gave her the option concerning her husband, so she chose herself.'
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that gives a female slave (including a mukatabah or mudabbarah) the option (khiyar) to annul or keep her marriage upon being freed. The commentator explains that this option, called khiyar al-'itaq, applies regardless of whether the husband is free or a slave. However, Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees in the case where the husband is free, maintaining that the option does not exist. The Hanafi proof is the well-known incident of Barirah, where the Prophet gave her the choice after she was freed, and her husband was a free man named Mughith. The Hanafi position is that the option is established by the hadith and analogy.

Key Terms
خيار العتق (khiyar al-'itaq): The option given to a female slave upon emancipation to annul or continue her marriage.
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A female slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) to earn her freedom.
مدبرة (mudabbarah): A female slave who is promised freedom upon her master's death (tadbir).

Ruling on Coitus Interruptus with a Slave Woman: Master's Permission Required
States the ruling that 'azl requires master's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'azl with a slave woman and explain why the master's permission is required.

Core Idea
The master's permission for 'azl is obligatory because potential offspring is his property.

This lesson examines a specific ruling within nikah: the permissibility of coitus interruptus ('azl) with a slave woman. While 'azl with a free wife requires her consent, the Hanafi school adds a unique condition when the woman is a slave: the master's permission. We explore the underlying rationale — the master's property right over any offspring — and distinguish this from the case of a free woman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the condition
What is the ruling? The phrase 'بإذن سيدها' establishes that 'azl is only valid with the master's permission. Identify the act ('azl) and the condition (master's permission).
2
Analyze the legal cause ('illa)
The text gives the reason: 'azl prevents offspring, and offspring is the property of the master. So the 'illa is the master's ownership of potential children. Why does that necessitate permission?
The master has a property right over the slave's children; preventing that right requires consent.
3
Contrast with a free wife
Consider the parallel case: 'azl with a free wife only needs her permission, not anyone else's. What differs? The free woman owns her own offspring (in terms of lineage and rights), so the decision is personal between spouses.
For a slave woman, the master is the owner of the child, so he must consent.
4
Note the cross-madhhab perspective
The pre-populated toolbox indicates that Malik and Shafi'i differ. What is their view? They may not require the master's permission under certain conditions, arguing that the right to offspring is not exclusive to the master in this context.
This is an ikhtilaf within the broader issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بإذن سيدها) ، فإنَّ العزل مانع عن حدوثِ الولد، وهو ملك مولاها.
With the permission of her master. For indeed 'azl prevents the occurrence of offspring, and that offspring is the property of her master.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling that coitus interruptus ('azl) with a slave woman (amah) requires the permission of her master. The reason given is that the benefit of any offspring belongs solely to the master, not to the woman herself. Therefore, the master's consent is necessary because he is the owner of the child that would result from conception. This contrasts with a free woman where the benefit of offspring is personal, so 'azl is permitted only with her consent, not needing anyone else's permission.

Key Terms
العزل (al-'azl): Coitus interruptus – withdrawal of the penis before ejaculation to prevent conception.
مولاها (mawlahā): The master of the slave woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the master must approve all marital acts of his slave.
D) Because 'azl is prohibited in general except with permission.

Key Takeaway
The master's permission for 'azl is obligatory because the potential offspring is his property.

Khiyar al-'Itq: The Option of Emancipation
Explains khiyar al-'itq with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on khiyar al-'itq, explain its basis in hadith, and contrast it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
A freed slave woman has the option to annul her marriage regardless of whether her husband is free or slave.

When a female slave is set free while married, a critical question arises: does she have the right to annul the marriage? The Hanafi school grants this option unconditionally, while the Shafi'i school restricts it. This lesson examines the evidence and reasoning behind the Hanafi position, centering on the famous hadith of Barirah and the underlying legal causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
Determine precisely what is being contested: whether a freed slave woman married to a free man has the option (khiyar) to annul the marriage.
The agreement when husband is a slave is not in dispute.
2
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
The Hanafi school holds that she has the option in all cases. The primary evidence is the hadith of Barirah, where the Prophet gave her a choice after emancipation.
Key phrase: 'Your pudendum has been freed, so choose.'
3
State the Shafi'i position and its evidence
Al-Shafi'i limits the option to cases where the husband is a slave, arguing that the cause ('illa) is the shame of a free woman being a bed for a slave.
Shame is absent when the husband is free.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
Hanafis argue that the cause is not limited to shame; the increase of ownership (milk al-bud') over her own person occurs with any emancipation, justifying the option. They also reconcile conflicting reports about Barirah's husband.
The principle 'divorce is attributed to women' (al-ṭalāq bi al-nisā') also supports the option.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, a Hanafi qadi will give the freed woman the choice to stay or leave, while a Shafi'i qadi will not allow annulment when the husband is free.
This difference affects real-life marital rights.

وهذا بناء على مسألة اعتبار الطلاق، فإنَّه عندنا بالنِّساء، فلها الخيار منعاً ؛ لزيادة الملك عليها، وعنده بالرِّجال فلم توجد علة الفسخ، وهو العار، أو زيادة الملك.

And a slave woman and a mukatabah who is set free while under a free man or a slave are given the option. [1] His statement: 'So it does not exist' because there is no shame in the case of a free husband, nor increased ownership; yes, shame exists when the husband is a slave.
