Commentary
This passage contains a detailed inter-madhhab dispute regarding the khiyar al-'itq (option of emancipation). When a female slave (including a mukatabah or mudabbirah) is freed while married, the Hanafi school grants her the option to remain in the marriage or annul it. This is agreed upon when the husband is a slave, to prevent the shame of a free woman being subservient to a slave husband. When the husband is free, Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, but the Hanafis maintain the option based on the hadith of Barirah, where the Prophet gave her the choice, and the reports indicate her husband was both free and slave in different narrations. The Hanafis reconcile this by considering the option applies regardless of the husband's status, because the increase of ownership (i.e., the former slave now owns her own pudendum) is the underlying cause. The school also distinguishes between 'azl with a free woman (requires her permission) and with a slave woman (requires master's permission), as seen in the supporting hadiths.

Key Terms
خيار العتق (khiyār al-‘itq): The option given to a female slave upon emancipation to annul her marriage if she wishes.
بضع (bud‘): Pudendum or sexual organ; used metaphorically to indicate the right over her own person and marriage.
عار (‘ār): Shame; a social dishonor of a free woman being in a subordinate position to a slave husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a female slave have the option (khiyar) after being freed while married?
A) Only if her husband was a slave at the time of her emancipation.
B) Only if her husband was free at the time of her emancipation.
C) In all cases, whether her husband is free or slave.
D) She does not have any option; the marriage continues automatically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants a freed slave woman the option to annul her marriage regardless of the husband's status, based on the hadith of Barirah and the principle of increased ownership.

Marriage of Slave without Permission: Suspended Contract and Emancipation
Ruling on slave marriage without permission that is consummated and then freed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a slave's marriage contracted without permission and apply the condition of emancipation to determine the marriage's validity and the absence of khiyar.

Core Idea
A slave's marriage without permission is suspended, not void; emancipation removes the impediment and it becomes effective.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi marriage law: when a slave marries without the master's permission. The ruling demonstrates the concept of a suspended contract (mawquf) and its activation upon removal of the obstacle. Understanding this principle is essential for distinguishing between void and suspended contracts in Hanafi fiqh, and it also engages with a famous historical incident, the case of Barirah, which involves conflicting reports about her husband's status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its initial legal status
The act is a marriage contract made by a slave without the master's permission. According to Hanafi fiqh, such a contract is initially suspended (mawquf), not void (batil). This means it is not immediately effective but can become valid when the impediment is removed.
Suspension here means the contract is not invalid; it awaits a condition to become fully effective.
2
Determine the condition for activation
The key condition that activates the marriage is the master's emancipation of the slave. When the master frees the slave, the impediment (the master's right) is lifted.
3
State the resulting hukm after emancipation
After emancipation, the marriage becomes effective (nafadha) and the freed woman is not given an option (khiyar) to annul the marriage. The marriage is considered valid and binding.
The text explicitly says 'نفذ ولم تخير' (it becomes effective and she is not given an option).
4
Analyze the reasoning behind the ruling
The reasoning is that the only impediment was the master's right. Once that right is removed by emancipation, the marriage becomes valid from the beginning (ab initio). The woman had already consented at the time of the contract, so there is no need for a new option.
The Hanafi principle is that a suspended contract can become effective retroactively.
5
Recognize the inter-madhhab difference with the Shafi'i school
The Shafi'i school holds that a slave's marriage without permission is void (batil) and cannot become valid even after emancipation, unless the master ratifies it. The Hanafi position is more flexible, treating it as suspended rather than void.
This difference arises from differing views on the nature of the master's consent and the contract's validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمة (٢) نُكِحَتْ بلا إذن فعتُقِتْ ،نفذ، ولم تخير) ؛ لأنها قد رضيت وكان عبداً بالاتفاق قبل ذلك ، هل كان عند عتقها حراً معتقاً، أو عبداً؟ فروى البخاري وأصحاب السنن عن إبراهيم عن الأسود عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : قصة بريرة رضي الله عنها ، وفي آخرها قال الأسود: «كان زوجها حراً» (١). وأخرجوا عن عكرمة عن ابن عباس : أن زوج بريرة رضي الله عنها كان عبداً أسود، يقال له : مغيث ). فاستندت الشافعية بأحاديث عبوديته، وظنُّوا أنه لا خيار لها إذا عتقت تحت حر، وأصحابنا رجّحوا روايات الحرّية ؛ لكون خبرها خبراً عن تحقق وعيان وخبر عبوديتها مبنياً على استصحاب الحال: أي إبقاء ما كان على ما كان.
ومنهم : من جمع بينها بأن الخبر عن العبودية خبر عن حاله السابق، وخبر حريته خبر عن حاله الطارئ ، فأثبتوا الخيار في الحالتين، وهو الأقوى نظراً ودليلاً، وأيدوه بحديث الدارقطني وابن سعد الدال على أن منشأ الاختيار هو ملك بضعها، وشيدوه بما تقرر عندهم من اعتبار عدد الطلاق بالنساء كما مر، وسيأتي.
فإن الأمة تبين بطلقتين سواء كان زوجها حراً أو عبداً، والحرّة تبين بثلاث تطليقات حرّاً كان زوجها أو عبداً، فبعدما عتقت تُخيّر في الصورتين حذراً عن ثبوت الملك الزائد عليها ، وزيادة التفصيل في هذا البحث في كتب الأصول.
[٢] قوله : أمة ؛ وكذا الحكم في العبد، والحاصل أن العبد أو الأمة إذا نكح بدون إذن المولى، وصار نكاحه موقوفاً لِمَا مرَّ أن نكاحهما بدون إذن مولاها غير نافذ، ثم أعتقه المولى نفذ نكاحهما السابق لزوال المانع ، فإن المانع كان تعلق حق المولى، وقد زال.
A slave woman who is married without permission and then is freed: the marriage is effective and she is not given an option; because she had consented and he was a slave by agreement before that. Was he at her emancipation free or a slave? Al-Bukhari and the authors of the Sunan narrated from Ibrahim from al-Aswad from 'A'ishah, may Allah be pleased with her: the story of Barirah, and at its end al-Aswad said: 'Her husband was free.' And they narrated from 'Ikrimah from Ibn 'Abbas that Barirah's husband, may Allah be pleased with her, was a black slave named Mughith. So the Shafi'is relied on the hadiths of his slavery and thought that she has no option if she was freed under a free man. But our companions (Hanafis) gave preponderance to the reports of freedom because that report is based on confirmed observation, while the report of slavery is based on istishab al-hal (continuity of the prior state). And some of them reconciled by saying that the report of slavery refers to his earlier state, and the report of freedom refers to his later state. They thus established the option in both cases, and this is stronger in consideration and evidence. They supported it with the hadith of al-Daraqutni and Ibn Sa'd indicating that the origin of choice is the ownership of her pudendum, and they strengthened it with what is established according to them regarding considering the count of divorce in relation to women, as has passed and will come. For the slave woman is divorced by two pronouncements whether her husband is free or slave, and the free woman is divorced by three pronouncements whether her husband is free or slave. Thus after she is freed, she is given the option in both situations to avoid the establishment of increased ownership over her. Further detail on this research is in books of usul. [2] His statement: 'A slave woman' and the same applies to a male slave. The conclusion is that when a male or female slave marries without the master's permission, the marriage becomes suspended as was previously mentioned that their marriage without the master's permission is not effective. Then when the master emancipates them, their previous marriage becomes effective because the impediment is removed; the impediment was the master's right, and it has ceased.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a slave woman (or man) who married without the permission of their master, and then later is emancipated by the master. The ruling is that the marriage becomes effective (nafadha) upon emancipation, and the freed woman does not have the option (khiyar) to annul the marriage, because she had already consented to the marriage when it was contracted. The reason the marriage was suspended was only due to the master's right, and once that right is lifted by emancipation, the marriage takes effect. The commentary then enters an inter-madhhab discussion about the case of Barirah: there are conflicting reports about whether her husband was free or slave at the time of her emancipation. The Hanafis give preponderance to the reports that he was free because they are based on direct observation, while the reports of slavery rely on istishab (presumption of continuity). Some scholars reconcile by saying he was a slave initially then freed, so both reports are true. The Hanafis maintain the option in both cases, based on the principle that the option arises from the freed woman's ownership of her own person. The passage also notes the rule that a slave woman is divorced by two divorces and a free woman by three, and after emancipation she gets the option to prevent increased ownership over her.

Key Terms
موقوف (mawqūf): Suspended; a contract that is not immediately effective due to a legal impediment but can become effective when the impediment is removed.
نفذ (nafadha): Effective, executed; here meaning the marriage becomes legally binding.
استصحاب الحال (istiṣḥāb al-ḥāl): Presumption of continuity; the principle that a prior state remains unless changed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The marriage is void and must be renewed.
B) The marriage becomes effective and she has no option.
C) The marriage becomes effective and she gets an option to stay or leave.
D) The marriage remains suspended until the master explicitly approves it.

Key Takeaway
A slave's marriage without permission is suspended until emancipation, then becomes fully effective without an option for the freed spouse.

Khiyar al-'Itq: The Option Upon Freedom for an Enslaved Woman
Explains ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on khiyar al-'itq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on khiyar al-'itq, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view, and explain the role of the hadith of Barirah.

Core Idea
An enslaved woman gains the option to annul her marriage upon freedom to prevent increase of ownership over her.

This lesson explores a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether an enslaved woman who marries without permission has the right to annul her marriage upon being freed. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the Hanafi ruling and its basis in the principle that divorce counts are attached to women. We will analyze the hadith of Barirah and how Hanafi scholars reconcile conflicting reports to derive the option in both cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on khiyar al-'itq
The Hanafi school holds that an enslaved woman who marries without permission has the option to annul upon freedom. This is because the count of divorce is attached to women, so she fears an increase of ownership (milk) over her.
The matn initially states she has no option, but the commentary clarifies the actual Hanafi view.
2
Identify the Shafi'i counter-position
The Shafi'i school argues that the count of divorce is attached to men, so no cause for annulment exists, especially if the husband is free.
Shafi'is rely on reports that Barirah's husband was a slave, implying option only in that case.
3
Evaluate the hadith evidence for both sides
Both schools cite the story of Barirah. Hanafis prefer reports that her husband was free, based on direct observation over istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity).
The reconciliation view affirms option in both cases, deemed strongest (al-aqwa).
4
Analyze the Hanafi reconciliation
Some Hanafi scholars reconcile conflicting reports by affirming the option in both cases (husband slave or free), supported by the hadith of al-Daraqutni linking the option to ownership of the sexual organ.
This is the preferred view: 'al-aqwa nazaran wa dalilan'.
5
Apply the ruling to extended cases
The commentary extends the same principle to male slaves, mudabbara, and umm walad, showing coherence of the Hanafi position.
Consummation and 'iddah affect the outcome when the marriage is ratified.

Source Text

وهذا بناء على مسألة اعتبار الطلاق، فإنَّه عندنا بالنِّساء، فلها الخيار منعاً لزيادة الملك عليها، وعنده بالرِّجال فلم توجد علة الفسخ، وهو العار، أو زيادة الملك.

Book of Marriage, Chapter on Marriage of Slaves and Disbelievers. An enslaved woman married without permission then freed, the marriage is valid, and she is not given the option. This is based on the issue of considering divorce: according to us it is attached to women, so she has the option to prevent increase of ownership over her; according to him it is attached to men, so the cause of annulment is not found, which is disgrace or increase of ownership. (An enslaved woman married without permission then freed: valid, and she is not given the option) because she has consented. And he was a slave by agreement before that; was he at the time of her freedom a free man who had been freed, or a slave? Bukhari and the authors of Sunan narrated from Ibrahim from al-Aswad from Aisha: the story of Barirah, and at the end al-Aswad said: 'Her husband was free.' And they narrated from Ikrimah from Ibn Abbas: that Barirah's husband was a black slave named Mughith. So the Shafi'is relied on hadiths of his slave status, thinking that she has no option if she is freed while under a free man. Our scholars preferred the reports of his freedom because that report is a report based on actual observation, while the report of his slave status is based on istishab al-hal: i.e., maintaining what was as it was. Some of them reconciled by saying the report of slavery is about his previous state, and the report of freedom is about his later state. So they affirmed the option in both cases, and this is the stronger view by reasoning and evidence. They supported it with the hadith of al-Daraqutni and Ibn Sa'd indicating that the source of the choice is ownership of her sexual organ. And they fortified it with what is established with them regarding the count of divorce being attached to women as has passed and will come. For the female slave is divorced by two pronouncements whether her husband is free or slave, and the free woman is divorced by three pronouncements whether her husband is free or slave. So after she is freed, she is given the option in both cases to avoid the increase of ownership over her. More detail on this research is in books of usul. [1] His saying: 'so it is not found' because there is no disgrace in the case of the husband being free, nor increase of ownership. Yes, disgrace exists when the husband is a slave. [2] His saying: 'An enslaved woman'; and similarly the ruling applies to a male slave. The conclusion is that if a male or female slave marries without the master's permission, and his marriage becomes suspended as it has passed that their marriage without the master's permission is not effective, then the master frees him, his previous marriage becomes effective because the impediment has been removed, for the impediment was the attachment of the master's right, and it has been removed. Similarly, the ruling applies when ownership is transferred from the first owner to a second by sale, gift, or inheritance, and the second ratifies the previous marriage, then it becomes effective. And it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr' and others that the same is the ruling for a mudabbara if she is freed by the death of the master, and similarly for an umm walad: if the master frees her or dies, the marriage becomes effective if the husband had consummated before the freedom according to the narration of Ibn Sami'ah from Muhammad because the 'iddah becomes obligatory from the husband, so it is not obligatory from the master. As for the apparent narration, the 'iddah is not obligatory from the husband, so the 'iddah is obligatory from the master, and its obligation before ratification necessitates the dissolution of the marriage because a woman in 'iddah is not permissible for another man during her 'iddah.

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the option (khiyar) of an enslaved woman who marries without her master's permission and is later freed. The matn states she has no option, but the commentary explains the Hanafi view that she does have option because the count of divorce is attributed to women (i.e., she is divorced by two pronouncements), so increase of ownership over her is prevented. The Shafi'i view is that the count is attributed to men, hence no cause for annulment. The hadith of Barirah is discussed: reports differ on whether her husband was free or slave; Hanafis prefer the report of freedom. The stronger view, according to some Hanafi scholars, is to give option in both cases, supported by hadith. The commentary extends the ruling to male slaves, transfer of ownership, mudabbara, and umm walad. The author follows a methodical presentation: base ruling, basis, evidence, dispute, reconciliation, and extensions.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The option given to a freed slave woman to annul her marriage upon freedom, to prevent increase of ownership over her.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity: maintaining the previous state of affairs until evidence shows change.

Mahr of an Enslaved Woman Married Without Permission
States the ruling on who receives the mahr when an enslaved woman is married without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on who receives the specified dower when an enslaved woman is married without her master's permission, and identify the condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
The dower belongs to the master if consummation occurs before freedom; to the wife if after.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the dower (mahr) of an enslaved woman who is married without her master's consent. The key factor is the timing of consummation relative to her emancipation. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that the specified dower goes to the master if consummation happens while she is still enslaved, but to the wife if consummation occurs after she is freed. We will also explore how the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) for slave women is determined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the act under discussion? Marriage of an enslaved woman without permission. The ruling: the specified dower belongs to the master if consummation occurs before freedom; if consummation occurs after freedom, it belongs to the wife.
The ruling is based on who owned the usufruct at the time of consummation.
2
Locate the key condition
The critical condition is the timing of consummation relative to the woman's freedom. If consummation precedes freedom, the master receives the dower; if freedom precedes consummation, the wife receives it.
The passage explicitly states: 'If consummated and then freed, it is for the master; if freed first, then for her.'
3
Understand the rationale
The compensation for the use of the woman's sexual organ (usufruct) goes to the owner of that usufruct at the time of enjoyment. While enslaved, the master owns the usufruct; after freedom, the woman owns it.
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding ownership of benefits.
4
Note the definition of mahr al-mithl
The commentary discusses two methods for determining the proper dower for slave women: a fractional method (one-tenth of value for a virgin, half that for a non-virgin) and a market comparison method. The preferred opinion (al-mukhtar) is to look at the dower of similar women.
The fractional method is mentioned but not adopted; the chosen view is market comparison.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, determine the owner of the dower based on the timing of consummation and freedom.
Remember: the specified dower is given in full; there is no reduction to mahr al-mithl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما سمي للسيد وإن زاد على مهر مثلها لو وطئت فعتقت، وإن عتقت أولاً فلها.
[١] قوله : وما سمّي ; حاصله : إن المهر المسمى يكون لمولاها سواء كان المسمى مساوياً لمهر مثلها أو أقل منه أو زائداً عليه إن وطئها الزوج قبل عتقها ؛ لأن الزوج استمتع بمنافع بضعها حال قيام ملك المولى، فيكون عوضه للمولى، وإن كان الوطء بعد العتق ، لا يكون المهر للمولى، بل للزوجة نفسها ؛ لأنه أخذ المنافع في حالِ زال ملك المولى عنها، واستقلت بنفسها، فكان العوض لها.
[٢]قوله : مهر مثلها ; ذكر بعضُهم أن مهر المثل في الإماء عشر قيمة البكر ، ونصف عشر قيمة الثيب ، وقيل : ينظر إلى مثل تلك الجارية جمالاً ومولى، بكـم تتزوج ، فيعتبر بذلك، وهو المختار . كذا في «الفيض» لابن الكركي.
And what is named (as dower) is for the master even if it exceeds the dower of her like if she is consummated and then freed, and if she is freed first then it is for her. [1] His saying: 'and what is named'; its summary: the specified dower belongs to her master whether it is equal to her proper dower or less or more if the husband consummated before her freedom, because the husband enjoyed the usufruct of her sexual organ while the master's ownership was in effect, so its compensation is for the master. If the consummation is after freedom, the dower is not for the master but for the wife herself, because he took the usufruct when the master's ownership had ceased and she was independent, so the compensation is for her. [2] His saying: 'dower of her like'; some mentioned that the proper dower for slave women is one-tenth of the value for a virgin and half of one-tenth for a non-virgin. And it is said: one looks at a similar woman in beauty and master, for how much she marries, and that is considered; that is the chosen opinion. So in al-Fayd by Ibn al-Kirki.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage provides a clear ruling on the distribution of mahr (dower) when an enslaved woman is married without her master's permission. The matn states that if the husband consummates the marriage before the woman is freed, the specified dower belongs to her master, regardless of whether it is more or less than her proper dower. If she is freed before consummation, the dower is hers. The commentary explains the rationale: the usufruct of her sexual organ belonged to the master at the time of consummation, so compensation goes to him. After freedom, she owns her own usufruct. Additionally, a note on determining the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) for slave women is given: some say it is a fraction of her value, but the preferred method is to consider the market rate for a similar woman.

Key Terms
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The proper dower determined by looking at the dower of women similar to the bride in status and beauty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an enslaved woman is married without her master's permission and the husband consummates after she is freed, to whom does the specified mahr belong?
A) The master
B) The wife
C) Both equally
D) The husband's family

Key Takeaway
The ownership of the dower in an unauthorized marriage of an enslaved woman depends solely on the timing of consummation relative to her freedom.

Intercourse with a Child's Slave-Girl: Ruling on Lineage and Hadd
Ruling statement on intercourse with a child’s slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on lineage and hadd when a man has intercourse with a slave-girl owned by his child, and distinguish it from cases involving other relatives' slave-girls.

Core Idea
Shubhah (doubt) averts hadd but lineage is not established when a man fathers a child with his relative's slave-girl.

This passage from a classical Hanafi text addresses a specific scenario: a man who has intercourse with a slave-girl owned by his son or daughter. The ruling is contrasted with cases involving slave-girls of his wife, father, or grandfather. In all these cases, if a child is born and he claims paternity, the lineage is not established, but the hadd punishment for fornication is averted due to the presence of shubhah (doubt). The text suggests that permission from the child-owner might affect the ruling, but the passage is incomplete. Let's explore the legal principles behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its General Ruling
The text begins 'Whoever has intercourse with the slave-girl of his son or his daughter.' This is the subject. The ruling that follows is implied: if a child is born and claimed, lineage is not established and hadd is averted due to shubhah. But the text immediately distinguishes this from other slave-girls.
The phrase 'احتراز عما لو وطئ أمةً غيره' signals a precautionary distinction.
2
Distinguish the Cases
The text contrasts the child's slave-girl with slave-girls of the wife, father, or grandfather. For those cases, it explicitly states: lineage is not established and hadd is averted. This implies that the same ruling applies to the child's slave-girl by analogy, but the incomplete text may have a different conclusion.
The key difference might be ownership: the child is under the father's authority, possibly creating a stronger claim.
3
Understand the Role of Shubhah
Shubhah (doubt) is the reason hadd is averted. The man may believe he has a right to the slave-girl, e.g., because his child's property is considered like his own in some contexts. This doubt prevents the application of the fixed punishment.
Hanafis consider shubhah a powerful factor in averting hadd, even if the act is sinful.
4
Analyze the Incomplete Phrase
The text cuts off at 'If he says: She made her lawful for me...'. This likely introduces a scenario where the child-owner grants permission. In that case, the intercourse may become permissible (halal) and the ruling would change significantly.
The full text is needed to complete the rule. This shows the importance of complete transmission.

This brief passage addresses the ruling for a man who has intercourse with a slave-girl owned by his son or daughter. Key Terms

Mahr of a Slave Woman: Timing of Intercourse Determines Entitlement
Ruling on entitlement to mahr for a slave woman freed after marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who receives the mahr when a slave woman is married and later freed, and distinguish the two scenarios based on the timing of intercourse relative to manumission.

Core Idea
The mahr belongs to the master if intercourse occurs before freedom; to the wife if after.

This passage addresses a classic Hanafi scenario: a slave woman is married with the master's permission, and she is later freed. The key question is who receives the dower (mahr). The ruling hinges on the timing of the husband's first sexual intercourse—before or after the slave's freedom. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of compensation for marital rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage describes two situations: (1) the husband has intercourse before the slave woman is freed, and (2) she is freed before intercourse. Note the order of events.
2
Determine the mahr entitlement for each scenario
For scenario 1 (intercourse before freedom), the named mahr belongs entirely to the master, even if it exceeds her proper dower. For scenario 2 (freedom before intercourse), the mahr belongs to the wife herself.
The reason: the master's ownership of her sexual benefits existed at the time of enjoyment, so compensation goes to him.
3
Understand the legal rationale
The commentary explains that the husband enjoyed the benefits of her sexual capacity while the master's ownership was in effect, so the compensation (mahr) is for the master. After freedom, she owns her own benefits, so the mahr is for her.
This is a straightforward application of the principle that compensation follows the ownership of the benefit.
4
Define mahr al-mithl for slave women
The passage defines the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) for slave women. One view: one-tenth of the value for a virgin, one-twentieth for a non-virgin. The preferred view: compare to similar women in beauty and status.
This definition matters because the named mahr can exceed mahr al-mithl; the master still gets the full named amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما سمي للسيد وإن زاد على مهر مثلها لو وطئت فعتقت، وإن عتقت أولاً فلها
قوله : وما سمي؛ حاصله : إن المهر المسمى يكون لمولاها سواء كان المسمى مساوياً لمهر مثلها أو أقل منه أو زائداً عليه إن وطئها الزوج قبل عتقها؛ لأن الزوج استمتع بمنافع بضعها حال قيام ملك المولى، فيكون عوضه للمولى، وإن كان الوطء بعد العتق، لا يكون المهر للمولى، بل للزوجة نفسها؛ لأنه أخذ المنافع في حال زال ملك المولى عنها، واستقلت بنفسها، فكان العوض لها.
قوله : مهر مثلها؛ ذكر بعضُهم أن مهر المثل في الإماء عشر قيمة البكر، ونصف عشر قيمة الثيب، وقيل : ينظر إلى مثل تلك الجارية جمالاً ومولى، بكم تتزوج، فيعتبر بذلك، وهو المختار. كذا في «الفيض» لابن الكركي.
And what is named (as dower) is for the master, even if it exceeds her proper dower (mahr al-mithl), if she was had intercourse with and then freed; but if she was freed first, then it is for her. His statement: 'What is named'; its gist: The named dower belongs to her master, whether the named amount equals her proper dower or is less or more, if the husband had intercourse with her before her freedom; because the husband enjoyed the benefits of her sexual capacity while the master's ownership was in effect, so its compensation is for the master. If the intercourse was after the freedom, the dower is not for the master, but rather for the wife herself; because he took the benefits when the master's ownership had ceased from her, and she was independent, so the compensation is for her. His statement: 'Her proper dower'; some mention that the proper dower for slave women is one-tenth of the value of a virgin, and one-twentieth of the value of a non-virgin. And it is said: one looks to a similar girl in beauty and status, and how much she marries for, and that is considered; that is the chosen view. Thus in 'al-Fayd' by Ibn al-Karakī.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the dower (mahr) of a slave woman who is married and then freed. The base text states two scenarios: if the husband had intercourse with her before her manumission, the entire named dower goes to her master, even if it exceeds her proper dower (mahr al-mithl). If she was freed before intercourse, then the dower belongs to her. The commentator explains that the reason is that the husband enjoyed her sexual capacity while the master's ownership was intact, so the compensation belongs to the master. After freedom, she becomes independent, so the dower is for her. A sub-point defines 'mahr al-mithl' for slave women: according to one view, it is one-tenth of the value of a virgin and one-twentieth of a non-virgin; the preferred view, from Ibn al-Karakī's al-Fayd, is to look at similar women in beauty and status.

Key Terms
مهر مثلها (mahr al-mithlihā): The proper dower for a woman of similar status; for slave women, defined as one-tenth of the value for a virgin and one-twentieth for a non-virgin, or by comparison to similar women.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the entitlement to a slave woman's mahr is determined by the timing of intercourse relative to her freedom: before freedom it goes to the master, after freedom to the wife.

Man Intercourses with His Son's Slave-Girl: Lineage and Ownership
Ruling on lineage and umm walad when a man has intercourse with his son's slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl establishes lineage and becomes her owner, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of manumission on an existing marriage.

Core Idea
A father who claims a child from his son's slave-girl becomes her owner, but only if he qualifies and the son confirms; the hadith 'Anta wa maluka li-abik' establishes the principle.

This passage addresses a sensitive scenario in Hanafi family law: a man who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The legal consequences hinge on whether a child results, whether the father claims the child, and whether the son confirms that he made the slave-girl lawful. The text also examines a dispute within the Hanafi school about the validity of the slave-girl's prior marriage if she is freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the overall hukm (sahih/fasid/batil)? The passage rules on a man who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The outcome depends on conditions.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith 'أنت ومالك لأبيك' (You and your wealth belong to your father) is the key evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the father's right to own the son's slave-girl? It establishes the father's general authority over the son's property, which includes ownership of the slave-girl when lineage is established.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
What is the intra-Hanafi dispute? The dispute is about the effect of freeing a married slave-girl (mudabbara/umm walad) before the master ratifies the marriage. Two transmissions from Imam Muhammad differ.
The two positions: Ibn Sama'a's narration says marriage continues; zahir al-riwaya says it annuls.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is the mufta bihi position. The marriage is annulled if the slave is freed before ratification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وطئ أمة ابنه أو بنته
زال، وكذا الحكم فيما إذا انتقل من المالك الأول إلى الثاني ببيع، أو هبة، أو إرث وأجاز الثاني النكاح السابق، فإنه ينفذ.
وذكر في «البحر»، وغيره : إنّه كذلك حكم المدبرة إذا عتقت بموت المولى، وكذا أمّ الولد فإنّه إذا أعتقها المولى، أو مات عنها نفذ النكاح إن دخل الزوج بها قبل العتق على رواية ابن سماعة عن محمد؛ لأنه وجبت العدةُ من الزوج، فلا تجب من المولى.
أما على ظاهر الرواية لا تجب العدة من الزوج، فوجبت العدة من المولى، ووجوبها منه قبل الإجازة يوجب إنفساخ النكاح؛ لعدم حلية المعتدة من رجل لغيره حال عدته.
قوله : أمة ابنه؛ احتراز عما لو وطئ أمةً غيره، فإنه لو وطئ أمة امرأته أو والده أو جده، فولدت وادّعاه لا يثبتُ النّسب ويدرأ عنه الحدّ للشبهة، فإن قال : أحلها لي، لا يثبت النسب إلا أن يصدقه المولى في الإحلال، وفي أن الولد منه، فإن صدقه في الأمرين جميعاً، ثبت النسب وإلا فلا. كذا في «البحر».
قوله : فولدت؛ فلو لم تلد لَزِمَ عليه عُقرها، وارتكب محرّماً، ولا يحد قاذفه. كذا في «النهر». والعقر : بالضم: هو مهر مثلها في الجمال، وأما ما قيل : ما يستأجر به مثلها للزنا لو جاز فليس هو معناه، بل العادة أن ما يعطى لذلك أقل ما يعطى مهراً؛ لأن الثاني للبقاء لا الأول. كذا في «الفتح».
قوله : فادّعاه؛ أي ادعى الأبُ الواطئ كونَ الولد منه عند قاض، فلو لم يدع لا يثبت النَّسب؛ لأن ثبوت نسب ولد الجارية منه موقوفٌ على الدعوة ابتداءً بخلاف المنكوحة.
قوله : وهي أم ولده؛ فإن أم الولد هي الأمة التي ولدت من المولى، وادعى نسبه، فلما ثبت نسبه من الواطئ ثبت تملكه جارية الابن ضرورة، فصارت أم ولد له، وهذا إذا كان الواطئ حراً مسلماً عاقلاً، فلو كان عبداً، أو مكاتباً، أو كافراً، أو مجنوناً لم تصح دعواه، ولا تصيرُ أمّ ولد له لعدم ولايته على مال الابن.
قوله : فإن قوله ... الخ؛ هذا الحديث أخرجه ابن ماجة عن جابر أن رجلاً قال: «يا رسول الله إن لي مالاً، وولداً، وإن أبي يريد أن يحتاج مالي، قال : أنت ومالك لأبيك»، ورجاله ثقات، قاله المنذري، وأخرجه البيهقي والطبراني في معجمه الصغير.
And whoever has intercourse with the slave-girl of his son or his daughter... It is void, and likewise the ruling when the ownership transfers from the first owner to the second by sale, gift, or inheritance, and the second ratifies the previous marriage, then it is executed. And it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr' and others: likewise is the ruling of a mudabbara if she is freed by the death of the master, and similarly an umm walad if the master frees her or dies, the marriage is executed if the husband had entered with her before the manumission according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad; because the waiting period is due from the husband, so it is not due from the master. As for the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), the waiting period is not due from the husband, so it is due from the master, and its obligation from him before ratification necessitates annulment of the marriage; due to the lack of lawfulness of a woman in waiting period from one man for another during her waiting period. His statement: 'Slave-girl of his son'; a precaution against if he had intercourse with another's slave-girl, for if he had intercourse with the slave-girl of his wife, father, or grandfather, and she gave birth and he claims it, the lineage is not established and the prescribed punishment is warded off due to the ambiguity; if he says: 'He made her lawful for me', the lineage is not established unless the master confirms him in the making lawful and that the child is from him; if he confirms him in both matters, the lineage is established; otherwise not. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. His statement: 'And she gave birth'; if she did not give birth, he is obliged to pay her 'uqr (compensation), and he commits a prohibited act, and the one who slanders him is not punished with the hadd. Thus in 'al-Nahr'. And 'uqr (with damm) is her proper dower in beauty; as for what is said: 'what she is hired for for fornication if it were permissible' is not its meaning; rather the custom is that what is given for that is less than what is given as dower; because the second is for permanence, not the first. Thus in 'al-Fath'. His statement: 'And he claimed it' i.e. the father who committed the intercourse claimed the child as his own before a judge; if he did not claim, the lineage is not established; because the establishment of lineage of the child of the slave-girl from him is dependent on the claim initially, contrary to the free married woman. His statement: 'And she is an umm walad for him'; for an umm walad is a slave-girl who has given birth from the master and he claimed his lineage; when the lineage is established from the one who committed intercourse, his ownership of the son's slave-girl is established necessarily, so she becomes an umm walad for him. This is if the one who committed intercourse is a free, Muslim, sane man; if he is a slave, or a mukatab, or a disbeliever, or insane, his claim is not valid, and she does not become an umm walad for him due to his lack of guardianship over the son's property. His statement: 'For his statement... etc.'; this hadith was narrated by Ibn Majah from Jabir that a man said: 'O Messenger of Allah, I have wealth and a son, and my father wants to take my wealth,' he said: 'You and your wealth belong to your father.' Its narrators are trustworthy, said al-Mundhiri. Also narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Tabrani in his small dictionary.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on a man who has intercourse with his son's or daughter's slave-girl. The base text states that the marriage (if any) is void, and then the commentator expands on several points. First, if the slave-girl was married before being purchased or inherited, and the new owner ratifies the marriage, it is valid. Second, a dispute within the Hanafi school: according to the narration of Ibn Sama'a from Muhammad, if a mudabbara or umm walad is freed after being married and entered, the marriage continues because the waiting period is from the husband; but according to zahir al-riwaya, the waiting period is from the master, which annuls the marriage. Third, the commentary specifies that the rule applies only to the son's slave-girl, not others (wife, father, grandfather). If the father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she bears a child, he must claim the child before a judge; if the son confirms that he made her lawful and that the child is from the father, then the lineage is established, and the father becomes the owner of the slave-girl, making her an umm walad for him. The conditions for the father are: he must be free, Muslim, and sane. If no birth occurs, he must pay 'uqr (compensation equal to her mahr al-mithl). The proof for the father's right over the son's property is the hadith 'Anta wa maluka li-abik' (You and your wealth belong to your father).

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who gives birth to a child from her master, becoming entitled to freedom upon his death, and likewise here from the father upon proof of lineage.
عقر ('uqr): Compensation for intercourse with a slave-girl, equal to her proper dower (mahr al-mithl) based on beauty and status.
مهر مثلها (mahr al-mithlihā): The dower of a woman of similar status; here specifically applied to slave-girls as a measure for 'uqr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school regarding the marriage of a slave-girl who is freed by her master before the master ratifies her existing marriage?
A) The marriage continues.
B) The marriage is annulled.
C) The marriage continues only if the husband entered before manumission.
D) The marriage is annulled only if the husband did not enter.

Key Takeaway
For a father to establish lineage from his son's slave-girl and become her owner, he must be free, Muslim, sane, claim the child before a judge, and the son must confirm that he made her lawful and that the child is from him.

intermediate

Father's Intercourse with Son's Slave-Girl: Ruling and Conditions
Explains ruling, conditions, and proof for paternity and umm walad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl establishes lineage and umm walad status, and identify when the father must pay value or 'uqr.

Core Idea
Father's claim to paternity requires freedom, Islam, sanity, and a judicial claim — lineage is not automatic.

This passage addresses a specific scenario: a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The legal outcome depends on several conditions. The Hanafi school holds that if the father claims paternity and meets certain criteria, the child's lineage is established, she becomes an umm walad, and he must compensate his son with her value. If no child is born, he pays 'uqr (fair dower) and commits a sin. The commentary unpacks each element, including the proof from the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and basic ruling
The passage concerns a father who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The basic ruling is that if conditions are met, the child's lineage is established, the slave-girl becomes umm walad, and the father must pay her value to his son.
This is a specific case of property rights between father and son.
2
List the conditions for a valid paternity claim
The father must be free, Muslim, and sane. Additionally, he must explicitly claim paternity before a judge. Without a claim, lineage is not established.
Unlike a free wife, paternity is not automatic for a slave-girl's child.
3
Determine the consequences when conditions are met and when not
If conditions are met: child's lineage established, she becomes umm walad, father pays value. If not: no lineage, no umm walad, father still pays 'uqr and commits a sin.
The sin is for illicit intercourse, but the financial obligations remain.
4
Understand the proof from the hadith
The hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' is the basis for the father's right to take the son's slave-girl in this necessity. The Hanafi school uses it to justify the transfer of ownership.
The hadith is a general principle applied here by qiyas.

كذا في «النهر» (٢).
والعقر : بالضم: هو مهر مثلها في الجمال، وأما ما قيل : ما يستأجر به مثلها للزنا لو جاز فليس هو معناه ، بل العادة أن ما يعطى لذلك أقل ما يعطى مهراً ; لأن الثاني للبقاء لا الأول. كذا في «الفتح». [۲] قوله : فادّعاه ; أي ادعى الأبُ الواطئ كونَ الولد منه عند قاض، فلو لم يدع لا يثبت النَّسب ; لأن ثبوت نسب ولد الجارية منه موقوفٌ على الدعوة ابتداءً بخلاف المنكوحة. (۳) قوله : وهي أم ولده ; فإن أم الولد هي الأمة التي ولدت من المولى، وادعى نسبه، فلما ثبت نسبه من الواطئ ثبت تملكه جارية الابن ضرورة، فصارت أم ولد له، وهذا إذا كان الواطئ حراً مسلماً عاقلاً ، فلو كان عبداً ، أو مكاتباً، أو كافراً، أو مجنوناً لم تصح دعواه، ولا تصيرُ أمّ ولد له لعدم ولايته على مال الابن.
The book of marriage, chapter on the marriage of slaves and disbelievers. She gave birth, then he claimed it and his lineage was established, and she is an umm walad, and the value of her is obligatory upon the father. She gave birth, he claimed it, 'his lineage is established, and she is an umm walad', and the value of her is obligatory upon the father. For his statement: 'You and your property belong to your father.' The master said to me: lineage is not established unless the master confirms both the setting free and that the child is from him. If he confirms both matters, lineage is established; otherwise not. Thus in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'She gave birth'; if she did not give birth, then he is obliged to pay her 'uqr, and he commits a prohibited act, and his slanderer is not flogged. Thus in al-Nahr. And al-'uqr (with damm) is her dower equivalent in beauty. As for what is said: it is what one would hire her like for fornication if that were permissible, that is not its meaning; rather, customarily what is given for that is less than the dower, because the latter is for permanence, not the former. Thus in al-Fath. [2] His statement: 'He claimed it': meaning the father who had intercourse claimed the child from him before a judge. If he does not claim, lineage is not established, because the establishment of lineage of the slave-girl's child from him is contingent on the claim initially, unlike the free married woman. (3) His statement: 'and she is an umm walad'; umm walad is the slave-girl who gave birth from the master, and he claimed his lineage. When his lineage is established from the one who had intercourse, his ownership of the son's slave-girl is necessarily established, so she becomes an umm walad for him. This is if the one who had intercourse is a free Muslim sane man. If he is a slave, or a mukatib, or a disbeliever, or insane, his claim is not valid, and she does not become an umm walad for him due to his lack of guardianship over the son's property.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This is a matn-and-sharh passage from a Hanafi fiqh work. The base text (matn) gives the ruling: when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, she gives birth, and the father claims the child, the child's lineage is established, she becomes an umm walad (mother of the child), and the father must pay her market value to his son. The commentary (sharh) clarifies details. First, the claim must be made before a judge; lineage is not automatic as with a free wife. If she does not give birth, the father must pay her 'uqr (equivalent dower) and commits a sin, but no one is liable for qadhf (slander) for accusing him. The 'uqr is defined as the fair dower of a woman of her beauty, not the hire for fornication. The father must be free, Muslim, and sane; otherwise the claim is invalid because he lacks legal guardianship (wilayah) over his son's property. The proof for the father's right is the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' which is discussed in the next section. This ruling is based on the Hanafi principle that a father has the right to take his son's property in case of necessity, and here he takes the slave-girl to avoid unlawful intercourse.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who gives birth to a child from her master, and the master claims paternity; she thereby becomes free upon the master's death.
عقر ('uqr): The fair dower (mahr al-mithl) of a slave-girl when a man has intercourse with her without a valid contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What condition is necessary for the father to claim paternity and have the slave-girl become umm walad?
A) The father must be a free Muslim and sane.
B) The father must be the sole owner of the slave-girl.
C) The slave-girl must consent.
D) The son must agree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school establishes paternity and umm walad status for a father's child from his son's slave-girl only if the father is free, Muslim, sane, and claims paternity; otherwise, he owes 'uqr and commits a sin.

The Father's Right to Property: Between Text and Restriction
Explains the hadith and its application, restricted to necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of combining proofs (talif al-adillah) to derive the ruling that the father's authority over his son's property is restricted to necessity.

Core Idea
The father's authority is limited to necessity, combining the hadith with general prohibitions on taking property.

This lesson examines a key hadith often cited for the father's extensive rights over his son's property. The Hanafi school insists that this hadith must be read alongside other texts that prohibit taking another's property without consent. The result is a nuanced ruling: the father may only take ownership when genuinely necessary, and he must compensate. Understanding this process of combining proofs is central to Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Authenticate the textual basis
Begin by verifying the hadith's chain and authority. The commentary cites multiple narrations and scholars to confirm it is sound (sahih) and thus a valid proof.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut (single-transmission), but accepted for legal rulings.
2
Extract the apparent meaning
The literal wording 'You and your property belong to your father' suggests a broad, unqualified right for the father to take ownership. Identify why a literal reading could be problematic if taken alone.
Literal interpretation conflicts with Quranic verses like 'Do not consume one another's wealth unjustly' (2:188).
3
Identify the conflicting proofs
Recognize that other Quranic and hadith texts prohibit taking a person's property without their permission, even if that person is one's son. This creates a contradiction with the apparent sense of the hadith.
The commentator references 'ayāt and ahādīth' that establish the general prohibition.
4
Apply talif al-adillah (combining proofs)
The Hanafi method resolves the conflict by restricting the hadith's absolute scope. The father's right applies only when there is necessity (darurah / hajah), thus honoring both sets of texts.
This is a classic case of 'al-jam‘ wa al-tawfiq' between seemingly contradictory evidences.
5
Derive the concrete ruling (far‘)
Apply the restricted ruling to the example: a father who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl must first acquire ownership of her (by paying her value) to avoid unlawful intercourse. The ruling directly follows from the necessity condition.
The father's action is permissible only as a means to avoid a greater prohibition (unlawful intercourse).

Then when he takes ownership, her value becomes obligatory upon him. [2] His statement: 'In case of need.' An objection is raised: The hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' is absolute, indicating the father's absolute authority to take ownership. So where is the condition of need introduced? The answer: It is known from other verses and hadiths that indicate the impermissibility of disposing of another's property without permission, even if he is his son, unless with the owner's permission. So when the proofs indicate the impermissibility of taking ownership of another's property without his permission, and this hadith indicates that the son and his property [belong to the father] in the absence of need, it is interpreted as ownership in case of need. [3] His statement: 'So that the intercourse not be unlawful'; this indicates that the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, when he brings them together by transferring her to his own ownership...
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father.' The commentator first authenticates the hadith, citing multiple narrations and verifying their chains. He then explains the legal implication: the hadith grants the father a limited right to take ownership of his son's property only when necessary (darurah). The commentator argues that the hadith cannot be taken absolutely because other Quranic and hadith texts prohibit taking another's property without consent. Thus, when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, he must first acquire ownership of her (e.g., by paying her value) to make the intercourse lawful; otherwise it would be forbidden. An objection is raised: the hadith appears absolute. The answer is that the absolute nature is restricted by other texts, so the father's right exists only at times of need. The passage concludes by noting that the father's transfer of ownership is to avoid unlawful intercourse, analogous to his right to take maintenance for himself. This is a classic case of combining proofs (talif al-adillah) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
ولاية التملك (wilayat al-tamalluk): The legal authority to take ownership of another's property, here specifically the father's authority over his son's property.
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that justifies an otherwise prohibited act; here the father's need to avoid unlawful intercourse or to preserve himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Necessity (al-hajah/al-darurah).
D) The slave-girl's consent.

Key Takeaway
The father's authority over his son's property is restricted to necessity and requires compensation, derived by combining the hadith with general property protections.

Father's Ownership of Son's Property: The Case of the Slave-Girl
Ruling with proof on father's ownership and compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father's obligation to pay the value of his son's slave-girl after intercourse that results in pregnancy, and explain the evidence from the hadith and the distinction between types of need.

Core Idea
A father may own his son's property in need, but must compensate when the need is for lineage preservation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a specific and subtle ruling: when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she becomes pregnant, what compensation is due? The Hanafi school, using the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' establishes that the father may take ownership in necessity. However, not all needs are equal—preserving one's own life is a stronger need than preserving one's lineage, leading to different obligations. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning, the proof from hadith, and the key disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, determine the specific act being ruled upon: a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she becomes pregnant. What is the legal consequence?
The act is not the intercourse itself (which is deemed lawful to avoid zinā) but the obligation that arises from it.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to Abu Hanifa and the relied-upon view, the father must pay the value (qimah) of the slave-girl, not the mahr (dower). Understand the distinction: qimah is the market value; mahr would be the compensation for unlawful intercourse.
The Hanafi position is that ownership is established just before intercourse to prevent zinā, so no mahr is due.
3
Trace the Dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'You and your property belong to your father' (narrated by Ibn Hibban, al-Bazzar, etc.). The Hanafi interpretation restricts this absolute statement to cases of need, reconciling it with other texts that prohibit taking another's property without permission.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its meaning is zanni (requires interpretation).
4
Understand the Restriction
The father's authority to own is not absolute; it is limited to necessity (hajah). But necessity is of two types: stronger (self-preservation, for which no compensation is required) and weaker (preservation of lineage, for which compensation is wajib). This explains why the father must pay the value.
The need to avoid zinā and establish paternity is a weaker necessity because it involves another's property, so compensation balances the rights.
5
Analyze the Dispute
Zufar (within the Hanafi school) and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must pay the 'uqr (mahr-like compensation) because ownership is established only before conception, not before intercourse. Understand the practical difference: if pregnancy occurs, Hanafi says qimah; Shafi'i says 'uqr. If no pregnancy, all agree 'uqr is due.
The dispute hinges on when ownership is deemed to have taken effect—before intercourse or before conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[١] قوله : فيجب قيمتها ؛ يَرِدُ عليه : أنه لما ثبتت للأب ولاية التملك عند الحاجة شرعاً، فلا معنى لإيجاب القيمة عليه ؛ ولذا يحل له عند الحاجة الطعام من مال ابنه بغير وجوب القيمة. وجوابه : إن للأب ولاية تملك مال ابنه للحاجة إلى إبقاء نفسه، وإلى صونِ نسله ؛ لأنه جزء منه، لكن الأولى أشدّ ، والثانية فيها قصور؛ فللحاجة جاز التملكِ وللقصور أو جبنا عليه القيمة مراعاة للحقين. كذا في «الفتح» (٢) قوله : لا مهرها ؛ فيه خلاف لزفر والشافعي له فإنهما قالا : يجب العُقرُ (r) لثبوت الملكِ فيها قبيل العلوق لضرورة صيانة الولد، فعند الوطء هي مملوكة الغير. ونحن نقول : يثبتُ الملك قبل الوطء، فإن الضرورة لا تندفع إلا بإثباته قبله ؛ لئلا يكون الوطء زناً، بخلاف ما لو لم تحبل حيث يجب العقر. كذا ذكره الزَّيْلَعِي وابنُ الهمام
He made obligatory the father's authority to take ownership of the son's property when there is need, so before intercourse it becomes his property so that the intercourse not be forbidden. And Ibn Hibban recorded in his Sahih from 'Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, that a man came to the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, disputing with his father concerning a debt owed to him, so he said: 'You and your property belong to your father.' Al-Bazzar and al-Tabarani also recorded it from Samurah, and also from 'Umar according to al-Bazzar, and Ibn Mas'ud according to al-Tabarani, and Ibn 'Umar according to Abu Ya'la al-Mawsili, and in some of its chains there is weakness that is not harmful, as detailed by 'Abdullah al-Zayla'i in his Takhrij Ahadith al-Hidayah called Nasb al-Rayah. [1] His saying: 'He made obligatory...' etc. The summary: This hadith indicates that the father has the authority to own the son's property in case of necessity, because it is clear that the intended meaning is not that the son and his property are owned by the father, and it is known that the speech is about exclusivity and ownership; this cannot be except by his having authority to own, not absolutely, but in necessity. So if he has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, he owns her just before intercourse, because it is not permissible to have intercourse with someone else's property. When he owns her, her value becomes obligatory upon him. [2] His saying: 'when there is need' is objected to: The hadith 'You and your property belong to your father' is absolute, indicating the father's authority to own absolutely, so where is the restriction of need added? The answer: It is known from other verses and hadiths that indicate the impermissibility of disposing of another's property, even if it is one's son, except with the owner's permission. So when proofs indicate the impermissibility of owning another's property without his permission, and this hadith indicates the son and his property when there is no need, it is interpreted as ownership in case of need. [3] His saying: 'so that the intercourse not be forbidden' indicates that the father's intercourse with his son's slave-girl, if she becomes pregnant, is lawful. This is stated explicitly in al-Bahr where he said: 'It is restricted to childbirth because if he had intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she does not become pregnant, then he commits a sin and does not own her, and he must pay her 'uqr. But if she becomes pregnant from him, then it becomes clear that the intercourse was lawful because his ownership preceded it, and the one who accuses him of zinā is not flogged in both cases. As for when she does not give birth, it is apparent because he committed forbidden intercourse while she was not his property. As for when she becomes pregnant, that is because the doubt of disagreement—whether ownership is established before penetration or after—negates his ihsan.' As in al-Fath and others. End. There is a discussion: That his ownership is established before intercourse in our view, and before conception according to al-Shafi'i, is only for the necessity of establishing lineage, as clarified in al-Fath. And it does not follow from that that initiating this intercourse is lawful, just as if one seizes something and destroys it, then pays its compensation to its owner, the permissibility of what he did does not go back to the time of seizure. Unless it is said: By their saying 'lawful' they mean that it is not zinā, because if it were zinā, the hadd would be obligatory and lineage would not be established. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. [1] His saying: 'her value is obligatory' is objected to: Since the father's authority to own in necessity is established legally, there is no meaning to imposing the value on him; likewise he may take food from his son's property in necessity without imposing value. The answer: The father has authority to own his son's property due to the need to preserve himself and to preserve his offspring, because it is a part of him. But the first need is stronger, and the second is deficient. So due to need, ownership is allowed, and due to deficiency, we impose the value on him out of regard for both rights. This is in al-Fath. (2) His saying: 'not her mahr' there is a disagreement with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, for they said: The 'uqr is obligatory because ownership is established before conception for the necessity of preserving the child, so at the time of intercourse she is owned by another. And we say: Ownership is established before intercourse, because the necessity is not removed except by establishing it before, so that the intercourse not be zinā. Unlike the case where she does not become pregnant, where the 'uqr is obligatory. This is mentioned by al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a father may take ownership of his son's property when necessary, and it applies the ruling to the specific case of the father having intercourse with his son's slave-girl. The author explains that the proof is the hadith 'You and your property belong to your father,' but clarifies that this authority is not absolute: it is restricted to cases of need (hajah) because other texts prohibit taking another's property without permission. When a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, ownership of the girl is transferred to him just before intercourse to make the act lawful and avoid zinā. However, the father must pay the value (qimah) of the slave-girl because his need to preserve his lineage is considered a deficiency compared to preserving his own life, so compensation is required to balance both the father's right and the son's right. There is a dispute: Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must pay the 'uqr (equivalent of mahr) instead of the value, arguing that ownership only takes effect before conception, not before intercourse, so at the time of intercourse the girl is still another's property. The Hanafi position is that ownership is established before intercourse to prevent zinā, so no mahr is due, but the value is wajib as compensation for taking the property. The practical difference is that if the slave-girl becomes pregnant, the father pays the value; if she does not, he must pay the 'uqr because ownership did not precede the act. The commentary also addresses an objection: if the father can take ownership in need, why must he pay? The answer distinguishes between preserving one's own life (stronger need, no payment) and preserving one's offspring (weaker need, payment required to respect the son's ownership).

Key Terms
العُقر (al-'uqr): The compensation or dower equivalent for a slave-girl when intercourse occurs without a valid marriage or ownership.
التملك (al-tamalluk): The act of taking ownership, especially by the father over his son's property in necessity.
الحاجة (al-hajah): Necessity that justifies the father's taking ownership of his son's property; it includes preserving one's own life and preserving one's lineage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must a father pay if he has intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she becomes pregnant?
A) The mahr (dower) of the slave-girl
B) The value (qimah) of the slave-girl
C) Nothing at all
D) A fine to the ruler

Key Takeaway
A father may own his son's property in necessity, but when the need is for preserving lineage (weaker need), he must compensate by paying the value; when the need is for self-preservation, no compensation is due.

intermediate

Father’s Intercourse with Son’s Slave: Value Obligatory, Not Mahr
Inter-madhhab dispute on father's payment for intercourse with son's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father's intercourse with his son's female slave and evaluate the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, including the underlying proofs and practical difference.

Core Idea
Necessity establishes ownership before intercourse, making value due, not mahr.

The passage addresses a nuanced case where a father has intercourse with his son's female slave. The legal question is: what compensation must the father pay to his son? The Hanafi school rules that the father must pay the slave's market value (qimah), not a dower (mahr) or compensation for unlawful intercourse ('uqr). This ruling hinges on a legal fiction: for the sake of establishing lineage, the father's ownership of the slave is deemed to have preceded the intercourse. However, Zufar (a Hanafi dissenter) and al-Shafi'i argue that ownership is established only at conception, making the father liable for 'uqr. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the Hanafi school uses necessity (ḍarūra) to solve conflicts between property rights and lineage preservation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the legal issue
The scenario: a father has intercourse with his son's female slave. If she conceives, what must the father pay the son? The text compares this to cases where she does not conceive.
The intercourse is assumed to have occurred; the key variable is conception.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The father must pay the value (qimah) of the slave, not mahr. The proof: ownership is deemed to have preceded intercourse to avoid zinā, making the father the owner at the time of intercourse.
This retrospective ownership is established by necessity (ḍarūra) to allow the child to be legitimate.
3
Recognize the opposing view and its proof
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must pay 'uqr (compensation for unlawful intercourse). Their proof: ownership is established only at conception, so at the time of intercourse the slave was still the son's property, making the act unlawful.
'Uqr is the compensation for unlawful intercourse, equal to the female's mahr.
4
Trace the Hanafi response and the role of necessity
The Hanafi response: if ownership were not established before intercourse, the act would be zinā, which contradicts the need to establish lineage. Therefore, necessity requires pre-intercourse ownership.
The Hanafi principle: al-ḍarūra tuqaddar bi-qadarihā (necessity is measured by its extent).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the slave conceives, the Hanafi school says the father pays qimah; the opposing school says he pays 'uqr. The difference is the amount: qimah is the market value of the slave, while 'uqr is typically a fixed amount (like her mahr).
In cases without conception, all agree 'uqr is due.

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Wiqaya, discussing the case where a father has intercourse with his son's female slave. The main text states: 'He must pay her value, not her mahr.' The reason given is that the intercourse becomes lawful if she conceives, because the father's ownership over her is deemed to have preceded the intercourse. The commentary elaborates that this ruling applies only when she gives birth; if she does not conceive, the intercourse is unlawful and the father must pay 'uqr (compensation). The passage then addresses an objection: since the father has the right to take from his son's property for his own need, why must he pay value? The answer distinguishes between two types of need: preserving oneself (stronger need) and preserving one's lineage (weaker need). For the stronger need, no compensation is required; for the weaker need, compensation is necessary to balance the rights. A major difference of opinion is then presented: Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that the father must pay 'uqr because ownership is established only at conception, so at the time of intercourse the slave was still owned by the son. The Hanafi response is that ownership must be established before intercourse to avoid the act being zina. This difference is practical: if she conceives, the Hanafi school says father pays value (qimah) to the son, while the opposing view says he pays 'uqr. The relied-upon position is the Hanafi one.

Key Terms
قيمة (qīma): Market value of the slave girl as compensation paid to the son.
مهر (mahr): Dower in marriage; here it is not obligatory because marriage did not occur.
عقر ('uqar): Compensation for unlawful intercourse with a female slave, equal to her mahr.
ضرورة (ḍarūra): Necessity to establish lineage, which justifies retrospective ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Value of the slave
C) Neither
D) Half the value

Key Takeaway
Due to necessity of establishing lineage, a father's intercourse with his son's slave is lawful if she conceives, but he must pay the slave's value to the son.

Child Born from Father's Intercourse with Son's Slave and Grandfather's Position
Expansion on rulings of child's freedom and grandfather's role

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a child born from the father's intercourse with his son's slave is free, and explain the condition under which the grandfather has the same authority as the father.

Core Idea
Child is free because born in father's ownership; grandfather steps in only after father's death.

This lesson expands on two related rulings from the Hanafi school: the freedom of a child born when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, and the limited authority of the paternal grandfather. You will learn the legal reasoning behind these rules and the conditions that apply when ownership is shared or when the father is unavailable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two core rulings
First, the child born from the father's intercourse with his son's slave is free and no compensation is due for the child. Second, the true grandfather (father's father) is treated like the father after the father's death for these rulings, but not before.
The same logic extends to cases where the father has died, become insane, apostate, or a slave.
2
Understand the reason for the child's freedom
The child is free because at the time of conception, the father had ownership over the slave-girl (due to retrospective establishment of ownership upon birth of the child). This ownership precedes the child's birth, making the child free.
This differs from the case of a deceived buyer (al-maghrur) where compensation is due.
3
Analyze the condition of shared ownership
If the slave-girl is not exclusively owned by the son (e.g., she is jointly owned with a stranger or with the father), additional payments for 'uqr (dower) and value may be required to the partner, because ownership is not fully established at the time of intercourse.
The ruling changes because full ownership must exist for the child to be free without compensation.
4
Recognize the grandfather's limited authority
The true grandfather takes the father's place only when the father is absent due to death, insanity, disbelief, or slavery. This is because the Lawgiver treats the grandfather as a father in many rulings, but only as a substitute when the original cannot act.
Other relatives like maternal grandfather or other mahram relatives do not have this authority.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage continues from the previous, now addressing two additional rulings. First, the child born from the father's intercourse with his son's slave is free, and no compensation (value) is owed for the child because he was born while owned by the father (due to retrospective ownership). The commentary clarifies that this is when the slave is not jointly owned; if she is shared, different rules apply for shared ownership and compensation. Second, the grandfather (true grandfather like father's father) holds the same position as the father after the father's death, meaning he can similarly have intercourse with his grandson's slave and the same rulings apply, but only after the father's death and when the father is absent (deceased, insane, etc.). Before the father's death, the grandfather does not have this authority. The commentary also specifies that other relatives (like maternal grandfather) are not included. The passage illustrates these rulings with examples from the Hanafi legal corpus (references to Fath al-Qadir and al-Muhit).

Key Terms
ولد (walad): The child born from the father's intercourse with the son's slave.
جد صحيح (jadd ṣaḥīḥ): True grandfather (paternal grandfather) who is treated like the father in these rulings.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who becomes free upon her master's death after bearing his child.

D) Only for maternal grandfather

Key Takeaway
The child is free because born in the father's ownership; the paternal grandfather steps into the father's role only after the father's death or when the father cannot act.

Nikah — Marriage

Father Marries Son's Slave: Validity, Mahr, and Child's Status
Ruling when father marries his son's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a father marries his son's slave girl and identify the obligations (mahr) and the effect on her status (no umm walad) and the child's freedom.

Core Idea
A valid marriage overrides ownership interests: the father owes mahr, not value, and the child is free through paternity.

In the previous lesson, we saw the case of a father having intercourse with his son's slave. That case involved compensation for the son's loss. Here, we examine a distinct scenario: a full marriage contract. The ruling changes significantly because marriage is a valid contract with its own obligations. This lesson will clarify the father's duty to pay mahr, the slave woman's status as a wife (not umm walad), and the child's freedom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The father marries his son's slave girl. The act is nikah, and the ruling is sahih (valid).
2
Determine the woman's status after childbirth
She does not become umm walad for the father because the child is born from marriage, not from ownership. She remains a slave of the son but is a wife of the father.
umm walad status requires birth from intercourse under ownership; marriage changes the basis.
3
Identify the father's obligation
He must pay the mahr (dower) agreed upon, not the slave's value (qimah). This is because marriage is valid and mahr is a right of the wife.
4
Determine the child's status
The child is free (hurr) due to the father's paternity (qarabah). The child is not owned by the son; the father's lineage is established.
The child's freedom is based on kinship, not on umm walad status.
5
Contrast with intercourse without marriage
In the case of mere intercourse, the father had to pay the slave's value and she became umm walad. Here, due to marriage, the obligations shift to mahr and no umm walad status.
This contrast highlights the critical role of marriage in determining legal obligations.

Source Text
